O, children, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the medical sciences, and through its completion, its end has been reached, and through its explanations and clarifications, it seems to me that I have completed it for you with this treatise, which is a part of the operative art with the hands, that is, surgery.
Because the operative art with the hands is almost entirely absent in our region and time, the science of it is greatly endangered, and its knowledge is obstructed; and there remain only some small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which have been misinterpreted and have fallen into error and hesitation, so that the understanding of it is clouded and its strength and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I will revitalize it [the operative art] through the organization of this treatise, in accordance with the method of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation; and so that it may come equipped with the forms of cautery instruments and other tools of the craft, including their explanation and preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled craftsman with his hands is not easily found in our time is because the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, which includes knowledge of the arrangement of the limbs, their form, the connections and separations of those parts,  the knowledge of the bones, nerves, and ligaments, their number, and their arrangement; and the knowledge of the pulsating veins and the stationary ones, and the locations of their origins.
And therefore Hippocrates says that with a name alone, the subject is little, but especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction of this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said about anatomy will inevitably fall into error, for which men will die, as I have seen many such, because some are trained in this science and yet operate without it [the scientific knowledge] and without experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician, having incised a scrofulous abscess on a woman's neck, and cut some of the arteries of the neck, so that much blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who claimed to have removed a stone from a man who had suffered long, the stone being large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, causing the man to die on the third day.
And I myself, having been called upon to remove that stone, saw that because of the size of the stone and the patient's condition, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician, who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and went to a black-skinned man who had a fractured thigh.  The physician, with his ignorance, treated the fracture, placing the injury on top of cushions and bandages with excessive tightness, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound him according to his desires, and then he bandaged him, and then he left him for a few days and commanded that we should loosen the bandage; and it remained like that until the thigh and foot became full of pus, and he perished.
And I was called to him, and I proceeded to loosen the bandage, and tranquillity was achieved, and he was freed from his pains.
However, corruption had already set in within the limb, so that I could not halt it, and the corruption continued to spread through the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who opened a cancerous abscess, and it became ulcerated over several days, until the severity of the condition was magnified with the patient's suffering.
Which cause is that because cancerous sores are pure of melancholic humor, therefore they should not be removed by complete cauterization, but rather the affected limb should be amputated.
And therefore, children, it is necessary for you that the surgical procedure be divided into two parts: one being the procedure associated with health, and the other the procedure that poses a risk in the majority of cases.
And I have already shown this throughout this book, where there is a procedure that is error-prone and fraught with fear; therefore, it is necessary for you to guard against this and abandon it, so that the mistakes do not find a way to speak and disgrace you.
Therefore, take your tools with diligence and God's protection, and your patients with kindness and firmness.
And use the best path, by God's grace, to achieve health and praiseworthy success, and abandon the terrible illnesses of difficult cure, and free your tools from that which makes you fear, so that your skill in your faith and in your name does not escape you: for more remains for your glory, and higher in the world and behind your holiness.
Galen already said in some of his teachings: Do not treat a severe illness, lest you be called bad doctors.
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter is ordered from head to feet, concerning the forms of instruments and the tools of cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of incisions and perforations, and of cupping, and of sores, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered by the forms of instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of twisting and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of instruments.
It is necessary for me to describe the benefits of this cauterization and its harmfulness, and in which constitution cauterization is prohibited.
Therefore, I say that the discussion of the benefits and harm of cauterization is long and lengthy, subtle in science, and secretly hidden; and already many wise men have spoken of it, and they have disagreed about it, and for me, a small part of their discussions suffices, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cauterization is beneficial to all constitutions universally, which are with matter or without matter, except for two constitutions, which are hot constitutions without matter.
In the hot and dry constitution with matter, there is disagreement; for some have said that cauterization is beneficial to it, and others have said the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable in illnesses that are due to qualities and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry illness be cured with something that is hot and dry.
And those who say the opposite argue that cauterization is beneficial in all hot and dry illnesses that affect people's bodies, because when you compare the body and its moisture to the constitution of fire, you find the body to be cold.
And I say, regarding this argument, that experience has shown me this to be true on several occasions.
However, this should not be accepted by someone who has not already been trained and experienced in the chapter on cauterization through prior practice, and who has not understood the variety of human constitutions, the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, their symptoms, and the timeframes associated with them.
But in other constitutions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, for all doctors have agreed upon these, and there is no disagreement on the benefit of cauterization in them.
And understand, children, that the secret of healing through cauterization with fire, and its effectiveness, is superior to cauterization using medicinal burning substances because fire is simple.  Its action only affects the member being cauterized and does not harm other connected members except through minor damage.
However, the action of medicinal burning substances spreads to those members distant from it, and easily causes severe illnesses in the affected member, making healing difficult and prolonging suffering.
But fire, because of its purity and goodness of substance, does not do this; rather, it simply flows over.  We already know this through experiment and a long period of study of the art, understanding the truth of its causes.  Because of this, I am excused from a long discourse.  And if it were not for the fact that my book in this place, dedicated to the secrets of fire, and its effect on the body and the expulsion of illnesses, is already written (the operation of which it describes); a philosophical discourse demonstrating it, suitable for your comprehension, would be unnecessary.
And understand, children, that they disagree on the time when cauterization is most effective, and they consider springtime the best time.
And I say that cauterization is appropriate at any time, because the harm caused by time is overcome by the benefit gained from the cauterization, and especially if the cauterization is for acute and sudden pains or necessary ailments, which cannot tolerate delay, because the risk of greater harm from worsening is greater than the minor harm from the passing of time.
And this is not considered by you, or, children, this is not understood by the ignorant crowd or foolish physicians; they believe that every illness treated by cauterization is not always cured, and they thus conclude this.
And the reason is not as they imagine, for cauterization is a form of medicine that alters the complexion and dries up the moisture that causes the pain.
But nevertheless, cauterization is superior to other medicine because of its power, the efficacy of its action, and the grandeur of its effects.
And it is possible that the illness may return at some time, depending on the patient's constitution, the location of the illness, their strength, and the buildup of excess fluids within them, and what they have consumed in food and similar things.  However, if the illness for which cauterization is administered is a localized ailment with few excess fluids and moisture, like toothache and similar cases, it is possible that the pain will not return, but this is in small instances.
And it is commonly said that cauterization is the last resort in medicine, and that is a true statement, not in the way they understand it, for they believe there is no cure that works with medicine or any other means after cauterization.
And the reason is the opposite of that; the idea that cauterization is the last resort in medicine is only true when we have tried other methods of treatment for certain illnesses, and those methods did not work, and then, at the end, one resorts to cauterization, which does help in that situation. Therefore, the statement that cauterization is the last resort in medicine is not true according to the understanding of the common people and many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they said this only because of the purity and superior nature of gold's substance.
And they said that the cauterized area does not heal, but this is not entirely true, for I have proven this and found that this only happens in certain bodies and not others. Cauterization with gold is good and better than with iron, as they said; however, when you cauterize with gold-heated fire, it is not clearly explained to you, because the heat is specific to the amount you want due to the gold's resilience.  And because it is prepared to accept cold, if you apply the heat, the gold melts due to the fire, and some filings fall into the fire, causing the artisan trouble because of that.
Therefore, cauterization with iron is done more easily and more effectively for the correct execution of the procedure.
And I have organized this chapter on cauterization according to topics, and I have arranged those from the head down to the feet, so that the seeker may find what they want in it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization is effective against dampness and coldness affecting the brain, the cause of which is often twofold: a chronic and profuse rheumatic flux, known as a cold, flowing from the head to the eyes and ears, and excessive sleep; it also causes toothaches and sore throats, and generally any ailment stemming from cold, such as paralysis, stroke, and similar conditions.
The method of this cauterization is as follows: first, order the patient to be purged with laxative medicine, clearing the head, for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and constitution.
And then order him to shave his head, and then place him on a padded bench with your hands on his feet.
And then place the blade of your palm near the root of his nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that spot, mark the area with a mark, and then apply the olive cautery, which is done like this:
And then place that [cautery] on the marked spot, in such a way that it descends, with your hand being gradually tightened, and you turn it.
And then gently lift your hand and observe the area.  If you see any significant amount of bone exposed, about the size of a grain of barley, then lift your hand.  If not, then return your hand with that iron, or another, if it has cooled, until the bone is visible as I have described to you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area, and leave it there for three days.
Then apply a cloth soaked in broth and leave it on until the scar from the fire is destroyed. And then treat the area with four-drug ointment until it is healed.
And if the wound remains open, the better it is to keep the arm elevated, because this is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized down to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some part of the bone's substance has adhered.
And then remove what has adhered to the bone, and then it should be treated.
And others say that with the cautery they should reach the back, until it firmly presses on the bone until the bone breaks, and this is the method of a small cauterization. And they believe that vapors from the head escape from that location.
And the wound should be kept open for a long time, and then treated until it is healed.
However, I have not seen these two types of cautery in all cases, but only in some individuals, according to a cautious approach.  And, in my view, it is better, when possible, to use a gentler treatment; for the head is weakened by a disruption of its continuity, just as we have observed in other parts of the body, especially when the patient's head is of a frail nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better in my opinion; and by using it and with that technique, it's done with care.
Second Chapter.
Of cautery of the head.
When a chronic and prolonged pain affects the entire head of a patient who has been treated with cupping, leeches, head purges, plasters, washes, poultices, fowl and similar remedies, and especially if the previous cauterization with the method we've described has been ineffective, then consider that if the patient's head is of a strong constitution and not frail, and is experiencing intense cold, then cauterize with another cautery just below the first one slightly. And then cauterize that area on the temple of their head with a cautery until the skin is broken. Cut the skin open, and expose the bone to the extent described earlier, and cauterize that area on the back of the head in the area known as the "vas" (and it is the area where a cupping glass is placed). And ease your hand in this, and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience a great pain, different from the pain of other head cauterizations.
And I would recommend this cautery in its place, for the cautery used for cauterizing the temples and the back of the head should be a more delicate cautery than the one used for cauterizing the middle of the head; and this is the method for it.
Chapter Three.
Of the cauterization of non-chronic migraines.
When a sudden or widespread pain occurs in the head, or extends to the eye, the head should be cleansed with medicines, and the other cure, which I have described in the treatments of diseases, should be administered.  If this does not work, then cauterization is performed in two ways: either with a sharp, burning medicine or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is done by taking a clove of garlic, peeling and cutting off the ends of both its heads. Then, using a sharp lancet, cut the painful area on the temple until you create a small, hidden cavity beneath the skin.
Then, apply a compress to the area to gently constrict it, and leave it for three hours.  Then remove it and discard the garlic, and allow the wound to heal for two or three days.  Finally, apply a cloth soaked in broth to the area until it heals properly.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines, which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron, according to this method:  (or,  using fire as a cauterizing agent, the procedure is as follows:)
and it is called a "nail-clavil," because the head of it is like a nail's shape, in which there is some curvature, and a small eminence in the middle of it.
And then place it on the painful area, and hold your hand—so that the flesh below isn't burned—and turn the small iron slowly and quickly.
And if the amount that is burned by the intensity of the cautery is as much as half of it, lift your hand so the underlying artery isn't burned, to prevent bleeding.
And then soak some cotton in salt water and place it on the area, and leave it for three days; and then apply cotton soaked in butter, and then treat with ointment until healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the blade's edge, following the cautery method, and be careful not to cut an artery in this, a properly executed, but not antiquated method.
Fourth Chapter.
Of cauterization of chronic migraines.
When you treat a migraine with what we've discussed in the previous treatment, and what we've discussed in the classifications of illnesses, and the treatment doesn't work, and sometimes because the illness prevents the use of what we've described in the initial cauterization with medicine or cauterization with fire, then you must heat the knife cautery until it is white-hot, then make a line of cauterization on the site of the pain, a length equal to half a finger or less; hold your hand still once and press it until you cut the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to be careful not to damage the continuity of the jaw, which is strained by chewing, and that the ligaments and nerves that control it, are not injured, and do not cause spasms.
And if it is about the protection and caution of the flow of blood from the severed artery, for in its severance there is danger, especially for one who doesn't understand what they're doing, is not skilled, and hasn't practiced; and leaves the procedure unfinished at the beginning.
And I will return with a reminder of the treatment of accidental blood flow from the artery, according to its method, in its place within this book.
And if you see that this treatment doesn't help with this illness, and you see the patient's condition is tolerable, cauterize in the middle of the head as we have described, and treat the wound until it heals.
And if you wish, administer the cautery that we have discussed in the chapter on artery constriction with a cautery having two blades; this is a better and more effective cauterization.
Chapter 5.
Of cauterization of earaches.
When in the ear there is pain due to cold and it is treated with laxative remedies and the other treatments mentioned in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and this is the one called "puncta," which has this form:
And then apply the heated point, moving it around the entire ear in a circular motion, or in a series of small circles, if the pain is localized in specific areas. Move the cautery from the base of the ear slowly, after you have marked the location with a probe.
And if the cautery touches several points in the ear, cauterize each one, and then treat the affected areas until they heal.
Chapter 6.
Of cauterization of tumours.
Tumours that are cured with cauterization are only of the types that arise from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type caused by dryness or spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of tumour with leeches, purges, and other remedies, and your treatment is unsuccessful, then you must cauterize the patient with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, a second a little below the temple, and a third at the juncture of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area, for softening occurs only on the apparently healthy side.
And the method of cauterization is to cauterize with the cautery on the right side of the ear's extremity, a little higher than the temple, and another in the temple, whose length is equal to the length of the index finger, and hold the cautery until the size of half of the affected area is burned.
And this is the method of cauterization, and it is a type of surgical procedure, the method of which is described beforehand. However, it is more subtle than what you have seen; and it is necessary to cut away some excess fat.
And then treat the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the cauterization of old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old and you have treated it with what we have said, and you do not see improvement in the patient and the illness does not have a fever, then cauterize the area with four cauteries: on all the temples of their head; and with a cautery on the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the throat. And the method of cauterization is done according to what has been previously stated.
And it has already been cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is further behind; and it should be further treated with whatever you proceed with.
Chapter 8.
Of cauterization of forgetfulness performed by flame.
Large jars and pills that cleanse the brain should be given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the patient's entire head and place a plaster of the described kind, found in the treatise on plasters, upon the patient's crown.
And apply it several times, for this is a method of cauterization. And do this according to the same order that I have already described.
And therefore, if this treatment is effective, then do so; and if not, then make three cauteries on the back part of his head, extending downwards from the highest part of the head down to the lower part of the neck. Place the width of a finger between each cautery, and then treat the cauterization with the previously described treatment.
But if you add more and the patient tolerates it, then make a cautery in the middle place; and if you want a greater application, then cauterize it over the two horns.
Then treat it until it is healed; and let the cautery be oiled according to the previously given form.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
You should proceed with the cleansing of the head with herbs and what we have said; then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with cautery in the middle of the head, and with cauteries on all the horns of the head, and with cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if, in the illness of softening of the body, the patient is weak and the illness is severe, then cauterize that with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and subsequently with a cautery until the body is relaxed, very loose, and then remove your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be olive oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
Of the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
Therefore, it is necessary to treat the brain of the patient at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment that we have described in the division when the illness is great and tolerates the exception of medicines.
And if it is a child who does not tolerate the medicines, then administer poultices and masticatories that treat the brain, beforehand for many days, with a good diet.
Then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery on the back of the head, and cauterize above each temple. And if the illness is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize it with the cauteries I have mentioned, having simultaneously paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
Let the cautery be olive-like, as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then use a delicate cautery in this manner.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize it with the cauteries that we have mentioned in the previous discussion of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess leaning towards melancholy, and the patient's body is humid, then give them to drink that which purifies their brain, according to what has been previously stated in the division.
And then shave the patient's head, and then apply a properly made cauterization to the scalp, placing it in the middle of the head.
And let the patient be seated firmly, and be held in place on all sides.
And then apply a liniment; and a sheep's wool poultice is more worthy. And warm that over a fire with a measured, moderate heat, which the said patient tolerates when applied. And then place that in the middle of the patient's head, on the scalp, and leave it until it has cooled down.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with the other treatment for him, until he is healed; or, if you wish, cauterize that area with small punctures, following the patient's tolerance, but keep your hand with a cautery instrument appropriately prepared: this is a type of cautery that moistens the brain evenly.
And then place that cloth soaked in wool or hen's dung.
Chapter 12.
Of the cauterization of descending water in the eye.
When you are informed of the beginning of water in the eye through the signs I have mentioned in the section, then begin and give the patient to drink what will modify the fluids in their head, and protect them from all moisture.
And make that person sweat in a bath for several days.
Then command the patient to shave their head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on both temples if it is the beginning of watery discharge in both eyes, or on one temple if the watery discharge is beginning in one eye.
Cut with a cautery all the veins and arteries that are below the forehead; and let the cauteries be at that length, in the width of the temples.
And be careful of blood flow when cauterizing; and if you see blood flowing from them, stop it with any medicine possible to you.
And I will come with the knowledge that is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the prevention of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck below two bones with two cauteries placed directly.

Of cauterization of old sores.
When sores on the eyes are old, persistent, and evident through the veins and arteries in the exposed area of the head, and it is confirmed that this is due to cold phlegmatic superfluities, then cauterize it with the cautery we described in the chapter on beginning with hot water on the sores, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries on the neck below the temples, and with two cauteries on the neck below the bones. And if more is needed, then cauterize that along the entire area of the eye socket over the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Of cauterization of nasal foul odor.
When you treat it with what we have described in the division and your treatment doesn't work, then you prepare and give the patient cocculus to drink for three nights; and afterward, shave the patient's head, and cauterize with a moderate cautery and an olive cautery. And then cauterize it with a clove cautery, using two cauteries just above the two eyebrows, slightly below the hair.
And be careful of the sparks, so you don't set it on fire.
And this is the form of the clove cautery.
Chapter 15.
Of cauteries for softening the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened by illness or moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery using this lunar cautery; and this is its method:
And if you, cauterize a small area above the two eyebrows with two cauteries, on all parts, and they should be positioned away from the temples. And the length of each cautery should be according to the amount of eyebrow.

Chapter 16.
Of cautery of the eyelid when the hairs are turned inwards and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cautery in such cases is done in two ways, either with cautery or with a burning medicine.
If with cautery using fire, command the patient beforehand to remove their eyelashes as one would clean them, until they are lengthened and thinned; and if they prick the eye near their origin, then you should bind the eyes with a bandage so that they are not injured until the hairs have grown back.
Then, once they have grown back and thinned, place the patient's head on your lap, and then gently press against the eyelid with a probe, with a marking shaped like a myrtle leaf, and begin the marking near the hairs.
And then place cotton soaked in egg white or in a ptilli mucilage on the eye.
And then apply the cautery, which has this shape.
And then cauterize over the shape you have marked, little by little, many times, until the surface of the skin, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burned.
A sign of success in your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs raised towards the puncture in the eye.
And then raise your hand and leave it for three days, and then place cotton with a border over it until the scab is removed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if, due to some cause, the hair begins to grow again and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are *under* the eyelid, then cauterize them until they are raised to their natural position and the wound is healed, so that the hairs don't irritate the eye.
However, a cautery with a burning medicine, which is what you command the patient to do, is to allow the hairs to grow out and heal, and then a leaf-shaped piece of cloth (like a myrtle leaf) is made.
And then take known herbs and living vinegar, or whatever that is, and mix those things well.  Do this quickly so it doesn't get cold; then spread this mixture onto the cloth shaped like a myrtle leaf. Place this on the affected eye(s). Put a cotton ball soaked in egg white under the eye. And the patient's head should be supported by you.
And place your fingertip on the medicine and press gently, but don't remove it until the patient feels the medicine's burning sensation, for it burns like fire.
And when it persists in causing a burning sensation, then remove the medicine and gently rub it off with your fingertip; when the burning stops, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; then check if the eyelid has been raised (like it has been released from the constriction by fire or incision). If so, it is well. If not, reapply the medicine to the place where it has not yet been applied and do not give up until your treatment is completed and the eye is healed.
And then apply a cotton ball with a bandage until the burn is healed, and then treat it with a definitive ointment or something else until it is healthy.
It is necessary for you, in the course of your treatment, to carefully ensure that no medicine gets into the eye.  If, after a few days, the eyelid becomes softened and some of the hairs get into the eye, then reapply your medicine to the softened area of the eyelid, just as you did at the beginning, and continue treating it until it is healed.
And this is the method of using myrtle leaf.
And understand that human eyes vary in size and thus your treatment is adjusted accordingly.
And the path to recovery for those studying this art should not be hidden.
Chapter 17.
Of cauterization of fistulas in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula using the methods described in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment doesn't work, then you should cauterize it as follows:  Instruct the patient to rest their head on your lap, and have an assistant hold the patient's head between your hands in such a way that it is neither moved nor shaken by their head's movements.
And then place a piece of cotton soaked in egg white or plant mucilage over the patient's eye.
And then heat the cautery, which is shaped as follows:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's wing, at the end where the cautery is made.
And if you wish, it should be pointed towards the other end. And if you want, it should be shaped like a probe.
However, the one that is concave is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and drain and dry that area. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus from it.
And then place the cautery, which is well-heated, over it, and hold it with your hand a little distance from the cautery's edge, near the nose, so that your hand does not stray or the patient is not disturbed. Then place the cautery on the sore area and cauterize it.
And so, if at the beginning your cautery penetrates the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place a poultice with lint over it and surround it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if more than ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply an aggressive corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula, which some earlier physicians said:  Open the area of the fistula and make it open, then place in that fissure a thin lead rod, which is its shape: and put in that quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold it with your hand firmly, so that the patient is not moved, lest the molten lead reach the eye; and it is necessary that you place on the patient's eye a poultice soaked in egg white or water, because lead adheres to the fistula area and heals it with remarkable healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about cauterization and treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a trepanation of the nose and to return the fistula to the nasal cavity to its proper place.

Many times fissures occur in the lips, and more often in the lips of children.

Chapter 19.
On the cauterization of fistulas in the mouth.
When an abscess forms at the base of the uvula, or on the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, and it bursts, and is old, and pus drains and a fistula is formed, and you treat it but it does not respond to treatment, then you should apply a heated cautery according to the size of the opening in the fistula, and then insert the heated cautery into the fistula opening, and hold your hand there until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it, and this is done once or twice; and then treat it with the cure we previously mentioned until it is healed.
And therefore, if the discharge is cut off and healed, that is good; but if not, then it is necessary to uncover the area and remove the corrupted bone according to what is described in its relevant chapter.
Chapter 20.
Of the cauterization of teeth and the softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by moisture and the teeth are decayed, and you treat them with remedies but it does not improve, then place the patient's head on your lap, and now apply a cautery tool whose shape is appropriate here, in such a way that you place the cautery instrument on the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold your hand there a little until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as you wish.
And then the patient should fill their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the decayed tooth is fixed, the softened gums are tightened, and the corrupt moisture is dried up.
Chapter 21.
On cauterization for tooth pain.
When tooth pain is caused by coldness, or there is a cold or worm in it, or you do not find a cure with medicine, then cauterize it in two ways: either with cow dung, or with fire.
Cauterization with cow dung involves taking cow dung, boiling it in an iron pot, then taking cotton and wrapping it around the end of a probe.  Then, dip the cotton into the boiled dung and quickly place it on the painful tooth until it cools down.
And repeat this several times until the heat's effect reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, immerse wool or cotton in the aforementioned dung, place it over the aching tooth, and then place a heated iron on top of that until the iron reaches the cavity of the tooth.
However, cauterization with fire involves using a bronze or iron probe, and making sure it is thick enough so that the heat doesn't reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired form, place it on the tooth, and hold it there until it cools; repeat this several times, as the pain may disappear either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, by pressure of that cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good dung and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the method of cauterization.
And cauterize whatever extremity you are able to.
And this is the method of the cannula:
Chapter 22.

When extrophulos are of phlegm and cold humors, and are not amenable to digestion by medicines, and you desire the digestion of them easily, then you ignite the cautery with the form of which is this following: having two open ends, so that smoke may escape from the cautery at the other end, and place that ignited one on the abscess once, and another if needed, until it reaches the bottom of the abscess.

And then treat it with ointment, bandages, and cloths until it is healed.

And then treat it with what was previously described until it is healed.

Chapter 25.

And then heat the cautery, having two heads; and this is its form:
Then cauterize the tendon until it penetrates through to the other side; and the figure of cauterization will then be of four-headed cauteries.

Chapter 26.
Of cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and excessive moisture enter the stomach, disrupting its composition and multiplying within it, and if you continue the methods of treatment and do not succeed, then the patient should lie on their back, extending their arms and legs. And separate those parts that are not joined, so that they create resistance.
And if the depth of the cautery is the quantity of two-thirds of the thickness of the cord.
And let the figure of the cautery be according to this shape without additions.  And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this shape; and this should be a cautery in the shape of a circle according to this shape:
And let the stomach be cauterized by pricking it where the cautery has created a fear or alarm,  and that you prick the stomach in the amount you desire with a probe, and then cauterize that with cauteries in those pricks;  and then cure it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
Concerning the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain in the liver is caused by coldness, or humidity, or great windiness, until its normal condition is expelled through excess, and the patient is treated as described and not differently, then the patient must be lying on their back, and make three cauteries according to this shape and this same size onto the liver under the ribs, where a person's body lies down.
And the space between each cautery should be the width of a finger, and the cauteries should be the length of the body's straight side.
And do not press with the cautery too much.
And if the amount of what is cauterized is the size of half a finger's breadth, and no more should be added; and if the patient is standing on their feet, and if not standing, then lying down, with their legs spread, and their arms raised.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these cauteries with a knife-cautery, when you have skill and prudence in the art.
And be careful not to proceed too much in the cauterization, and cauterize only the size of a fingertip, so that the entire body is cauterized and reaches the intestines, for the fingertip is thin here; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of the puncture of a liver abscess with cautery.
When an inflammation or abscess is present in the liver, and you want to know if that abscess is in the liver's flesh or its covering, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience significant weight and sharp pain.
And if it is in the covering of the liver, the pain is sharp and intense, and often the doctors are already exhausted in treating it.
Therefore, the patient should be laid on their side; then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then apply the cautery, which is shaped like a rod, the form of which is shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the pus is consumed, until it reaches the fibrous tissue, until it becomes healthy.
And then treat that as a cure for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not appropriate to use this type of cautery unless it is one whose use in the art of medicine is well-established, and by which many illnesses have been known.  However, sometimes, with some experience, it is risky to use this method on this operation. It is better to leave it to us.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cauterization of the roots of aristolochia for cold pleurisy, done in this manner, is effective.  This means that you should take a long, dried root of aristolochia, a pointed long root that is found, and which is the thickness of a finger.
Then, immerse it in oil and heat it over a fire.  Then, cauterize with a cautery in the area between the continuation of the collarbone and the neck; and with two smaller cauteries guiding under, slightly descending towards the area below the beard.  And with two cauteries on the area descending between the 5th rib and the 6th, angled slightly towards the back. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest/stomach and with another on the stomach, and with three cauteries in the back; and with one cautery in the area between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, beneath the cautery located between the shoulders.
And it is not appropriate to hold the cautery in your hand, instead it should be held in the manner of a cautery used in practice.
And some ancient doctors said that some men use a cautery made of iron, resembling a ray, and heat it, placing it on the area between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess and the pus is discharged, as we have described in the case of a liver abscess.
But this cauterization is not without risk, for one might accidentally cause a fistula in the affected area, which would be incurable.
Chapter 30.
Concerning the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat spleen disease using the methods previously described in the division, and your treatment doesn't work, then cauterization should be performed in three ways, all of which are proper. One method is to make three or four cauterization lines along the length of the spleen, following the shape of the cauteries for the liver, as previously illustrated.
And there should be a space of about one finger's width between each cautery mark, and the method of cauterization should be the same as that used for the liver.
And do not burn your hand with the cautery; and let the patient be properly positioned on their back.
And there is another method of cauterization, which is to apply the cautery with two applications, as described in the chapter on muscle dislocation.  Lift the ribs which are on the spleen's side,  towards the patient's left side.
And your lifting should be along the length of the body, so that the cauteries are made across the width of the body.
And then, quickly apply the two cauteries, burning intensely, to the point where they penetrate the ribs on the other side.
And then remove the cautery; and thus four cauteries have been made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three applications.
And then you treat the cautery, since it has stopped emitting smoke after many days, for this is more important than the other previous actions in the cure.
Chapter 31.
Of the cauterization of dropsy.
Cautery does not apply to dropsy, but to something else specifically.
When you treat dropsy with the methods of treatment we have described in the division and your treatment does not work, then it is necessary that you cauterize with four cauteries around the navel, with one cautery on the stomach, another on the liver, another on the spleen, and two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, one cautery on the right side of the foot, and another cautery on the stomach. And the depth of the cautery should be proportional to the severity of the condition.
And then leave the cautery open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not evacuate the illness from the wound after the cautery with what is necessary, for this will add two intentions to it, and hasten its healing with an additional aid.
The shape of the cautery used to cauterize the abdomen should be a wedge-shaped, following the shape of the previous one.  The cautery used to cauterize the back should be olive-shaped, following the shape of the preceding one.
Chapter 32.
Of cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are swollen with dropsy and filled with yellow water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the back of the foot, in the space between the malleolus and the anular region.  Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it. Then, raise your hand, and don't completely lower it again, because the yellow water is stubborn.
The cautery should be shaped like this:  Then make two cauteries on both thighs, and on each thigh, use the knife-like tip of the cautery. And the cauterization should follow the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and two cauteries on each thigh.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that any remaining water drains from it.
And then treat it with the methods we have described.
Chapter 33.
Of cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is affected by cold and dampness, weakening the digestive and retentive powers, located in the stomach and intestines, this is treated with the methods of treatment mentioned, and if the patient tolerates cauterization, then cauterize the area with a large cautery on their stomach, following the method for cauterizing the stomach previously described, using a circular cautery and four smaller cauteries in a circle around the navel, a small, pointed cautery, and a cautery on the flank, or two cauteries.

Chapter 34.
Of cauterization of old hemorrhoids.
When there are old hemorrhoids caused by thick, cold humors or corrupted moisture, and they are not cured by the methods mentioned earlier, then cauterize with three cauteries on the pus below the spine of the lower back, a small, triangular cautery, and a cautery above the navel on both sides.
And if you fear that their stomach is cold, that their food is not digested, and their face often appears inflamed, then cauterize the area on their stomach with a large cautery as described before; and with one cautery on the liver, and another cautery on their spleen with a pointed cautery. Leave the cautery open for a period of time, and then treat them until they are healed.
Chapter 35.
Of cauterization of the armpit near the removal of them (or, more literally, near the excision of the armpit).

Chapter 36.
Of the cauterization of fistulas that are formed naturally in the affected areas.
When nature does not respond to the treatment we have described in its place, and the patient is distressed by this, it should be treated with cauterization.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula naturally in the back, and it is old, and has pus, corrupted matter, and therefore, the most appropriate thing to do is to probe it with a fine probe, and then ascertain the depth of the fistula with the aforementioned probe.
Then heat the cautery, which is similar to a ray. Then apply that heated cautery to this fistula, and according to the depth to which the probe entered.
And repeat the application of the cautery until those corrupted tissues are burned once, twice, or three times, according to your need.
And be careful not to burn nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or the intestine, then be careful of all those areas; and do not do this unless the fistula is in a fleshy area and you believe it is not penetrating.
And then treat the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the materials are removed from it, and it remains like that for a long time, then know that it is now completely cured.
And if the materials are not removed from it, then know that it is penetrating or in a pre-existing corrupted area, or that this comes from the causes which will bring remembrance to their place.

Chapter 41.

Albucasis says:  "Oh, children, of this type of cautery we have used little due to the fear of its effects, and because few have been found to withstand it.

Take an equal part of alkali and an un-extinguished ash,  grind them, and place them in a new, shallow bowl, the bottom of which has a small hole, sized to fit the patient's wound.
And place another, glazed, bowl underneath the bottom of the first bowl. It was said that the remedy, when applied in a bath or medicated poultice, quickly worked.

And this method of cautery is called cautery of the veins (or "cautery of the kine").

Chapter 43.
Of cauterization at the beginning of a swelling.
Often this ailment afflicts young children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it affects their limbs and causes difficulty with raising and moving them.

Chapter XLIV.
On the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors that have infiltrated each part of the body, and when pain arises in the feet, it is customarily called gout by physicians.
And therefore, if gout caused by cold humors is treated with other methods of care and the pain does not subside, then use cautery to relieve it.
And you should cauterize the area around the affected joint of the foot with several cauteries.
And let the cautery be an olive-shaped one, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary to make punctures on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if many cauteries are needed, then cauterize the area, but do not press too hard with your hand on the cautery beforehand; it should only be around the size of a fingertip.
And if the pain reaches the knee and thigh, then apply the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pain is only in the hands, and is called "suagra", then make punctures in the outline of the veins, as if circling them, making punctures along those two lines.
And if pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the outline of the veins above each finger and above the hand's knuckle.
And if the pain spreads to the knees or muscles over a few days, then cauterize each of the affected areas from all sides.
And you shall not abandon the patient to a good regimen and the taking of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is expelled through this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
Of the cauterization of ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, and the intestines protrude before the hernia in the groin area, and this is the beginning of the problem, then it is necessary that you instruct the patient to abstain from his normal diet for a time, and that he use soothing substances so that the opening in his abdomen subsides.
And then hold the patient's abdomen between your hands, and instruct him to hold his breath until the hernia or intestines are fully out.

Chapter 46.
Of the cauterization of twisting (or torsion).
When, in some limb, pain arises due to injury or impact, and that pain persists for a long time, and you do not find a cure through medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.

Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some leprous individuals receive considerable benefit from cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.

Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of sores and other similar afflictions.
Often, beneath the diseased foot, a condition called a sore develops, a large, deep wound, which damages the foot; and cauterization in these cases is done in two ways: cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But cautery with fire involves heating a concave cautery resembling a small, sharp knife, made of iron according to the size needed to cover the entirety of the sore, and made of fine, thin pieces.
And then place that heated cautery on the sore, and then rotate your hand with the cautery around the sore until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it for three days until it begins to heal. Hold your hand, and rotate the cannula with pressure all around, ensuring the edge of the cannula presses on the root of the sore.  The hot water will find its way to the base of the sore.  Let the patient feel the effect of the hot water for an hour.  Then remove it, as the sore, with its roots, will be eradicated.
And thus do this to these and then to the others until you have applied it to all of them that have this issue in the body.

Chapter 7.
Of the treatment of ear obstructions.
Sometimes children inherit this from their mothers' unperforated wombs, and this also occurs in some men, a blockage in the ear canal due to a plaque or a growth within; and this blockage is sometimes formed at the entrance of the ear canal which prevents drainage, and sometimes is in the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the entrance, which does not allow drainage, is in the most difficult cases to cure.
And in those cases where drainage is possible, you should place the patient's ear towards the sun and watch it. And therefore if a blockage appears, open it with a subtle instrument, of which this is the form:
If its end has a small width, with one part sharp and the other not; and the instrument has two thin strands so that it does not injure the ear.
And therefore if the blockage is due to flesh growing in the ear or its canal, then remove it with fine forceps, cutting out the flesh with forceful ease until all the flesh is removed.
And if the blockage is in the front of the ear, then take a thin, sharp reed and slightly warm it over a fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have sensed a blockage that the patient finds difficult due to the difficulty of hearing, ease the blockage with care; and be careful not to harm the nerves of the ear.
Then place a cloth of a size fitting the hole you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing, or you take a poultice and place it in boiling water, pulverise it over, and use it; and if the ear is already suppurated, then you must remove the poultice and replace it with another poultice wrapped in a compress made of roasted oil, until the abscess subsides; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cool water and place it on the ear, and use another treatment that constricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on bleeding.
Chapter 8.
Of the treatment of a boil that forms on the eyelids.
Boils that appear on the eyelids of the eyes sometimes are moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or tweezers, and cut them away from their roots, and then apply a poultice to the areas.
But if pain with blood flow occurs, then cauterize those areas with a thin, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is done near the incisions, and often they return if any part of the roots remain; and when cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids.
Often in the eyelids there is some cause which resembles a swelling in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called swelling.
And it is a collection of thick moisture on the eyelid below and above.
Therefore, the operation for them is to consider whether the swelling is proceeding from within the eyelid and is spreading in all directions; and its cause is slight.
Therefore, make a slit across it in the width, and then gently scrape it from all sides until it opens, and then suspend it with a forceps and cut it.
And if the figure (swelling) is large, then you adjust it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not experience any harm from it, because the ointment will restore it and cause it to heal.
And if the swelling is located towards the inner parts of the eyelid, near the surface inside, then lift the eyelid and suspend the swelling with forceps, in order to make an incision, and press on it from all sides.  And if the eyelid is elongated by the incision, this causes no harm.
And after the incision, wash the wound with salt water; and treat the area with something that promotes healing until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the treatment of a boil on the eyelid above the eye.
A boil is a painful swelling that forms in the eyelid tissues above the eye, and it's particularly common in children.  It irritates the eyes of those afflicted and frequently causes them discomfort in the morning.  They cannot look at the sunlight due to this, and their eyes become watery.
And therefore, you see them lying on their faces constantly, sleeping on their faces.  The eyelids below their eyebrows are swollen, and the presence of a boil is apparent near the eye.  And when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the boil within them.
And a method of treatment is that the patient rests their head on your lap, and you place a cloth over them and apply heat.  Make a circle around the boil that encompasses the entire swelling, and place that over it, pressing with your fingers to force the boil to the center of the circle.  Then, make a small incision in the center of the inflamed area, using a lancet, and the incision should not be larger than the one made in a bloodletting procedure.
But in the incision, you must break open the skin entirely until you reach the boil, for in many cases the boil is caused by a white pus-like substance.
Then remove that [pus] with cloths that you have wrapped between your fingers, working it out and turning it between your right and left hands until it is gone.
And be careful not to push too hard into the incision with the lancet, so that you do not pierce the boil and damage it.
But if the boil does not appear at the beginning of the incision, but rather you must push a little into the incision with ease until pus comes out; then remove it as we have said; then soak cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them over the area and bind them with powders.
And some sprinkle salt and place it in the cavity of the incision, to absorb any remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if you encounter a hot abscess in the area, then treat it with cooling plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Of ways of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes there are growths beneath the natural skin, and they become hardened,  these press on the eye and cause various illnesses, like constant tears and softening of the eyelids and tissues, until this becomes the cause of the eye's destruction, which is called 'tactimir' of the eye.  It is treated in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with sharp medicine, as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization; or it is treated by incision and suturing; or by using canes, as I will describe.
And therefore you should place the patient's head on your lap; then, gently hold the eyelid with your left hand, and if it's already repositioned, good.  Otherwise, insert a needle under the eyelid, where the tissue is most prominent beneath, and push the needle through, with the thread above it. Do this close to the edge of the eyelid, near the base of the hairs.  Pull the thread up and out from beneath the eyelid, and reposition the eyelid using the needle.
Then, using a sharp instrument, cut into the tissue under the eyelid's hairs, from the main tear duct down to the smaller one.  Then, remove the thread and place small, powdered cotton or cloth under the eyelid.  Next, gently compress the eyelid with a bandage, forming it into a shape like a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be adjusted according to how much the eyelid is raised, as this varies greatly between individuals; some require a larger portion of the eyelid to be cut, according to the amount of swelling.
And then use the instrument on the two lines you have drawn, starting from the main tear duct and moving towards the smaller one. And if your incision is near the natural hairs, it should be as large as the king's finger.
Then place the bone forceps at one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape that entire area.  Next, using suture material (like fine wool thread), bring the two edges of the wound together. Clean the blood away, and if desired, use any available sticky substance to glue any excess thread or material to the two eyebrows. Otherwise, don't worry about it.
And leave the sutures in place for three or four days, and then treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised towards the sealing of the wound and its adhesion; but sutures are better.  Therefore, the ancient physicians say this.
However, be diligent in your care of the patient, and it is a good procedure, and there is no need for fear.
There is another method in treating this, which is to shape the eyelid like a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid with three separate and joined forceps, in this shape:
However, if this is not possible, hold the forceps, and if this is not suitable, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other suture near the major lacrimal duct, and the second suture near the minor lacrimal duct, and join the sutures equally between your fingers, and then lift your hand with a lifting motion, lifting the entire skin flap in the shape described.
Then bring the two edges of the wound together with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if an abscess arises near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with poultices and similar remedies, and with ointments.
And sometimes the eyelashes turn inward at the eyelid; then you should use what we have said about incision on that, but sutures are more suitable in each case.
To treat with canes is according to this form: And you are to turn the eyelid and cut the figure inside as I have described; and then, with skill, make two thin canes or splints whose length is equal to the length of the eyelid and whose width is smaller than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you hold the threads.
Then carefully attach each one to the excess of the eyelid, and tightly bind the canes to each part, and leave it for some days, because the flesh will die and become useless and rotten until it falls off by itself.
But if it is delayed, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Therefore, when it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised and the skin is not sore.
And this is the method for two canes, which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of lifting sore skin in the eye with a needle when the skin is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough hairs, irritating the eye, then the operation on it is that you take a fine needle and place it on that fine linen cloth, and then you adjust the extremities of it, and tie each end very subtly. And the length of the threads should be about the length of one palm.
And then you attach another fine thread below it, and tie the ends of that if you wish.
And then position the patient's head on your lap, and close to the sun so that your work is clear, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then, insert the needle into the root of the hair that needs to be removed, and pierce the eyelid quickly until you pull that hair out over the natural hair.
And then pull the hair out over the top and place it in the container, and there should be one or two or three hairs, but no more.
And then pull your hand with that until the hair is removed, along with any other hairs adhering to the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the follicle is pulled but the hair isn't removed, you will pull the follicle in different directions with the ointment you prepared, until the follicle is removed from the opening.
And you will observe this with the eye's vision.  Then you will remove the follicle and the ointment, and everyone will clean and close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a longer hair from the natural hairs, and raise it with that, or leave it until it has grown to a reasonable length, and then you raise that.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of a scratch that occurs on the upper eyelid.
When a scratch appears on the eye, and there is a contraction of the eyelid, whether on the upper, right or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, incision, or cauterization, and it is called leporine, this scratch or abrasion is either natural or accidental and is caused by the poor healing of wounds, incisions, or cauterizations, or similar ill-treated injuries.
The method of treatment in such cases is to break apart the scar tissue and separate the edges, and place a piece of soft linen between them, and bandage them until healed.
And you should not use treatments that dry out or constrict it, for if you do this, the scratch will return worse than ever before.
Use remedies that soften it, such as fenugreek, and apply a poultice made from water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil, and soak a piece of cloth in it and treat it with that.
And the key to your treatment is that you should break open any unhealed part according to its initial shape.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing more than a correction of a scar that does not restore the area to its original form.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the treatment of a scar on the lower eyelid.
This scar on the lower eyelid, truly called a scar, may be either of natural or accidental origin.
But an accidental scar is caused by a wound, or a cut, or a cautery, or other similar injuries.
And the method of its treatment is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and pass it from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the two extremities of the flesh.
Then, gently pull the flesh apart with the needle and stitch it together with the needle, and cut with a wide spatula. And so if the eyelid's shape returns as necessary, it is good; and if not, take a strip of cloth and place it in the split, and with that, reposition the eyelid, and close the two gaps on the lower eyelid's edge.
And let the ends of the two slits, which you have made, be the two angles of the incision, until they meet, and let their joining make a shape when they come together.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh that is on the lower sharp edge of the eyelid.
And then join the separated parts together with the wool thread; and then treat them with the softening medicines and poultices we mentioned until healed.
And if a repair is required through a slit, suture, or cautery, then you must make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid, as previously described. And then separate with a poultice between the two lips, as we have said.
In summary of the treatment for a displaced eyelid, whether above or below, let the operation proceed according to the preparation of the displaced eyelid, and this is in various forms.
And the skilled craftsman always prepares each possible manner until he restores the figure to its natural form, or nearly so, and the patient, according to what is appropriate for the operation and the instruments in the best arrangements.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this adhesion occurs in such a way that the eyelid is firmly glued to the white part of the eye, and prevented from its natural movement and functions, then you must insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid, and lift it up, or extend it with forceps.
And then you carefully cut the adhesion with a thin blade, not excessively sharp, so the patient isn't distressed by the procedure, and leave the blade so it doesn't injure the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should be precise enough to easily sever the adhesion, until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the entire adhesion is removed.
And then you apply salt water to the eye, with which you wash it; or apply distilled vinegar dissolved in water.
And then you gently separate the eyelid from the eye, and place wool soaked in egg white over the eye, and then on the third day use healing agents until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of incisions of the nail and the protruding flesh of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is of two types: either it is nervous, and resembles a hard, fine chip; or it is non-nervous, resembling frozen white moisture, which when touched by an iron, or if forced to be taken with forceps, is separated and the forceps are not stuck to it.
And both types do not originate from anywhere but the large tear duct, until it gradually grows to cover the eye and blocks the light and movement of the eye.
But the way to proceed with its incision is that the patient places their head on your shoulder, then open their eyes wide, raise the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with forceps of little leverage and pull it away from the surrounding tissue. Then take a needle and insert into it a horse, ox, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or repeatedly thin the end of the needle and place it in the middle of the nail, penetrating it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and pull it away from the surrounding tissue, and scrape the nail bed with the hair, which is touching the pupil. And thus you remove it with the hair until the back of it.
And then you cut the remaining part at the root of the tear duct with a very thin scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural flesh of the tear duct intact so that by cutting it, there is no continuous flow of tears.
Sometimes the nail with only forceps, or with thread, as we have said, and then be scraped with a lion's scalpel, of which this is its shape:
And be careful that with the scalpel you do not touch the cornea and cause a rupture, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then put a little crushed salt or a prepared powder in the wound, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with whatever is necessary until it heals.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it embedded in the nail bed, then often this type of nail is treated with medicines and healed as I have described in the division.
And if you want to dissect it, then you open the patient's wound and take a sharp, fine lion's scalpel of this shape: with subtlety. And if it is often inflamed and festering and has pus in it, then distil from your flask into the wound a prepared powder, or with rose water, or with crushed salt, and cover the wound until the next day.
And then repeat the operation until all that is pus is removed, unless a hot abscess remains in the wound; in that case, leave it, and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it with care until it is healed.
If an inflammation of the tear ducts is found in the wound causing great discomfort, it should be suspended with a bandage; and cut a small portion of the inflamed area, but do not cut too deep, so as to avoid causing a flow of tears.
And then instill into the tear duct rose water and prepared powder until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of boils and of what arises from inflamed flesh in the wound.
Sometimes in the wounds of some people red flesh grows and spreads until it covers the pupil, or it approaches or tightens around the eyelids, and forcefully compresses the eyelids on the outside, resembling a row of small towers (or a balustrade).
But the procedure for him is that the patient rests his head on your lap; then open the wounds and gather all that flesh with appropriate forceps, and hold it with a forceps or pliers; then cut the flesh at the beginning until it is all finished with the scalpel, and be careful not to damage the wound during your procedure.
And your incision should be with the same instrument with which we described the incision of the nail, or with small forceps, with which the flesh is gathered according to the form required by this chapter.
Thus, when your incision is complete and all that flesh has been removed, then fill the wound with crushed salt, or apply red powder similar to corrosive medicines, and place a poultice of egg white over the wound until a safe, suppurating healing has occurred.
And if any pus or foul blood remains, and you fear a suppurating abscess, then leave the wound and treat the abscess with whatever will resolve the abscess, and then again operate on it until it is healed.  Do likewise with the adhering flesh that you find in the wound of this kind.
However, you should avoid any treatment of the wound through natural or accidental means, such as using iron, and do not put your hand on any of these procedures until you see that the limb can tolerate that operation.

Of the collection of pus in the wound.
Pus that forms, red and thick on the wound, and prevents its healing, and should be cleaned from the wound for many days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the wound containing pus is strong and there is no other illness than pus, then you collect that pus.
And you command the patient to place their head on your lap, and then suspend those veins with a bandage or two, according to your judgment; and let that bandage be of a subtle double layer in this manner:
or two bandages gradually wrapped together in this manner:
Then gently collect those veins with subtle force, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins to be removed from the wound and are rolled up.
And be careful not to damage the wound with the edges of the spatula; and if your operation is in the middle of the day before the sun, be very firm in your operation so that you only cut those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill green or red vinegar into the wound, so that with its sharpness it corrodes what is left of the pus.
But if it is not possible for you to collect everything at that time, then you bandage everything with what relieves his pain, and leave it for a few days until the pain is relieved and a hot abscess is assured.
And then repeat according to the same method until it is healed.
