Chapter 19.
Of the reduction of a nasal injury.
When you treat a nasal injury with a cautery or a sharp, burning medicine, according to the narrative that preceded it, and it is not healed, then there is no use in it, but you should make incisions on the abscess to promote its maturation, and remove all the moisture and pus from it until the bone is exposed.
And therefore, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness there, then you scrape it with an instrument whose shape is this:
And it is called a "sharp head," and it is made of iron, and the head is round like a nut, which is made pointed by puncturing with a file or a chisel (like in the following illustration).
And place that on the corrupted area of the bone, then turn it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And do this several times, and then restore the area with drying and astringent substances.
Therefore, if the area is consolidated, and good flesh has grown there, and the body is free from pus and remains for 40 days, and is not renewed and no abscess or accident occurs there, then you know that it is healed.
And if not, then it is not a true cure, but rather to reduce the fracture to the shape of the nose, and in this manner:  and you must expose the second bone with iron or with sharp medicine, and once the bone is exposed, then you take a strip according to this shape:
Let the tip of the iron be triangular, and the shaft be thin and tapered towards the tip like this (or, in this way).
Then place that on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and move your hand a little away from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the strip until it penetrates the entire bone, and the patient feels air escaping when you move their hand.
Therefore, then restore the area with astringent, drying medicines, as was shown to you.
Then, as the flesh heals and the area hardens, the material that spread outwards returns to the nose, so this is easier for the patient.
Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the prominence of the eye.
When the eye protrudes completely and you observe no harm or diminution in vision, then you should leave the patient alone, and then follow it, and then apply leeches to the neck without incision and suck thoroughly; and then apply a liniment made of aconite, aloe, and incense and sarcocolla to the eye, and then press the plaster on the eye with many powders.
And the method for the powders is to take very thin linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four folds; and make many of these.
And then place those cloths one over the other according to the quantity the eye needs. And then press on those with a firm cloth with strong pressure; and repeat the leeches with suction without incision; and then remove the leeches and keep the eye tightly covered day and night.
But if meanwhile the swelling has softened, then it should be very tightly bound.
And then remove the bandage every day and night, as we have said.
And if you see the eye already reduced, good; and if not, then repeat the poultice with powders, the pressure, and the leeches until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the prominence of the uvea (or vascular layer of the eye).
When a rupture occurs in the uvea, and it is protruding and has become apparent outside the eyelids, resembling a grape cluster, and you wish to incise it, then the operation in that location is as we have described, that you insert a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower part to the upper part, and then place another needle, on which there is a double thread from the tear duct area, and cause that to penetrate and let the first needle follow its path; and then you cut the spot where the double thread is, and with that thread you lift the portion of the uvea that surrounds the needle, and hold it firmly.
And then remove the needle and place over the eye wool soaked in egg white and leave it until the thread comes away with the grape-like protrusion.
And then treat the eye with that which strengthens it until the injury is healed.
And sometimes, gathering from all sides with a circular cloth, a large protruding area in the eye is contained, and then it is pierced with a fine needle that reaches the surface of the eye, and the white fluid flows out, and the eye descends and quickly recedes inward.
And then hold it firm until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of blindness.
This illness, called blindness, is nothing but a fluid that gathers in the eye, resembling descending water, but it is not that.
But the method of treatment is to have the patient sit on a prepared and upright seat, and then grasp their head with both your hands, and gently hold it until you see the fluid descend to the lower parts, and then it is dried up and does not descend, and the light is restored, and the patient sees things as before.
And if it does not descend further, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the fluid is not prepared by what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands, and then take a fine, soft, and gentle substance, and apply it over the affected area, just inside the transition between the conjunctiva and the cornea (on the empty part), until the fluid is expelled.
Then, once it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye, in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of water descending from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of water in the division, and which ones should be pierced with a lancet, declaring the reason, then according to the health.
Then you should place the patient between your hands on a bed, and tightly bandage the eye.

And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right eye.
And then place the tip of the almond near the brow bone, along the thickness of the eyebrow, in the white of the eye on the side of the smaller tear duct, and then press on the almond gently and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has now reached the empty space.
And the distance from the almond to the [desired] point should be the same as the distance between the pupil and the end of the black part [the iris], which is the brow bone of the eye; for you will see through that pupil, through the clarity of the cornea, with the vision of the eye.
And then place the almond into the area where the water [presumably fluid] is, and then pull it down a couple of times, and if the fluid descends immediately, then you see the patient immediately focus their vision on it, and the almond is in their eye.
And then rest for a little while; if the fluid has descended, then you pause again before the almond is thrown.
And then, if it is dried up and does not descend, then you extract the almond with ease, and gently rub it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in water and wash the inside of the eye with it; and then apply to the outside of the eye lint or a bandage soaked in warmed oil and egg white and bandage the eye.
But we, and those of our time, put [something] on that crushed [substance] with egg white.

And place this upon him in a way that his nature will be soothed, and he will not move his head to either the right or left at all.
And let the ligament be according to its disposition until the third day.
And then, remove it from that dark room, and remove the painful sight, and have him see some things, and then return the ligament until the seventh day.

Then you remove his sight and place a bandage over his face, through which his vision is directed downwards for several days, him remaining in the dark room.
And gradually he is released from that room and returned to his usual activities.

Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When various growths of flesh arise in the nose, some of which are caused by something akin to a very heavy scorpion or a large polyp; and this illness is called polyps because it resembles that depicted fish. And from it is cancerous flesh, ulcerated, dark colored; and from it is flesh that is like a lion, not of dark color.
Therefore, for that flesh which is leonine, not fraudulent nor cancerous, you should have the patient sit between your hands, exposed to the sun, and open the patient's nostrils, and pierce the affected flesh with your lancet. And then extract that outwardly, and then separate what you have grasped of it with a sharp, thin, pointed instrument, one piece at a time, until you know that all the flesh has been removed; that if any part of it remains from which excision is possible, then you should cut that away with one of the thin instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it is swollen with blood or a hot abscess, then you apply what is necessary; or if it is a malignant abscess, then cauterize and cut it until the blood is extracted and all cavities are cleared.
And then, after the incision, put vinegar and water, or wine, into the nose. But if the nose is open and moisture flows from it to the palate, then you know that it is already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that there is still unremoved flesh in the pus, surrounding the bones of the nose, which have not been reached by the excision.
Therefore, you should introduce some thick, fatty material, and put many little pieces of it, placing the quantity of a finger or less between each piece.  Apply this to the patient and insert one end of the fatty substance into the nose, using a probe or any other suitable tool.
And after doing this, stimulate the patient's sense of smell until it reaches the nasal passages, and exits through their throat, for many times children are taught this at school and it is an easy thing to do.
Then hold the two ends of the fatty substance, one end protruding from the nose and the other remaining inside, and carefully separate the flesh with the pieces of fat.  Continue doing this until you see that the flesh is entirely surrounded by the fatty material.
Then remove the fat, and apply a poultice to the nose near the area where the blood was drawn.  Do this for three days or more, until the poultice has dissolved any remaining flesh.
And finally, insert a lead cannula into the nose for several days until healing is complete.
But if you require a more thorough cure, use this method.
And this is the form of the instrument for nasal purging, with which oils and medicines are instilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or brass, resembling a small, open lamp, or a small shell, and the opening similar.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a reed, and the cannula of the nasal purging instrument be flat, open; to which there should be a handle at its rear, with which you hold it when heating oil or whatever juices and subtle substances you desire within it.
Chapter 25.
Of ointments applied to the nostrils.
Often an ointment is applied to the nostrils, and it is applied and augmented over several days until the healing of the area is very obvious.
And therefore you should intervene at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then place on the affected area either a cautery or a burning medicine that is suitable for that spot.
However, if its removal is delayed until it has become large, then consider if it is hardened, tough, dark in color, and insensitive.  If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a deep-seated abscess; because often the one who cuts into this abscess will cause great harm to the person who had it.
And if the abscess is easily touched and is not dark in color, and incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the patient and cut it without fear, and treat the area with that which dries and constricts it until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when the continuity is broken by plague or similar causes.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, little success is achieved in this operation except in some men.
Therefore, when encountering such a cause, consider if it is fresh plague with its own blood, and if so, you should join the two sides of the wound with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the break in continuity are already separated and each is healthy, then you must scrape each part of its apparent scab until blood comes out. Then, join the two edges with sutures and tighten them.  Dust the sutures with powdered dragon's blood and crushed herbs, and place a piece of diaphanous plaster, or some other consolidating plaster, over the powder. Leave it bound for two or three days, and then remove it, replace the plaster, and leave it until the pus is gone.  Then treat with ointment until healed.
But the method of suturing is that you either join the break in continuity with needles, as we have described in suturing the belly, or join the break in continuity with glue, as you have been taught.
Chapter 27.
Of extracting those things that appear between the two edges within the parts.
Often, hard abscesses resembling lumps of fat appear inside the lips in some men's mouths, and some are smaller and others are larger.
Therefore, you should separate the lips and cut away each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then fill the space with scraped sage until the blood is stopped, and then wash the area with sour wine and treat it with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, flesh adhering to the gums, which the ancients called albulus, forms on the gum. Then, you should detach it with forceps or cut it at its root, and allow the pus or blood to drain.
And then place on the area scraped sage or other astringent or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then you must cut it again and cauterize it, for the cautery will not allow it to return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning toothache with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both on the inner and outer parts, and between the gums, there are hard, rough, black, and sometimes yellow or greenish crusts, which cause corruption in the gums and the teeth become decayed.
And thus, you must have the patient sit between your hands, and rest the patient's head on your lap, and scrape the teeth where those crusts and similar dark things appear, until no trace of them remains, and do this for the blackness, yellowness and other discolorations until they are cleaned.
Therefore, if that which is on those teeth at the beginning of the procedure has been removed, good.  But if not, then repeat the procedure on them the following day, and the second, and the third, until it reaches the desired state.
And know that teeth require many different kinds of scrapers and various shapes depending on your preparation for the operation. And therefore, the scraper with which the tooth is scraped from the inner part is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, with other shapes, and these scraper shapes must be prepared for you, which you will see soon.
Chapter 30.
Of the eradication of teeth.
You should treat the tooth's pain with all diligence and take your time with any tooth extraction, for there is no remedy that can replace the tooth's functionality when extracted, as it is a sublimated substance that needs consideration in the extraction process.
However, when the patient insists on having that tooth extracted, you must be absolutely certain which tooth is the painful one, for many times the pain deceives the patient, suggesting it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one. Then the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have seen this in the practice of barbers.
Therefore, how should you be certain which tooth is the painful one, and that you know it well?  Then you must scarify the area around the tooth with a substance possessing some virtue, until it has loosened the tooth from the gum on every side. Then, gently remove it with your fingers, or with fine scissors, beginning small and gradually until you have extracted it.
And afterward, if needed use strong forceps for better grip or firmness, and place the patient's head between your knees to hold it steady so it doesn't move. Then, carefully pull out the tooth in a straight manner, so as not to break it, and to avoid causing further pain to the patient.
And therefore, if it is not fully dislodged, then take one of those instruments and place it beneath the tooth from all sides with ease, and exert the removal in the same manner as the initial attempt.
However, if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you must fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with the tip of a thin stick, so that it is not broken during your application of forceps.
And you must carefully open the surrounding gum with scarification from all sides, and be very careful to avoid breaking it and leaving a portion of it, lest you cause more pain to the patient than the initial pain.
And be careful not to do what some ignorant barbers do in their eagerness and rashness when extracting teeth, without following what we have described; for many times they cause serious illnesses in people, which are even harder to treat than the initial problem, leaving the roots entirely or partially embedded, or pulling the teeth out with some of the jawbone as we have often witnessed.
And after the extraction, the mouth should be rinsed with warm wine or vinegar with salt.
And if there is bleeding from this, as often happens, then apply a small quantity of alum to the wound, and if that doesn't stop the bleeding, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers used to remove the tooth at the beginning should be of a long end, a short and thick handle, so they don't bend when using them.
And they should be like thick handles so that when you squeeze with them they don't bend; the ends should be short.
And they should be made of Indian steel, with straight ends; on the ends of these are teeth which interlock and grip tightly by constriction.  Sometimes the ends of these are shaped like the beak of a stork (i.e., a kind of hook), and are very strong and constricting.

Concerning the removal of teeth and the extraction of bones from fractured jaws.

Their ends should be made smooth like a file or a rasp.
And if you cannot remove it with these forceps or pliers, then you must cut into the root and expose all the flesh and insert the tool called a small lever, which is shaped like this:

with a triangular, curved or short end, with some thickness, and not too blunt so that it is not broken.

Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth protruding beyond the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural position, causing an irregular shape, especially in women, and particularly in young women, then you must consider: if a tooth is directly next to another tooth, and separation or a ligature is not possible, then remove that one; but if it is not adjacent to another tooth, then you cut into it using this instrument, which is shaped like a pig's snout, and is made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And your incision should be gradual, over several days, due to the hardness of the tooth, and so that it doesn't damage other teeth.
And if the protruding tooth is exposed for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file of this shape:
And it should be entirely made of Indian iron, and easily shaped; and it should have many tiny points, just like the file used to make needles, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then, finally, line it (the tooth) and extract it with some razors.
And if a tooth has a broken part that cuts the tongue, then you must file it until the sharpness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and it is filled so that the tongue is not injured and the speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver fillings.
When teeth are damaged by impact and displacement due to blows or bites, and the patient cannot firmly hold what is missing, and you have treated it with astringent medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then, because of their lack of solidity, restore those that are missing with gold fillings, and not silver, because silver turns green and can become spoiled in a few days, but gold is never altered.
And the filling should be of such a thinness and thickness according to the amount needed to fit between the teeth; and the method for placing it is to take the filling and place twice its amount between two healthy teeth, and then shape it with two curves of the filling between the broken ones, or if there is only one or many, until the material reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the process until you reach the starting point, and hold it gently and carefully until it is firmly fixed.
And your grip, with which you hold the filling against the roots of the teeth, must not be excessive.
And then trim the excess filling with forceps or scissors, and precisely shape it in each location, and blend it in and hide it between the healthy tooth and the broken tooth, so that it doesn't cause injury to the tongue. And then leave it in that tight, firm state.
But if it is loose or broken, hold it or them with another filling in such a way throughout the entire time.
And this is the method for teeth and the manner of restoration of two healthy teeth and two broken teeth, just as you would see these bound together:
And when one or two teeth are returned to approximately their socket, and they are re-grown as we have described, and they remain.
And only skilled and wise artisans can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is prepared, shaped like a tooth, and placed in the space where the tooth is missing, and is fastened as we said, and it remains and serves for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the constriction of the ligament which is in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which sometimes lies beneath the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, resulting from a wound that has healed.
And the operation on it involves opening the patient's mouth, their head resting on your shoulder; lifting their tongue, and then carefully cutting the nerve ligament along its length, until the tongue is released from its constriction.
However, if there is any hardness or knotting there, resulting from a healed wound, then you apply a cautery iron and cut along its length until you have severed the ligament and the knot has been loosened.
And be careful not to make a cut into the flesh that will cause a deep wound and lead to excessive bleeding.
And then wash the incision site with vinegar or cold water; and then place a linen pad under the tongue; this is a compress that the patient will wear under their tongue every night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, place powdered vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding continues, then cauterize the area with a suitable, blunt cautery iron. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the removal of a ranula under the tongue.
Sometimes, a boil resembling a small tumor develops under the tongue, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows significantly until it fills the mouth.
And the treatment involves opening the patient's mouth facing the sun and examining the abscess.
And if you see it of a dark color or black, hard, to which the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch that spot, for it is a cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then you place a lancet on it, and make a slight incision, and cut it from all sides; and if blood flows during your procedure, then place a crushed piece of alum (or vitriol) on top until the bleeding is stopped.
And then return to your work until you have removed it completely, until it has been washed with vinegar and salt; and treat it with further remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of abscesses from both ears, and of those that are in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When an abscess develops in the throat, these are glands that are collected together, which break open and are called the tonsils;  and when you treat them with what we have described in the division and they are not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, with little sensation, then do not touch it with an iron.
And if it is reddish in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron for the flow of blood; but let it remain until it has matured, and then either you will drain it, or it will burst open by itself.
And if it is white, smooth, and its root is thin, then that is what needs to be incised.
And the operation for it is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already rested itself with complete rest or if it has lessened with any diminution; and then have the sick person sit facing the sun, and their head be on your lap, and open their mouth, and the attendant, placing their hands around it, presses down the patient's tongue from below with a very delicate instrument, of which this is the shape:
And it should be made of silver or brass, and be thin like a knife.
So, as you press with that on the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, and you apply your gaze upon it, then take a lancet and place it into the opening, and pull it out to the sides as far as you can. Furthermore, when you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of this shape:
Furthermore, when you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut that with an instrument of this shape: and it is like forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is in line with the other, very sharp, which should be of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a scalpel, of which this is the shape: And let it be sharp on one side, and blunt on the other side.
And after you have incised one opening, cut the other following the same method of incision, and after the incision, the patient will recover from the cold.
However, if there is bleeding, stop it with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaf, or similar astringent substances, until the bleeding is controlled, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat or near the two ears, and incise them as I have described for the incision of two openings earlier.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat.  This abscess was dark-colored, insensitive, and had almost completely blocked her throat.  She was breathing with difficulty, and had been forbidden from eating or drinking, and she would have died if it had gone on for another day or two.
And the abscess had grown so large that two channels had formed before it finally burst through two openings.
Therefore, I incised it surgically and inserted a lancet into one of the openings, and pulled out a large amount.  Then I incised the area connecting to the other opening.  Then I opened her mouth, held her tongue down, and then inserted the lancet into the abscess.
And then I excised a large portion of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Thus, the woman's throat was completely cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, and then eat.  I continued to incise the abscess many times over a long period of time, and another abscess formed in the same location, which I incised again and again until it resolved.
Then I applied a cauterizing agent to her throat, which also helped; and afterward I left and did not know what God had done in that place near me.
Chapter 37.
Of the incision of a boil when it is suppurated.
When a sore throat affects the uvula, and it is suppurated and prolonged, it is then called a perpendicular sore; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a boil.
However, when it is cured with what we have said in the division, and you do not confirm your cure, and sometimes the boil has already been softened and is subtle, then you must incise that which is swollen and round; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and is not sensitive to touch, then you must try to incise it, for there is fear of the patient's condition in that.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, then you should have the patient sit down, and press the patient's tongue with an instrument, to observe its shape, and then place a probe on it, pull it down, and incise it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the incision of two sores.
And it is necessary not to incise it except what is aligned to the natural border without addition, for if you incise it further, you will damage their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have described in the incision of two sores, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by the incision, you must use a cautery without fear, and the way to cauterize it is only with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head on your lap, then press their tongue with the instrument we mentioned, and take the water we discussed in the chapter on cauterization, and with that, apply a live, not thick nor thin, cautery, and fill the cavity of the instrument of which the shape is [described].
Let the tip of the cavity where the medicine is placed be like the cavity of a leech cup.  Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and the patient lies on their side so that any saliva or mucus from the mouth flows due to the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it onto their throat and irritates it.
And then, apply your hand with the medicine and press it onto the uvula for half an hour until you see it blackened and softened by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the tip of the leech, and dip the cotton into the medicine.  Insert the leech with the cotton in a tube above it until the cotton adheres to the tube.  Repeat this as many times as necessary to achieve the desired effect on the uvula's cauterization, and then leave it to dry and fall off in about three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it.  Then, after the cauterization, clean the area around the uvula with cotton soaked in butter, and remove any traces of medicine. Wash with cold water, and apply an external remedy and internal healing until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incisions and less likely to cause fear.
Sometimes the uvula is cured by something more subtle than cauterization or incisions, through fumigation in this way: take balm, hyssop, oregano, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, marjoram and similar herbs, combine them all, or some of them, in a pot, and soak them with vinegar, and boil; and the pot should be tightly sealed with proper clay, and a hole should be made in the middle of the pot, over which an appropriate concave brass instrument is placed, made of silver or brass.
And the end of the instrument should be inserted into the patient's mouth, so the vapor rises to the uvula, through the tube, until the uvula is considerably dulled.
And repeat this several times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, for it would only worsen the abscess, and it is not appropriate to do so, unless advised by a qualified physician concerning the nature of the abscess.
However, if this instrument is not available, then take a reed and place a hard-boiled egg shell on its end, so the patient's mouth is protected, because the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the removal of thorns and things that adhere to the throat, and of other related issues.
Many times, a thorn or fishbone or some other object adheres to the throat.
Then it is necessary that you remove whatever of those is apparent, upon which you fix your gaze, pushing the tongue with an instrument in front of a light, so that what is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which is hidden and unseen in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food has digested in the stomach, for a cause closely attached to the throat will be forcibly expelled by vomiting; or have the patient swallow a piece of bread, or lettuce root, or a piece of dry bread; or have the patient take a piece of dried sea sponge, tightly bound with wool, and then the patient swallows it, because when it reaches the spot where the thorns are, it is expelled. Or take an eel and drink it, tied with wool, and it is placed inside, and quickly removed; or take a leek and quickly place it and quickly remove it.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
And it should be larger than a fingernail, and at its end is a cavity which you can easily insert into the patient's throat.
And have the patient raise their head, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that it does not cause them pain, and with that, you push the bone or thorn, or the doctor pushes it with their hand.
But the patient himself should do it, if possible, because of his knowledge of the place of the cause and push it down, and pull his hand with the instrument up, all according to the amount that is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of bloodsucker attached to the throat.
When the leech is treated with what we have described in the division of treatment with medicine and you don't get a response, then consider placing the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed the patient's tongue with the instrument we have described.
Therefore, if you see the leech, remove it with a small knife or with skillful, gentle gestures.
And if you cannot remove it with that, then take a concave tube and insert it into the patient's throat.  Then place a heated iron into the concavity of the tube, and do this repeatedly (until the leech is dead and comes loose from the throat).  And keep the patient away from water all day.
And then take a bowl full of cold water, open the patient's mouth into it, and rinse the mouth with it.  And let nothing be swallowed, and hold the water over the mouth for another hour with your hand, because as soon as the leech feels the water, it will fall off.
But if it is not dislodged by what we have described, then fumigate the patient with frankincense or asafoetida, using the instrument we described for fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall off.
And another method of fumigation for it is that you take a bowl containing burning coals, and the bowl is covered with a lid that has a hole in it, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole.  Then perform the fumigation above the patient.  Have the patient put their mouth on the end of the tube, and they should close their mouth to prevent the fumigation from escaping until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, because the leech will fall off soon.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation several times, and prevent the patient from drinking, and let them eat salty things and other foods, and do not let them drink water; and thus it is necessary that through this treatment it will be dislodged.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is treated when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, but it has some curvature in one part. which is placed on the leech, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is smooth, on which a file is used so that, when grasping something, it does not slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discussion on abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are many types, of many kinds, according to their recollection, each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is according to the type of abscess itself; and by what we acquire from the humors.
And the second way is according to the location on the body where it occurs, for an abscess in the head is different from an abscess in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy area is different from an abscess in a joint; and each of them has its judgment regarding treatment.
And further, some abscesses should not be pierced until the pus has matured in them or has reached fullness.
Other abscesses should be lanced when they are inflamed and not yet fully matured, such as abscesses near joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its development extends until what is in its vicinity is corrupted, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are severely damaged, thus causing a contraction of that limb.  Or if the abscess is near a major limb, if you delay lancing it until it matures, it may harm the main limb.  Or if it's near the anus, then lance it while unripe.  Do not lance an unripe one near the anus because of incomplete digestion, lest you endanger the anal passage and cause the abscess to extend to the interior regions of the anus, leading to a fistula, or to a condition that will not heal. However, you must know the time to lance the abscess, and that is when it is mature.
And you know it is mature when the pain subsides and the fever ceases, and with the decrease in inflammation and throbbing, and the sharpness of the abscess head, and the other signs.
And therefore, you must make the incision in the most suppurative area beneath the abscess and from the discharge coming from the prominence.
And the incision should always be along the flesh or the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and away from the tendons, nerves, and arteries; and, in short, in all similar places where there is no reflection; for in those that are reflective, use incision according to that specific limb.
However, when the abscess is in a fleshy area, it is better to leave the incision until the digestion of it is confirmed by improvement, as we have said; for if you incise it before this, you prolong the course of its pus and much filth, and make the edges of it hard and deep.
However, some abscesses are incised according to the width of the flesh or body, as necessity dictates, or according to the needs of the affected limb.
And it is necessary to make a small incision in small abscesses, and a large and wide incision, or several incisions, in large abscesses, according to the difference in size of the abscesses.
And others are of abscesses in which the pus must be hollowed out and cut, when done so that it can be fully drained, like those that occur in boils and buboes.
And some are incised with a three-angled cut; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like abscesses in the groin.
And some of them are treated with a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision, and similar ones.
But in those cases where there is no such need, like superficial lion-like abscesses, you should only make a superficial, or flat, incision.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and not try to remove all the pus at once.  Having removed some, then close the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and repeat this many times according to stages until it is fully drained.
And especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or a decrepit old person, for the animal spirit is often weakened by the discharge of pus, and the patient is easily killed, and you must be careful with the treatment.
Observe this chapter with great caution, and when making an incision to drain these abscesses, you must cleanse the wounds and then consider if the abscess wound is small, or a simple flat incision, then apply a bandage of linen or old cloth.  If the abscess is large and the incision has many openings, you must place a medicament in each opening until they come together.
And if a portion of the skin has been incised or peeled away around the abscess, then you must fill it with old cloth or linen strips, like when you would dress a wound from a loaf or root, without moisture; and hold it in place for up to three days. Then, continue to treat and heal it with suitable ointments until it is completely healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar in which you have soaked a linen cloth, and place this over the bleeding area repeatedly.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer a regimen and treatment, and use the powders that are described in many places in this book and in different sections.
However, if it is wintertime and the abscess is located in a region with many nerves, you must mix powders with warm wine and oil, and apply them to the affected area.
And if it is summer, and the affected areas are fleshy, then you must apply powders mixed with cool water and oil, or with wine and oil, and keep it cool until the third day, as we have stated.  Then, you must lance the abscess, clean it, and provide the appropriate treatment until healed.
And this is what is necessary to know about the treatment of abscesses in general; however, according to the method of distinction that I have already described, each type of abscess has a different method of treatment, which will be described separately in chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the lancing of abscesses that appear on the scalp.
Small abscesses form on the scalp, often as lumps (nodules) containing membranes that surround them; and these lumps contain pus.
And they contain pus, and that pus contains moisture similar to lime. And some contain moisture similar to dust, and others are like that; and some are stony, hard.
And in lancing them all, and in their removal, there is no fear, as their removal does not cause counteracting reactions.
And the procedure for lancing them is that you probe them with an instrument shaped somewhat like this, which is called a lancet, until you know what they contain.
If what it contains is moisture, then you divide that with a lancing motion along the length, by a superficial incision in this manner:
And begin the incision from the line of B to the line of G.  Then, as the moisture is evacuated, you scrape away the skin that contained the moisture, and do not leave any remnant, for it often returns if any remains.
Then soak cotton in Egyptian unguent, if present, or else in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, for by this you corrode any remaining skin film.
And then repeat applying the Egyptian unguent to the cotton a second and third time, if needed, until you are certain that no part of the skin film remains; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you should cut it in this manner: with an incision in the shape of a cross, and insert probes between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from all sides, and exert your strength in removing the membrane that contains it.
But if your efforts encounter resistance, then do what I told you first.
And similarly, do the incision and treatment of the calcified abscess as we have told you; and an incision on a calcified abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses that appear on the head of some men, which are not entirely moist; this is why I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if one were to strike it, it would hurt.

Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing more than a collection of moisture in the white sac, which is below the pubic bone containing the testicles, and is called the sac.

And this moisture has various colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or black, or whitish, watery, and that is what is most common.
And the signs by which one knows where this accumulation of water is are these: if it is in the white sac we mentioned, then the abscess is round, slightly elongated, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle appears to be partially covered by the moisture on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper covering, then the abscess is round, with a part of the testicle, and for this reason one thinks it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the scrotum and the white sac, then it is felt below the testicle.

Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture in the stretched diaphragm above the abdomen, on the side of the abdominal muscles, causing the intestines from that rupture to adhere to some of the intestines.
And this rupture is either of the diaphragm or of its extension, and these two types occur due to various causes: either from a blow, or from a fall, or from shouting, or from lifting something heavy, or from similar actions.
And the sign of this, when it's due to a stretching of the diaphragm, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly; and it is a gradual swelling towards the navel because the diaphragm is pressing on the intestine.
And the sign of it when it's due to a rupture of the diaphragm is that initially, it causes sudden and intense pain; and there is a different type of swelling appearing under the ribs close to the surface, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body being outside the diaphragm.
And sometimes the intestines are expelled along with the tissues, and this is called an intestinal hernia, or a "zirbal" hernia.
And sometimes it's caused by gas, and sometimes a tear develops in the intestines, and it's retained there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illnesses with iron, there is danger, so it is necessary to avoid using it in that situation.
And the method of surgery for this is to tell the patient to push the intestine back into its cavity with their hand if it returns, and then carefully place the patient on their back, holding their hands, and elevate their legs.
Then, carefully dissect the sheath that holds the testicles, opening it from above, and cut the sheath of the testicles all along its length; then, with a pair of forceps, separate the two edges of the incision according to how you need to open the incision with them.

Then, insert your finger into the area surrounding the testicle, between the white tunic that lies beneath the testicle's sheath, and the surrounding tissue, and separate any adhesions located behind the testicle.
Then, pull the sheath of the testicles outwards with your hand, and, using your left hand, pull the white tunic upwards, raising the testicle with the tunic towards the incision; and instruct the assistant to hold the testicle up while you break down any adhesions behind it with a suitable solution.
And use your fingers to check that there is no intestinal tissue entangled in the hard white tunic. But if you find any such tissue, then you push it back into the abdomen.
And then cut into the area below the testicle sheath, so that blood and pus flow out, as we have described in the previous section.
Then apply wool soaked in oil to the wound; and apply the bandage we described (and sometimes the white tunic is cauterized, which we said is applied near the incision to prevent blood flow, because often this happens) and leave the bandage until it falls off by itself.
But if the inflammation is delayed, then hot water should be applied, and kept on it until it falls off as we have said.
And then treat the wound with other wound remedies until it is healed.
