And then take a stick and place it on the prominent area of the groin, and press it against the belly button.
And then stitch the two prominent places on the end of the groin stick together, and join one to the other with stitches.
And then remove the stick, and do not cut the groin completely, nor extend the testicle, nor any other such thing, as I teach you in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and as you would cleanse the sores, cleanse and restore the wound until it is healed; for the groin is constricted and the rupture eminence is not mended.
And cautery is better in this location, for it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
On softening the scrotum (or testicles).
Often the scrotum in some men softens because the flesh between the testicles softens, and its appearance is suspect.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one attending to the cure to have the patient lie down according to their side, and to separate the entire softened tissue from the natural scrotum; and then to join the two flaps together with sutures.
And if you wish, stitch the excess softened scrotum tissue first with three or four firm sutures.
Then, make incisions between the sutures; and then treat with healing salves until the wound heals and the pus subsides.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our law, is forbidden, but by reason, it is permitted to me to remember it in this book; and this remembrance is not necessary except in two ways.  One is so that the physician, when questioned about it, may know how to treat the afflicted; the other is that we often need to castrate other animals for our own benefit, such as oxen, goats, pigs, and similar beasts.
Therefore, I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by slitting and cutting.
But in that which is done by crushing, the method of the operation is that we should immerse the animal in hot water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. And then grasp them with your hand until they are broken and no longer appear beneath the touch.
However, in castration by slitting and cutting, you must hold the animal and press the scrotum with your left hand.
And then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut into each testicle with a cut until both testicles are separated.
And then cut those further, once each is skinned. And do not leave any cause of the scrotum's outer layer, but only the delicate lining that covers the vessels.
And this method of castration, by rubbing, is better, for strong rubbing leaves no cause of the testicles that makes the animal desire mating.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when a space appears in the scrotum, between the testicles, resembling a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs.
And sometimes urine flows from the area between the testicles.
But in women, it's a single type: the woman's vulva over the pubic bone is like two male testicles, slightly reduced in size, positioned externally, like the male foreskin, and two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: for two of the types in men and one type in women. And it is necessary to cut those adhering tissues until the pressure is released, and then treat those wounds with the treatment for other sores.
And in the second type in men, where urine is in the area between the testicles, there is no cure nor surgery for it.
Chapter 21.
Of lens-like swelling and protruding flesh due to (afflictions of) women.
A mild swelling is often caused by natural causes until it is compressed and becomes less noticeable; and sometimes it becomes so large in some women that it spreads just like in men and interferes with sexual intercourse.
Then you should remove the excess swelling with your hand or with a lancet.
And do not cut deeply, especially at the base of the root, so that you do not cause excessive bleeding.
And then treat it with the treatment for sores until it is healed.
But flesh that is born within the womb of the mother until it fills it, and easily emerges from the parts outside resembling a tail (and therefore some early writers called this "tail disease"); thus, we must cut it as we cut swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of [a disease called] alratica.
Alratica is when the opening of the woman's vagina is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is due to an illness that has preceded it, and that illness is caused either by improperly aligned flesh or by a subtle or thick membrane.
And it happens either in the mother's groin, or in her ribs, or in the highest part of her womb, or in the lowest part, and you prohibit coition, and impregnation, and childbirth, and strongly prohibit menstruation.
And the knowledge of that is obtained through observation of the midwife, if the blockage is apparent, treat it quickly; but if it is not apparent, then you seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the blockage is due to a thin membrane, and is close to the two lips, then you cut and break that.
And you should place powders on the two lips, and then place your index fingers of both hands together on top.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully extend the two lips until that thin membrane is broken and the blockage is open.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it over the area, and let the patient rest with a man by their side that day so that the incised area doesn't heal.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you must cut it with a sharp blade or a myrtle leaf.
But if the blockage is due to a clot, then suspend that with forceps, and cut it.
And have with you the remedies that stop blood flow without causing pain, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and fennel, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it for a few days and apply poultices of dry herbs.
And then it should be treated with another healing method until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the major birth of other flesh, and then it is necessary to cut according to this method. However, if this is due to a cranium abscess, no iron should be used to treat a cranium abscess in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids and of the cut and red inflammation that occurs in the vagina of women.
Hemorrhoids are the enlargement of the openings of many veins through which much blood continually flows; and when hemorrhoids are long-standing and fistulas are formed.
And I have already stated in the division the species of those [hemorrhoids] and their signs, and will now say which ones receive treatment and which ones do not.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and fistulas, when they are deep within the rectum and do not appear on the surface,  then no treatment with iron should be done on them.
But those which are at the opening of the rectum, where one can feel them, are those which can be cured.
And you must place the woman in a cool place and then carefully cut away the fistula with scissors, or with a harsh cloth, and cut them away with the roots.
Then, sprinkle over them the flow of blood from one of the cutting powders without biting, like acacia and similar things.
And then bathe their feet for an hour, and afterwards place them in cool water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then have them sit in a decoction of sumat, and of quince bark, and of berries, and of similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine, and make a poultice with this on their back.
And if the blood has stopped, good; and if not, apply two leeches, and to the breasts without making incisions.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice, or juice of arnagloza, or juice of rue, mixed with diluted wine, and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of a bone fracture in the arm.
Many types of abscesses occur in the arm, such as cancer, stone-like abscesses, ulcers, swellings, eruptions, fistulas, hemorrhoids, sores, and other types of abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses and their types, their symptoms, and their treatments in the division.
And it is necessary for me to discuss in this treatise a particular abscess that comes to the arm, when it is one of the abscesses that collects pus, and if you wish to know how the operation on it is performed with an iron.
Therefore, you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is sharp with percussion, phlegm, and fever, and if it shows redness and if, when pressed upon it, you feel the inflammation, then do not treat it with perforation, but cure it with what aids maturation until the sharpness of the condition subsides.
And then you should have the woman lie on a stretcher with two supports; and lean her on her side and position her feet near the lower part of her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread open, and place her arms under her armpits, and tie each of them with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife, standing on the right side, administers the instrument with which the opening in the arm is made, the form of which will be explained in the subsequent discussions.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you should insert its point into the arm of the woman, and the part of the instrument that enters the arm should only reach the arm's point, and ease the patient's suffering. But if the instrument is larger than the arm's point, then you should place powders on the surface of the arm so that the instrument is prevented from entering the arm.
And you should place the liquid that runs through the instrument on the topmost part, and have the attendant hold the instrument and rotate the liquid until the arm's opening is made.
And when the discharge appears, and it is touched by the hand and is slimy and moist, then you should dry that area around the head with a linen cloth.
And when the pus is evacuated, then you should place a medicated cloth soaked in rosemary or green oil (in which there is some astringent), and put the cloth on the affected area on the arm.
And place outside the arm, and over the forearm, a clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And it should be treated for approximately two days with the ointment appropriate for this purpose until it is healed.
And sometimes the arm and the wound are washed in such a way that they are cleansed with water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or long-stemmed iris has been boiled, and then return to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep within the arm, then you should be prohibited from treating it with iron, following the treatment described in the division.
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living embryos when their development is not natural.
It is necessary for the midwife to know the natural pattern of childbirth from its signs.  When you see the woman pushing from below, desiring to breathe air, and is relieved from the pressure on her from the delivery, and hastens the delivery of the embryo, know that this birth is natural, and the delivery is of the embryo over its head, and the afterbirth attached or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then the woman's abdomen should be compressed so that the embryo descends easily, for when it descends over its head, it leaves the afterbirth, which is cleansed of those superfluities with proper cleansing.
But if the birth is different, it is unnatural and undesirable.  For when the embryo emerges feet first, and the body comes out before the head and feet, or one hand or foot protrudes, or the head is delivered and a hand is delivered, or a foot is delivered and a hand is delivered, or if the body is delivered with the head, or if it turns sharply around its neck – these are signs of unnatural, undesirable births.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and skillful in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these things, and the ingenious methods in them, so that it is signified by this and what has happened.
When the fetus emerges over its head through natural expulsion, and the labor is difficult and the woman's strength is weakened, then apply this treatment: or if the women have it, and evaporate the conh with collected fenugreek and moist oils.
And the midwife should insert her fingers into the small space and fissure, and with that remove the afterbirth, or cut it with her nail until she removes the moisture from it. And press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
But if it doesn't descend, then it is necessary for the woman to be treated with poultices of ptilli and poultices of fenugreek with cumin.
And then order the patient, apply the poultice, that it be pressed and make the patient sneeze with force; and cover the patient's mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will be expelled soon.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then it is necessary that you return them gently, little by little. But if you don't succeed in bringing it back inside, then place the woman on a stool and raise her feet up, and then push the stool down to the ground; and hold the woman so she does not fall during the pushing.
And if the hands are not returned and the fetus dies, then cut them and remove the remainder of the fetus, or tie its body to the woman's hand with cloth and pull it out carefully, for that will be expelled.
When the fetus is on its feet, then you must push them upward, and then turn the fetus gently, and then swallow it, and then take one of its feet and extend it slowly.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you tell her to strain, and make her cough with force, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recounted, then return the fetus slowly until you place it in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with a gentle exit.
And if it is still not expelled by all of that, then take marshmallow and dissolved gum, grind them thoroughly in a mortar, and then apply this mixture to the woman's vulva, the lower part of her abdomen. And then apply a compress in lukewarm water until the hypochondria (the sides of the chest) are relieved.
And when the parts below her are soothed, then apply a fumigation of myrtle and place it under her. And when the fumigation has been applied for an hour, then make her sit on a stool, and make her sneeze and tightly close her mouth, and remove that, and press the lower part of her abdomen gently with a compress, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
The fetus emerges lengthwise, using one of her hands. Be skillful in reducing (returning) the fetus with your hand, but if you cannot reduce it, then lift the woman and make her walk.
And if walking is not possible, then have her lie down on the bed, and forcefully compress her thighs, and then administer remedies which I have previously recommended (or listed)  made from mucilages.
And if labor has begun, then have her sit on a stool, and then carefully attempt to reduce the fetus with your hand, and gently coax the fetus into the natural position, and make the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it is expelled.
The fetus emerges onto the mother's lap, with its arms outstretched and its face turned towards her. The midwife should hold the baby's hand and gently and progressively turn it, helping it along.
And when it is properly positioned, then apply what we have recommended of oil and mucilage to its head.
Then instruct her to gently push it out, and when you have done that, have her sit on a stool and instruct her to lean to her right side, and then instruct her to bear down; and make her sneeze with (appropriate) encouragement. The prepared fetus is delivered onto the side.
When the mother is delivered of the horrifying thing (fetus) in this manner, consider it, and then you cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
And if the fetus is hanging from the face and neck, then you cut it from the umbilical cord, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into the appropriate parts until you place it as is proper, and instruct the woman to bear down a few times, and it will be expelled gently, if God wills it.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare and difficult to explain; but these are things outside of nature, and not alive.
And count them, whether they are multiplied, a second one, and separate them by the cord, distinguishing them at the umbilicus of each.  And sometimes they are expelled as if a single fetus, by gentle natural means, and sometimes by unpleasant means, as we have recounted.
And if some of them are difficult to deliver and come to what we have said about unnatural formations, then treat them as we have described.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is ingenious in accordance with every form suitable to the person's preparation for healing.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  It has been verified by me that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all formed, the cause of which was determined by the learned men's measurements.
And all this should be within the knowledge of the midwife, for it is easily recognized by such small signs, and therefore she should treat it accordingly.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus using what we have discussed in the division of medicine, and it has not emerged naturally, and you are forced to use manual procedures, then you must consider whether the woman is of healthy constitution and is not suffering from any of the conditions that cause fear of her loss.
Then the woman should be positioned on her back on the bed, with her head tilted downwards and her thighs elevated.
And the attendant should hold her in those two places, or she should be tied to the bed, so that her body does not move or stretch away from the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mother's mouth with moist oils and with mucilage of marsh-mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where she can hook her fingernails onto the fetus.
And suitable places for this:  Then consider if the fetus is ready to descend headfirst, and then you should hook your fingernails into its scalp, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under its chin, or in its armpits, or in the appropriate spots on the ribs, or under the lower ribs.
However, if the descent is feet first, then in the perineum, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the fingers with the right hand, and place the curve of the finger in the area between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and hook the finger into any of the places we mentioned, until reaching the empty cavity (or opening).
And then place and hook another finger, or a third if necessary, in close proximity to the first, so that it is a pulling action, and is not just an extension of the previous one.
And move it in all directions, so that its extraction is eased, just as a tooth is extracted.
And it is necessary in that area between them that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary for the midwife to grease some of her fingers with oil and insert those fingers and twist the retained part.
Therefore, if the part of the object is forced out, then the fingers should be moved to a higher location; and do this until the entire object is expelled.
But if the hand has slipped out before the other, and it is not possible to return it by retracting it, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until it is completely expelled, and cut it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus it is necessary that you do the same for the other hand and both feet.
But if the head of the object is large, or if constriction is applied to its protrusion, or if water is accumulated on its head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the spines, split the head, and remove the water, or to break it with an instrument called "alundat," the shape of which will be found with the shapes of other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
And similarly do so if the object has a large head by nature: and then it is necessary to cut it with a knife, or to remove it with an alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head has protruded and is constricted towards the fork, then it should be split until the moisture that is in the feet is drained away.
But if it is not complicated, nor obstructing a protrusion, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the abdomen is abscess-ridden or hydrops-affected, then it is necessary for you to puncture it until the moisture within it is drained.
But if the object is on its feet, then the pull on it is gentle and its direction towards the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is constricted towards the belly and feet, then you must remove it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and cut the belly and feet until what is inside is released.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head remains, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she inserts her hand into the air space, searches for the head with it, and pulls it with her fingers towards the mother's mouth. Then she inserts a finger, or two fingers, and pulls it with them.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted by a boil or ulcer that arises inside, then it should not be forced; instead, it is necessary to administer a poultice of greasy, moist substances. And apply plasters; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, if possible, it should be turned; and administer what we have said about the living fetus.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and removed.
And it is necessary to not leave any cause within the afterbirth.
And when you have completed all your operation, then administer what we have said about the treatment of the boils which occur in the mother.
And if a flow of blood occurs, then apply styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman whose belly contained a dead fetus, and soon after, became pregnant again, and another dead fetus resulted. A long time ago, an abscess appeared near his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and medicated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied some strong-drawing plaster to him, and a bone came out of him, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, as there are no bones inside the belly.
And then I thought that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I treated the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in her usual condition, and she lived in this way for a long time, getting better little by little.
And I did not consider this strange, but rather that in this lies knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician administers with his hands for healing.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary for extracting the fetus.
Form of twisting with which the womb is opened:
This is the form of tightening in which the books are bound, to which binding are two twists at the two ends of two cords. However, this twist should be more subtle than a constricting one, and should be made either of a ribbon or a wooden stick.
And the width of each stick should be about two fingers, and the margin of each about one finger, and the length of each is one and a half palms; and in the middle of the two sticks are two attachments of that same stick, which are already fastened, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the womb so that it is opened with those when you revolve the two prescribed twists.
Another similar form, more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of wood or ivory, similar in shape to forceps. However, at its extremity, there are two additions, just as before, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you wish to open the womb with these, then you have the woman lie on her back, her feet hanging, and open the space between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument, as with forceps, likewise, according to the amount you want to widen the opening of the womb, until the midwife does what you want.
The form of dizziness, which the ancients said:
Form of impulse by which the fetus is propelled:
Form of friendship/affection/love with which the large head is contained.
And it resembles forceps, which have teeth at the extremity, as sometimes happens, and sometimes it is made long, like forceps of this shape, as sometimes happens, with teeth like saw teeth, with which it is incised and contained. Form of slight affection/love/friendship:
Form of a hook having a spine/point/projection:
Form of a hook having two spines/points/projections:
Form of a spatula with two ends, with which the head is severed/cut off.
Form of two wide spatulas.
All those instruments are used, and the more the varieties of them are multiplied and prepared by the craftsmen, the lighter their operation is and the more esteemed they are by men due to their quantity.
And you should have those instruments ready, wherever you are, for the need for them is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should command the woman to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with a remedy, and press your hand over her mouth and nostrils.  If by this it is expelled, it is good.
And if not, then you take a bowl and perforate a hole in its cover, and place inside it herbs, opening the mother's mouth, such as mint, rue, fennel, chamomile, Arabic gum, cinnamon wood, and St. John's wort. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the bowl, and place that bowl over the fire.
Then place a reed cannula into the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the cavity of the mother.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if, despite this treatment, it remains lodged and does not come out, then you command the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or in a mixture of marshmallow, and then introduce it into the vagina and use it to seek out the afterbirth.  And once she has found it, then she should pull it out gradually. But if it is attached to the womb, then she should introduce her hand as we have described, and when she has found the afterbirth, she should remove it slowly and cautiously so that the mother does not suffer from undue traction. Beforehand, it is necessary that it be moved from side to side with ease.
And if the mother's cervix is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and with herbal poultices.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then be careful not to strain it further by pulling, but rather it is necessary that whatever has come out of her vagina lies on the woman's thigh, and then anoint it with ointment and a four-drug compound.  For that will cause it to decompose after a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it decomposes, there is a foul odor rising from the head and stomach, which weakens the patient; and then fumigations and appropriate remedies must be administered.
But some of the ancients have already tested fumigations with narcissus bulbs and dried figs.
Form of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for retained menses and afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or earthenware.
And place the thin end in the container and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed above the fire's flames.
And keep it until the fumigation is consumed and then repeat another one.
Treatment of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which are already tight and obstructed. Then the midwife should perforate that obstruction with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the sphincter.
And then place some wool soaked in wine and oil over it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear it is obstructed, then insert a cannula into the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wishes to swallow.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a plague or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then treated with what we have said about the cure and lead.
Of the treatment of fistulas that develop afterwards.
The fistulas that develop in the lower area, swollen and large, which develop near the anus externally or in the space of the lower area.
And it is a fistula or multiple ones.
And when that swelling is old and originates from watery white moisture or a subtle putrefaction, and sometimes these fistulas are even penetrating.
And those that are penetrating are known because of what has exited from them through excretion and by flatulence; and sometimes the patient experiences excretion, and often worms emerge from them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder, and into the urethra; and some of these penetrate into the hip joint and into the knee joint.
And how one recognizes a penetrating fistula in the anus, as opposed to a non-penetrating one, is that you insert your index finger into the anus, and gently probe for a narrow opening or metal rod within the fistula, if there is no twisting or distortion.
But if there is twisting in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair against your finger.
And if you do not feel any of this at all, nor does any discharge of matter, worms, or flatulence proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know it to be non-penetrating, and then dedicate yourself to the cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the urethra, then the sign of it is frequent urination and preventing it until the area is healed with medicine.
However, if it penetrates into the joint of the thigh or bone and causes pain in the surrounding area, then the sign of it is that the probe passes through until it reaches the bone, if there is no twist inside the bone and pain in the surrounding area; and that it will likely always be sore; and that treatment for it is ineffective, nor is it healed with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no complete cure, and the cure for them is the effort and vanity of learned physicians, on which it depends.
However, in those that are not penetrating and are not old, healing with iron is expected, as I have told you, and this I have already tested.
And it is necessary that the patient lies between your hands on his back, and elevate his legs upward, and the thighs should be inclined towards his stomach, and then insert a lead probe or, if there is a twist in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe passes through.
And if that sensation is around the anus, then you should insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe penetrating by itself on your finger, or if you feel something between your finger and that tissue or flesh, then know for sure that it is penetrating; and therefore do not work on it, for there is no cure in it, as we have said.
But nevertheless, some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And the treatment by which improvement is expected in him is that you heat a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously described, and insert that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all those fleshy, fistulous areas resembling a bird's feather shaft have been cauterized.
And then treat it with some bandages until all those cauterized tissues have healed, and then treat it with medicines that generate and strengthen the flesh, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate to your fingertip in the anus, and between it and the probe there is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula's opening is on the surface of the anal canal, then cut the anal canal from the beginning of the fistula, and cut with the probe, and let it remain in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the tip of the probe arrives, and by the probe it is released and extracted.
And then clean those fistulous tissues, which resemble the shaft of a bird's feather, and leave no cause for concern.
But if bleeding occurs between you and your procedure when cauterizing those tissues, then the best and most straightforward instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire has two good effects: it is the cauterization of those tissues and the excision of blood, removing the moisture.
And then treat it with bandages or a finely woven cloth until the area heals, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and strengthen strong flesh.
And those medicines that include astringency and excision; when it is thus restored, it is already healed and you need not fear a recurrence.
However, if the fistula extends to the anus and is distant from the body's surface, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
And thus, if you feel a resistance, and between you and it there is a covering of tissue or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no surgery to be done there, but rather only a route of trust and hope.
Which condition can be addressed in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or excise it until you reach its cavity, so you can insert medicaments and promote healing; and do not perform much excision, lest you injure the surrounding tissue containing the gut, and cause harm to the patient without their consent; and then treat it with what we have said, for it will be easily healed; or, separate the membrane and drain the pus that you excise close to the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or another instrument with a sharp end, and then clean the fistulous flesh that you have excised, to where you can use cauterization; and then endeavor to restore the entire wound with excision, and if the opening above is not healed, and the fistula remains open in the anal region, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is completed in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the fold of the anus near the body's surface, with the tissue and at the edge of the anus, then take a probe with a pointed end, which is like a needle for preparing the skin in this form:
and insert into it a fine thread wrapped with five threads, or around them; then insert the thread with the probe into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it is penetrating at the margin of the anus close to the inside, then you pull the thread from that opening, as if you insert your finger into the anus; and extract the thread's end and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you further cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh is cut between both ends of the thread and falls away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and sound.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in any way you are prepared, but not too far into the perineum, for it is not for you to do this so that you do not cut the ligament; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the spatula with which the fistula is opened: the curve of which is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as sometimes happens.
Of the suturing of hemorrhoids in which blood flows in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for either they are like lumps of coal on the anus, or they are like grapes. And they are small or large, and the blood flows from them continuously, and are one or many, and are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are according to the measures of little moisture, from which flows yellow water or a little blood with a continuous flow, and this is according to the color of the flow.
And the treatment of those inside the anus is to tell the patient to sit and strain until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grasp them and suspend them with tweezers or hold them with your fingernail, and then cut them off at their roots.
However, if the tweezers are not holding them due to their moisture and softening, then grasp them with a rough cloth or cotton or a wool pad, and pull them out with your fingers, and then cut them off and sprinkle some hot powders on their cuts, so that they are in the place of cautery; or cauterize them as is detailed in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not open, then prick the patient with a prickle in which there is a small bite, and wash it with what is in the anus, and that it opens slightly when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause of them is mild, and you should grasp them with your fingernail, or with tweezers, and cut them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And for the incision you have made, you should apply sutures to them like this: take a thread wrapped around it, and insert it into a needle. Then pull the hemorrhoids apart.  Push the needle through the base of the other side. Wrap the two ends of the thread in a horizontal line, just below the needle. Tie the hemorrhoid tightly with the thread, and then remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one, and do not sew it, so that the excess blood flows from that one.
And then place a cloth soaked in warmed oil or ointment on the anus, and put it on the patient's rear and instruct the patient to rest. And then leave it until it drops out; and when it has fallen out, treat it with ointments or other remedies until it is healed.
And if they are old and do not respond to that treatment, then you should scrape them with the tip of a spatula or your fingernail until they are moist and the pus-covered skin above is removed, which prevents their healing; and then treat them until they are healed.
Then remove them with a more forceful scraping than the first until a lot of blood comes and they are scraped clean; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that fistula, this is the key, and it is not anything but a round swelling the color of the body, like the head of a nail, and it appears throughout the body, but especially below the feet and in the fingers, and due to those pains, one should investigate the swelling.
Then it is necessary that what is around the swelling be incised, and held with forceps or tweezers, and the root of the swelling be removed; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is described in its chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round elevation on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to spread and incise it, or to squeeze it with a file or a needle until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
But a pimple is a small, ulcerated or fistulous, thick elevation on the body's surface, often appearing on the genitals.  When the cold touches it, one feels a sensation similar to an ant bite, and it is spread around the body.
And its treatment is to take a hollow tube, made of a bird's feather or a vulture's feather, and it should be strong. Place it over the pimple until the tube encircles the affected area. Then, rotate your hand with the tube to cut through the pimple's surrounding tissue, and press it into the flesh to eradicate the pimple from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube made of bronze or iron, following this method:
Let the part above the tube be thin, solid (that is, not perforated), and wrapped so that the grip on it is firm and its rotation is smooth.
And if you wish, cauterize the pimple as described previously in the section on cauterizing boils in the chapter on cauterization.
Concerning the forms of instruments used for providing care.
Sometimes care is provided with silver or brass or bronze, cast or hammered; and sometimes small or large instruments are used, depending on the patient's needs.  The instruments used for treating small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are sore or painful, the care instruments should be small as well.
The form of large and small care instruments:
The head of the cannula, upon which the bag is tied, should be wide in this manner; and there should be a clear distinction where the bag is tied, as it is done often.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the anus, should be very firmly closed (that is, not open), tapering to a narrow point; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little larger on one side.
And the bag, in which the bladder medicines are placed, be it of animal bladder or sheepskin, should be one palm in depth.
And it should be pierced with many holes, as it is rolled; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of a finger.
And then insert into those holes strong, potent, raw bile, which should be ten parts, or roughly that amount; and fasten the bag with that.  When the bag is placed on it, tightly bind the top of the pouch between two distinct points with strong constriction. Then bind each hole with that binding.  However, the manner of binding it is that you fill the bag with medicine, that is to say, warmed to a degree commensurate with the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
And now the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs up and place a cloth or sheet underneath them; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil, or egg white, or fenugreek mucilage or similar; and then insert that into the anus with ease, and the attendant should then take the pouch and squeeze it with both hands forcefully, until all the contents are evacuated into the bowels.  Then remove the pouch and the patient lowers their legs to the side.
And if the medicine causes him to sweat, keep him in that condition for as long as possible; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is better for his recovery.
However, if you cauterize for illnesses of loose bowels and sweating, then you repeat the cautery until it is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or festering sores in the anus, then the cautery should be very fine and gentle, like the cauteries used for children.
Of the treatment of plagues.
I have already said in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with hand and with medicines according to their condition.
Therefore, I say that plagues are diverse depending on how they are created and on the location on which the plagues occur.
And the causes why plagues are often made are like the throwing of a stone, or cutting with a knife or sword, or striking with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to these from many causes.
But the plagues that are in the parts of the body are like the plague that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the stomach, or the liver, and similar to these in the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the recollection of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is caused by a thrown stone or similar, and only the skull is cracked, and it is a serious wound, and you fear the development of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should quickly bleed the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract from him blood according to the extent of his strength, unless some cause prevents bleeding, especially if the blood that has come out of the wound is little.
And the bleeding should be on the opposite side of the affected part; and the patient should be guarded against repletion, and against strong food and drinks.
But if any reason prevents the bleeding, then the diet should be reduced, and he should not approach meat or wine.
And if an abscess develops on the wound, apply a poultice made of oil and herbs or wine with astringents.
But if you are certain of a hot abscess, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and if the air has not changed the powder that I will shortly recount.
And if the air has already changed it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or is caused by a cut or similar injury, and its edges are not brought together by sutures, then bring them together with stitches, as I will explain about their suturing.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendon, and it's not a serious injury but rather a small detachment, then cut away that part and treat the wound with medicines, and restore the flesh in place of the tendon, and tell the patient to eat foods rich in fat: heads and feet of fowl and similar things, until it is healed.
And if you find putrefaction in the tendon and it is not adhering to the bone, then you cut away all the putrid material and discard it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood might flow, and it is not severed by medicine, then you look for the artery, and if you find it not severed, then you cut it with a scalpel, or tie it off; and if necessity dictates that what we have said is not sufficient, then you cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; thus you powder them with this powder, for it will solidify them before the air spoils them.
And the recipe for the powder is this: take one part olive oil, two parts dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched lime; all finely ground and sifted, and apply it to the wound, and firmly bind it until it adheres well to the wound and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it over with bandages and leave it, and do not remove it until it is healed, for the medicine is not effective until the wound is healed.
And sometimes it is sufficient with just a cautery, if you do not have  oils and dragon's blood at hand.
And you do this same treatment for large wounds when it is necessary to apply it to them and bind them, especially fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not yet altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially in summer, then this powder application is not appropriate.  Instead, treat them with the remedies mentioned earlier.  Or, apply digestive ointments to them, or a poultice of barley flour mixed with water and honey until it hardens; then follow up with the other treatments until the wounds heal.
But if you encounter a wound with a fractured bone, and it's small, then you extract it with skill.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of wounds to the neck.
But if the wound occurs on the neck, there is no difference in treatment from a simple head wound.
But if the nerve or artery of the neck is cut, if it's a nerve, then there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with anything that causes intense astringency, such as zinc or vitriol, or similar things that damage and constrict the nerves. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold and their continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if this is treated with mild medications, such as washing with fresh water, sometimes infused into oil, whether rubbed or not, and a poultice when washed with fresh water and other minerals according to this method, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or close its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or extends to the lowest part of the bone, then perforate it in the most depressed place.
But if an artery is already cut in the wound and blood flow is occurring, then separate or cut off that artery, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity compels you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or the entire neck are already cut, and the veins of the neck are spared, then you must suture the edges of the wound along the spine of the neck and do not touch it; rather, align it and restore it to its natural form, and then tightly bind it with appropriate bandages, and leave it for several days; then treat it with ointments that contain astringents and stimulants until it is healed.
But if the wound has already reached the bone, you must clean it, and if there are bone fragments, remove all those you can; and whatever you cannot remove, leave it until the wound is suppurated, separated, and the pus is eased.
And if the wound is recent, then powder it with the powder that has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what the recollection will bring forth in its chapter.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if the wound produces windiness when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is deadly.
And if there is no great depth, and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you place on it, in the first hour, powder, and do not press it, so that the blood is not retained in its depths and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but apply a drawing ointment, and renew it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then you place old cloth on the wound opening, so that it draws out the humidity; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that whatever is accumulated there may drain out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound, and you do not see spasms or tremors in the patient's heart or constriction of the throat, nor other bad signs, then know that the wound is safe; and then cure it with poultices and other remedies until it is healed.
But if the healing is hindered, and it is always open, then know that a fistula has formed; thus treat it by its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or a sword, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with what is prescribed for sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if the wound has already penetrated the bone, and you have already cut out pieces from it, then you should squeeze the wound and try to remove those pieces, if they are detached. But if they are not detached, then leave them until the wound can close, for their removal is eased.
And for other wounds occurring in other limbs, the guidance in treatment is the same as what we have said.
Of wounds of the belly.
The ailment which occurs in the belly is either severe, or mild, or moderate.
And a minor rupture is more difficult to repair in another way, and it is seen that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not return the intrusion of them to their place promptly, for it will open up and become swollen, making the re-entry difficult.
Therefore, the best rupture is a moderate one, for in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two aforementioned types.
And know that the suture of the belly is according to four methods, but two sutures are common, which apply to sutures of other wounds in the body.
And two other specific sutures are applicable to other wounds, but nonetheless are more specific in the suture of the belly.
And I will explain each to you with exposition and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has protruded through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is so, then you should warm it in this way: either you immerse a damp sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or you infuse it in water with squinant, cyperus, and spic, and apply it to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, this works better in cases with astringency, and it's better than plain water in resolving the inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which althea and mallow have been cooked, for this eases its condition with little effort.
But if the condition of the intestine is not eased by this treatment, then carefully cut the rupture slightly with an instrument used for hemorrhoids.
And the first treatment is better, when it is properly applied without resorting to incision, and there should be no need to dry it excessively or cause further damage.
Of the instrument, take its shape (or form):
One part of it is sharp, the other part blunt, and the tip is fine, but not as fine as a needle; rather, it is slightly blunt.
And therefore, if the rupture is extensive and the intestine is protruding, then the reduction must be done in the natural way, returning it to its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and in the lower part of the abdomen, then you must have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then raise the head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then always focus your attention and consideration on ensuring that the part containing the rupture is higher than the other.
And thus you must administer this in large and medium ruptures.
And in small ruptures, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant stand between your hands, holding the rupture with one hand and gently bringing the edges together, and then examine for any other cause requiring further stitching.
And this is the common method of suturing: take a needle, or several needles depending on the size of the rupture; then place one at the edge of the rupture with the ring finger, and hold the needle in such a way that you do not put tension on the two ends of the thread, with the two ends of the suture material under the thread of the edges inwards, until you cause it to penetrate into that part. And now you have joined two ends of the thread and two ends of the suture material, which makes four stitches.
Then tighten with a double thread around the needle, sometimes from two sides simultaneously, and then bring the edges and the rupture together properly; then leave the thickness of a finger and insert another needle, and then tie it with thread just as you tied with the first needle; and do not stop doing this if necessary with the needles until you have completely closed the rupture.
And the needles should be medium in thickness between coarse and fine, for the fine needles pierce the flesh too lightly; and the thick ones are difficult to thread, therefore they should be medium in fineness and thickness.
And the needle should not be placed too close to the two ends of the thread, so that the flesh is not pierced too lightly and the rupture is opened before its healing.
And the sutures should not be prolonged, so that the rupture is not hindered from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not bother the patient during their rest; and you will place over the suture expanded powders on all sides, holding the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the rupture is already healed.
And this type of suturing with needles is most appropriate for small wounds, for in their suture only one or two, or so, needles are sufficient.
However, the second method of suturing is more common: and it is that you suture all four ends of the thread and two ends of the bandage, all at once, using a needle that is tightly wrapped in a delicate, yet substantial, way.
And then, after you have penetrated these four ends with the needle, withdraw the needle from the same point you started, so that the thread pulls back from the most profound part of the wound, so that the suture will be firm, like the way fabrics are strengthened by weaving.
And place between each suture the width of a little finger.
And this suture pleases the people (or is known/used by people).
And with this method of suturing, I healed a wound that had befallen a man in his abdomen, where he had been wounded with a knife.  The wound was a gap of more than a palm, and intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle of the wound, rupturing the middle of the internal organ.  I was able to return the intestines which had been outside the wound after 24 hours.
And the wound healed in about fifteen days, and I cured him. He recovered, and lived many years thereafter, and his condition improved.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not recover;  it was a miracle, for I did not treat him with ointments, for it was in a place where no ointments or medicines were found, but I applied warm wool to the wound twice a day, and continuously washed it with honey water until it healed.
But I will describe two kinds of sutures, proper according to the word of Paraula.
And it is that you begin with a suture using a needle, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And thus, as the needle pierces the thread and the same wound, following its length and the entire body, and leaves the tip of the thread where you inserted the needle; and push the needle from the inside to the outside of the other end of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make that penetration again in that same end of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is in this wound, and insert the needle from the inside to the outside of the other end.
And insert with that your penetration, with which you make that penetration into the thread at the wound's end that is on his side until all of that is penetrated.
And then, starting from this same side, stitch that with the end of the thread on the other side, and remove the needle from the thread you pierced, and then return that to that thread and stitch the end of the thread that is on the other side with this end of the wound; and remove that from the thread that is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this once more until you have sewn all that is outside according to this example.
And the sum of this method of suturing is that you sew with a method of skin stitching, with which you sew the skin, in such a way that you stitch the thread once from the end of the wound, and leave that again until you have completed it.
But the second kind of proper suture, which some call Gaulish suture, and this is told to you in detail, is that you stitch it according to a similar method as some physicians stitch it, so that they add each part to its corresponding part to him conveniently by nature; and they apply the end of another of him and the end of the wound to the other end of him, and this is done as I told you.
You must put the needle into the end of the wound near the external part, and make it penetrate to the internal part of that single wound, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside in the two ends of the thread.
And then turn those (threads), and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part in two other ends of the wound that are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common light suture, and it is a suture of which the memory precedes.
This is the Gaulish method.
And some might say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, which means that you insert a thread into each of them, and begin with the insertion of the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who know how to heal the skin equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle part of the belly, then the suture there is more difficult than in other parts of the belly.
But the judgment of plague treatment is the same as for other plagues.
Which means that when you capture the plague fresh with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestines and stitch it and fit it properly, then apply the flesh-healing powder over it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then apply some ointments that you have present until they form a hard stone and the pus falls out, and are consolidated, sound and healed; and then treat it as the treatment for other plagues until it is healed.
But if the remedies are not present, then apply immediately, once it begins to cast off, old cloth, and change this twice a day, as taught to you, until it is healed, for you do not need other treatment in many cases when plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague may spread to the vital members, then you should soak lion's wool in oil of good quality, or in rubbed oil, and place it around the areas between the armpits and under the arms.
And if there is pain or putrefaction in his intestines, as often happens, then cauterize it with black astringent wine, especially if the hardness has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating opening into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is easier to heal than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal, for the intestine called "jejunum" does not receive healing from plagues that strike it from all sides; and this is because of the vast multitude of veins in it, and by the magnitude of its volume, and the subtlety of its substance, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
But if the matter proceeding from the plague is the tissue, and you find it recent, reduce it according to your method used to return the intestines to their place.
But if it has spread to the surrounding tissues and is already green or black, then you should apply strong pressure with a poultice over the darkened area to bring blood flow, since the tissue contains veins and arteries.
And then cut the tissue around that ligament, and place the two ends of the poultice hanging down on the side of the plague, so that the pull of the matter from the diseased tissue and the emission of the plague's branch is alleviated.
A reminder of the plagues when they occur in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine, and it is small, it is possible in some cases to restore it.  For this reason, I have seen someone who had been afflicted in their belly by a lance wound, and the plague was on the right side of the stomach. The plague was old and had become a fistula, and there was discharge and gaseous emission.
Then I considered the cure, knowing it was not a sure cure, but I attempted to refine the treatment until the area was healed and the location solidified.
And after I saw the area healed, I told the patient that further injury to their abdomen was to be expected; and no further mishap occurred and they remained in their proper state, eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and was put to exercise according to their needs.
And some experienced persons have stated that when a small rupture occurs in the intestine, it should be bound in this manner:  Take large-headed leeches.  Then bring the two edges of the rupture together and place a leech with an open mouth on the edges of the rupture.  Thus, when it has attached itself and closed its mouth, sever the leech's head, as it will adhere and not dissolve.
Then place another leech near the first, and do not stop doing this with leeches, one after another, according to the size of the entire rupture.
And then you reduce the swelling and the rupture; and those leech heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and you do no harm to the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine be bound with a fine thread, which is made from the intestine of an animal, connected to it, since it is threaded onto a needle; and you should take the end of this intestinal thread, and make it very thin, and then tie the end of this thread with the thread of the ligature, enveloped, and then insert the thread into needles and close the intestine with it, and reduce this into the rupture.
This method of suturing with leeches is only by way of trust and hope.
But if the rupture is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, for then there is no cure but a small alleviation, and it is not complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of sores and fistulas.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes chronic, and turns into an ulcer, and is not healed, and discharges pus constantly and is not incised, then, in any limb, it is universally called a fistula, and we call that a sore.
And a fistula, according to truth, is a hard, white, fibrous swelling, without pain, and with a cavity like the cavity of a bird's nest; and therefore we call that ulcer a "rized" ulcer, that is, a nest.
And in some cases it is moist, discharging pus; and easily the moisture is incised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes it is thin, and sometimes it is large, and sometimes it is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of those, and the treatment of them with medicines, in the division.
And it is necessary that I make you aware of the treatment of a fistula, which is done through surgery with the hands.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for there are fistulas that reach major veins and arteries, or nerves, or the spinal cord, or the intestine, or the bladder, or to one of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the spine, or to any of the joints formed by this connection, such as the hand and foot; and those that are close to the main part of the limb and similar ones.
Therefore, those fistulas of this type are among the illnesses with difficult healing, and they do not receive cure, and working on them is a waste of effort and ignorance.
And if any of the fistulas is not deep to the areas I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then take a probe or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and you seek it out with it.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily guided and curved towards the winding path.
And if the fistula has many orifices, it is not possible for you to locate them with a probe.
Then irrigate the orifices with their orifices, for the moisture with which you irrigate it goes toward the other orifices and runs through them.
And then inquire meticulously, by what means you can ascertain if there are bones or nerves, and whether the cavity of the fistula is distant or close, or if it is a fistula with many orifices and is all about this, with the utmost of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the location of the pain by your pressing with your hand on the area, and similar to the meanings and accidental causes of an abscess.
And when you have considered all those matters with true judgment, then proceed to the cure according to your confidence;  and you should consider whether the fistula is a superficial, close, or exposed fistula; or in a salty place; distant from a joint, or nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places that I have told you about.
Then you should dry the fistula as previously described in the narration, and remove that which is in that filtration and putrid flesh, and that which has grown in the opening of the flesh, or of the boil and the like, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the fistula is of deep origin, and is of a severe nature, then it is necessary to incise it at its depth as much as possible for you, and then clean it of all its putrid flesh; and then administer some wraps in sharp medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have grasped with iron.
And do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine corrodes everything that remains in the fistula's cavity of corruption.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
However, if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not find any cause of a vein, nerve, or a major limb in it, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is any corruption or blackness on it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that promotes flesh growth until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and still discharges pus, as before, then know that you have not achieved complete eradication of the corruption; and then you open it up again more carefully, thoroughly scrape it and cleanse it with your most powerful tools, and then restore it.
And so, if it is healed, it is good; but if not, then know that the corruption lies in the distant depths of the body, which has not been reached, and so there is no remedy but to leave it alone.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then you remove it with delicate forceps suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then carefully remove all of them, and leave nothing behind with your strength.
But if some difficulty arises from the humors, and in their incision there is no fear or error, then cut through those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if there is no preparation to remove the bones at your hour, then apply to the wound something that can hold the wound, and leave it until it is about the bones, the flesh, and the ligaments, and keep the wound from becoming consolidated, and make the incision you have made in your operation constricted.
Furthermore, if you are afraid, apply to it cotton soaked in heated oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, because when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and extraction of them is eased.
However, if it is a large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone or similar to those, and only the surface of it is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only from behind until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
However, if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and cut entirely to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, around 30 years old, who experienced pain in his thigh, and the matter (disease) from within the body had spread to the thigh, and it was a matter of a large abscess, and it was not an external cause.
And then the condition was prolonged by the error of the physicians until the abscess opened, and many materials (pus) escaped, and its cure was delayed until the leg was fistulous, and many orifices were created in it, all of which discharged pus and the body's humidities.
And then a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and none of them were skilled in the art, until I was called upon.
And I saw his leg and the materials that flowed from those orifices in large quantities; and the man's body was already emaciated, and his complexion was yellow.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices, and the probe reached the bone.
And then I probed all the orifices and found that they led to every part of the leg.
And then I cut and incised on one of those orifices until I exposed part of the bone, and found it corrupted, already decayed and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, having penetrated into the marrow.
And then I closed what had been exposed and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I judged that it was not in the bone, but that corruption which I had incised and closed, and I had already extracted it.
And then I applied restorative medicines to the wound for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I reopened and exposed the bone a second time over the first exposure, and found continuous corruption within the bone.
And I closed what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was forced to perform the healing and restoration of it, and it was not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to dissect the bone little by little, specifically, forcing its restoration, and it was not restored until I incised it around a palm's width, and extracted it with its marrow.  Then I restored that with medicines, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And no further repetition in the operation and sectioning of it is necessary except for the weakness of the patient, and the patient's low tolerance and fear of death, for the patient had fainting spells at all hours due to excessive evacuation, a bad syncope.
And so it was healed entirely, and there was no soft flesh in the place of the bone; and its condition within the body was good, and its strength returned; and it was restored to its original condition, and it caused no difficulty in walking or harm in any way.
But if the bone is protruding from its place in the body, then you should section it in this way:  You should take a bandage and tighten it at the end of the protruding bone, and order someone to extend that outward; and place a thicker woolen bandage over the first bandage; and then bind it over the flesh beneath the bone, and extend the end of the bandage to pull the flesh back into its place, and you should uncover the flesh in the area you wish to cut so that the cutting doesn't harm the flesh.
And place a stick or board under the bone properly, for when you do this, the cut is not prohibited from severing the corrupted flesh.
And the incision should be made on the corrupted area little by little, because if the corruption is in the hollow of the bone and not apparent to the senses, it will need to be incised again.
And if the bone is corrupted but not protruding, with one part continuing into the other, and the corruption is in the middle or a part of it, then you should uncover all the flesh; then place a stick underneath, and then cut along the first part where the corruption is until the incision continues to the other part.
And let the incision be along the area of the corruption, a little bit at a time, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut that corrupted joint, and cut the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then it is not a reasoned thing but rather an unnatural process.
And if the corruption is in the palm of the hand or sole of the foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever way appears to you, and cut and cleanse it according to every possible method, and by any means rectify it, as long as it doesn't injure any vein or nerve.
And know that the incisions and saws to cut these bones are very specific to the placement of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety, size, and smallness, and their hardness and rarity.  Therefore, you must prepare instruments appropriate to every type of operation.
And know that this operation indicates to you the type of instrument you need; when you have great study, long experience, and knowledge of the methods of this art, and experience with the nature of illnesses, then you yourself will already know what instruments are appropriate for each illness.
