Of the cutting edges and the sawing of bones.
The extremities can be damaged either by an internal or external cause, or if your treatment with these medicines does not improve the condition, or sometimes the damage moves towards the limb not affected by any cause.  Then, you must cut into that limb, up to where the damage has spread, to make sure the patient is saved from death or severe injury, which is a greater harm to the limb.
And the sign of the affected area is that the limb becomes black, as if a fire has already burned it, or the blackness can spread until the hardness affects that limb and the entire body; and therefore cut it with a surgical incision.
And similarly, if the cause of the damage is a blow from venomous things, such as a scorpion, a viper, or a weasel, and similar creatures.
And if the damage or injury is in the extremity of the finger, then you cut the finger and do not allow the damage to spread to the hand.
And similarly, if the damage reaches the hand, then you cut the hand from the base of the wrist, and do not allow the damage to spread to the arm's socket.
And if, instead, it affects that area, then cut the arm up to the elbow at the joint.
And if the damage goes beyond the elbow, then in that case, death of the patient is certain; and administering any other remedy for this is a waste of effort.
And similarly, do the same for the foot. When the damage reaches the toe of the foot, then you cut it down to one of the joints.
And if it spreads to the sole of the foot, then you cut off the entire foot. But if it goes up to the knee, then you cut the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the damage has already reached the knee, then there is no cure for him but to let him go, and leave the patient to die.
And the way to cut or sever the limb is to tighten the ligament in the part below where you want to cut, and tighten another ligament over the spot; and the surgeon should extend a ligament to the lower parts, and extend another ligament upwards; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments with a wide blade until all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be cut or severed.
And it is necessary to place cloths around all parts so that the wound does not touch anything, and that the patient receives a well-fitted and carefully treated abscess.
But if you cause a flow of blood when you operate, then you cauterize the wounded area carefully, or put some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I relate to you what happened to a man on his foot, this same accident which I and you have discussed, which is that he got a blackening on his foot with a burn that resembled a fire's combustion.
And that corruption first occurred in his toe until it spread to the entire foot.
And then the man himself prepared, because he saw the healing of the limb proceed with the intensity of what he felt in pain and burning, and he incised that foot down to the joint, and was healed.
That same corruption happened to him for a long time in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I was compelled to counteract that excess with what I could place on his hand, to cleanse his body; but the excess was not repelled, and it began to spread to the other fingers until it began to affect his whole hand.
And he urged me to cut his hand, but I did not want to do this, hoping for the expulsion of that excess, and fearing that the incision of his hand might cause his death, for the patient's strength was at its lowest ebb.
And after he left me, he returned to his land, and word reached me that the man prepared himself and incised his whole hand, and was healed.
And I have not related this story except to be of help in understanding this disease, and that it might serve as a sign by which it is recognized and treated according to its nature.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs on a fleshy part of the body and the space of the abscess has enlarged to collect pus, and it has been broken and perforated, and pus has been discharged, leaving the area empty like a vessel, and the skin over it is thin like a fine cloth, and it has not progressed to deeper corruption, and has not affected the bone, nerves, or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
However, if the space has enlarged to the point that the corruption has spread to another part of the body, then it is called a fistula, and not a sinus.
And the treatment for it is to cut through that skin completely, just as it is involved, especially if it is thin and like a cloth, and make sure that it does not adhere to the area due to its corruption.
But if you expect the skin to adhere and that corruption has not yet spread deeper, and the skin has an opening on the surface, then treat it with an enema.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large, and the discharge has a foul odor, then irrigate it with an Egyptian medicine, which is made of oil, vinegar, and honey in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and heated over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and resembles honey.
Then take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and irrigate the sinus with that, and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine there for two hours.
Then extract that with a sponge; and do this for as many days as are necessary until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.
And sometimes it is irrigated with ash water, when this medicine is not available to you; and you should take the ash of wood, and pour water over it; then filter that and irrigate the sinus with it until you are confident that the sinus is now closed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then irrigate it with water and honey, and add crushed herbs.  Or, irrigate it with wine and honey mixed, because the property of honey is to cleanse and wash, and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when the wine contains an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus is already healed, then irrigate it with something that promotes the growth of flesh there.
And you should take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with warmed oil and astringent wine; or irrigate it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties I have already described in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, preventing the enema tube from entering, then gradually widen it with a small iron, and place warm, Egyptian or basil ointment there, until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the lips together with sutures, leaving just enough room for the enema tube to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the fistula comes out is elevated to the upper parts, then you should dissect it down to the lowest point possible, so that the fistula runs downwards, for when the fistula is obstructed deep inside the sinus, the flesh will grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to dissect the sinus to the lowest bone as desired, then you must prepare the affected limb with a method that allows the fistula to drain slightly, according to what is appropriate for you, and that nothing is obstructing the fistula's passage.
But that which is over the sinus opening, must remain in place at all times, to prevent anything that is causing the fistula from coming out. And so that you may know if what is draining from the fistula is much or little, or is digested, or undigested.
And by this, consider the location of the sinus, whether the patient feels pain there, and if there is an abscess.
And when your treatment has continued in this way, and you see the matter discharged over the sinus opening is beginning to adhere or stick, then on the third day, loosen it, and when you loosen the scab, then change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle, thin virus has spread, then do not despair of the adhesion of it, for healing of it is on its way, for there are some people in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and in others, the opposite is true.
And if from the sinus for many days undigested matter has come out, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the medicines towards drying, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and their virtue, they become dry like a diafinicon ointment, when in it a superfluity of zegi (a type of medicinal ingredient) has been added.
The final advice for healing is to take diafinicon ointment, dissolve it in rose oil, and add old wine to it, in its substance, and then mix it thoroughly, and use that.
And similarly in this treatment, if there is no matter, use diafinicon ointment, or honey when it is cooked until thick, and apply it; or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind them all into powder, and sprinkle them over the honey while it is on the fire.  Then spread this mixture on a cloth and press it onto the sinus; or apply the thick, cooked honey to a cloth and press that onto the sinus.
Sometimes, aristolochia longa, roots of lilies (and it is *lilium celesti*), and the powder of eryngo and centaury are administered.  Grind and sift these ingredients, and mix them into the honey I have previously described. Use this mixture.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone or other hard members, or in the ligaments. Then you should treat it with a fistulous cure, as described above.
Of the treatment of whitlow, and of a bruised nail, and the incision of a swollen finger, and the cutting.
Whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of a finger or toe, and frequently arises on other fingers.  If it is prolonged and its cure is less likely, or is an abscess of a hot abscess, or is corrupt, it will produce matter until it eats away the base of the nail, and often entirely corrupts it, and often spreads its corruption to the bone until it has a foul odor, the end of the finger is blackened, and its colour is dark.
And therefore, when you have done as we have said in the division and your treatment does not succeed, you should cut away all the excess flesh remaining around the nail with an iron and then cauterize the wound with that, for cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail's appendages have already begun to press on the flesh inside, and start to prick and irritate it, then you should place a thin strip under the edge of the nail that is pricking the flesh, and push that flesh up and cut it easily, and apply the remaining flesh with burning, corrosive medicines until everything is removed. Then treat the area with ointments until it heals.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut into and remove that bone, for a bone that is corrupted will never heal.
And if the erosion and corruption have spread to the finger, then cut it at one of its joints, according to the anticipated restoration, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail has suffered blows or friction, and there is intense pain, you must first bleed the patient, then dry the nail with a sharp lancet in a transverse cut from top to bottom, and be careful not to cut into the flesh under the nail, as you will cause the patient intense pain, and will cause the excessive inflamed flesh to grow in the area. Then treat the area with whatever soothes the pain.
However, a swollen finger, which is found in some men's hands, is easily fleshy, and is easily found in the bones, or is easily found in the nails.
And some of them are born at the base of the joint, or their origin is in some of the finger's joints. But that which is born in some joints of the finger is not harmful, and that which is born in the joints is easily inflamed.
And for those that are fleshy growths, the incision is gentle, and you should cut into that from its base with a broad lancet.
But for that which is born at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of the excision of it.
But for that which is born on the finger at the joints, you should first cut the flesh around it in a circular incision down to the bone, then use a suitable saw to sever it, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the fingers become fused together, either by the nature of the person's birth, or due to a wound, burn, or similar cause, then it is necessary to separate that fusion until the fingers return to their natural shape.
And then place linen strips soaked in warm oil between them to prevent them from fusing together, and separate them or put a thin lead plate between them until they are properly healed.
And similarly, if fusion occurs between any finger and the palm, then you should separate that fusion according to the appropriate preparation and the shape of the limb.
Concerning the cutting of varicose veins.
Varicose veins are large, twisted veins filled with melancholic excess that appear on various parts of the body, most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purge of black bile from the body several times with a strong expulsion, and afterward, bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
However, the cure for them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they are incised and the black blood is drawn out, and the other method is that the vein is raised and entirely extracted.
The cutting of it is done in this manner: first, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, phlegmy blood in the leg is resolved; then, firmly bind the patient's leg from just above the knee to below the knee with a bandage; next, cut the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision; then, lift the black blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and downwards, until the desired amount of blood has been drawn out, which will reduce the power of the ailment.
And then bandage it, and order the patient to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the cure by phlebotomy when the veins are filled, and this is useful to the patient.
But the raising of them is done in this way: you raise the patient's leg, if it is very hairy, and then place them in a bath and soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins are prominent; or, if a bath is not available, it is done by vigorous exercise until the limb is warmed.
And then cut the cord along the vein, either lengthwise or behind the knee, or just below the heel.
And then open the cord with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears, and it is near the appearance of that redness changing to blackness.
And then, as you empty it, you see from the cord that white thread which is a string.
And then you put a ray under that [the string] so that it is raised and pulled away from the cord, and suspend it with a hook or a lion's claw.
And then make another incision near that incision, after three days, and then scrape the cord on the part above the vein until it appears.
And then raise it and cut it in a posterior incision toward the heel, and then extract it and raise it until it is out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until all of it is out; and then cut it.
And if you are unable to extract and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double thread and tie it and pull it out, or insert a stick/rod under it and turn your hand around in every direction until it is thrown out. And be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it will be difficult for you to elevate it and it will have caused harm to the patient.
And when all of it has been expelled, then place on the affected area wool soaked in mixed oil and unmixed oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having tortuosity to which it is wrapped in parts, and is not straight as we have said, then you must cut along every part of its tortuosity and in the places where it is wrapped, and then suspend it with hooks until you have elevated it entirely.
And be careful in your incision not to cut the vein and not to harm it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not sharp like the hooks, nor does it have a sharp tip so as not to damage the vein; and it is of great flexibility, like a lion's, because if it were delicate, it would cut the vein due to its thinness; it therefore has an excess of thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like in the land of the Arabs and in the hot, arid lands of India, where there is little moisture and in which no grass or tree grows.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that arises under the skin, like when snakes, worms, and grubs, and worms that breed between the skin and the flesh, enter the body.
A sign of the onset of this condition is a violent inflammation in the leg where the vein occurs;  then the area becomes blistered;  and then the vein begins to emerge from the blister area, appearing as a sprout or plant root.
And when its extremity appears, then it is necessary to wrap a lead weight of one drachma (or two drachmas) around that area and bind it to the leg.
And as many times as any cause of this appears on the outward parts, wrap it in lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged, then you should cut off a part of it and wrap the remaining part; and do not cut that part off by the root until it has come out, for if you cut it by the root, it contracts and enters the flesh, causing abscesses and deep ulceration in the area.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled from the body, and no trace of it remains within.
And from this vein, there have been cases of it growing to five palms and ten fingers in length, and it has been reported to me that one grew to twenty palms in length.
And if it is incised during its treatment, then insert a probe into the wound and perforate it with a long probe, until everything within the matter is evacuated. Then apply putrefaction-reducing remedies to the area for several days.  Afterwards, treat it as one would an abscess.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears near the joint of the foot or on the foot itself.  It has multiple openings, and from each opening sprouts a branch.
And therefore treat it in the same way as we have described in the division and with the aforementioned methods.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called the bovine disease.
This illness is called "bovine disease" in some lands near us, because it often affects cattle.  It is a small worm which is born between the skin and flesh, and it travels throughout the body, ascending and descending, manifesting itself in the patient's limbs until it breaks through the skin and exits.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, serpents, maggots, and ascarids are produced in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it travels through the body and ascends to the head, and reaches the eye, it frequently causes a severe inflammation there and enters, destroying the eye.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it can only be done according to its movement and appearance as perceived, and then you must tightly bind and constrict that which is above it and that which is below it, and then you should cut open the skin over it and extract it.
And if it is embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the area until it dies, and the most feared consequence is the putrefaction of it, which corrupts the area as we have stated.
And if you see that creature has reached the head, near the eye, then tightly bind above the eyebrow with strong constriction, and then cut open the skin over it and extract it.  The patient should use medicines to cleanse their body of bad, putrid humors, and avoid foods that produce putrefaction.
Of the section concerning the illness of the nose.
This illness is called "nafir" in our land, and it is a pain that comes upon certain limbs, and then it moves from one limb to another.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein near the arm's elbow, and after an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm ascending, but more quickly than a worm would, like liquid silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain moved to that location and settled into the muscle, and then I stayed there for an hour.
And then it moved to the other arm until it reached the other arm.
And then I observed that it was moving throughout her body as I saw; and I was very amazed by its movement from one limb to another and the speed of this movement. And I had already seen this before with my own eyes in this way, although I have seen many cases where pain moves from limb to limb. But I did not see this with my eyes in this way as in the woman.
And I did not consider it to be anything else than this, because the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and in this condition, this windy sensation appeared and moved.
And it is necessary that this type of reaction does not appear in those who are delicate and with moist bodies and hidden veins.
And if you want to treat him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eyes as we have said, so you gently and lightly press on the area until this trapped wind leaves, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your eyes, then you treat that with remedies for the body and with those that expel winds, and cure them with stinky pills and with pills of serpent and similar remedies.
Of extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types, and according to the places of the body where they strike, for their diversity is that of those with large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, that is, solid; and of those that have three angles, and of those that have tongues; and of those that have fins.
And the signs of the brain are when an arrow strikes it, and pierces the bone, and exits from the skull which is above the brain, for it causes sudden violent pain, and scotoma (blindness), and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of thought and vomiting of bile, and the voice is often lost and the voice is destroyed, and white moisture resembling dust comes out from the wound, and the area looks like the flesh is rotting.
And therefore, if you see these signs, then you abstain from treatment and from extracting the arrow, unless it is on the outside.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart, are that it is near the left breast, and feels as if it were lodged in a hard, not hollow, substance; and quickly the arrow's movement is like the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and the extremities become cold, and there is cold sweat, and fainting; and thus you know that death is certain without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are the expelling of frothy blood from the wound, and the vessels near the neck become inflamed, and the patient's complexion changes, and the patient breathes with great difficulty, and seeks a draught of cool air.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane that is in the feet, the pain will be near the small ribs, and there is great suffering with violent pain and sighing, and all the muscles throughout the limbs are affected.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then quickly there is vomiting of undigested food from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen, and is lodged there, there is either a wound of the intestines, or the stomach, or a rupture of the intestines.  There is no hope of a cure if the arrow is not extracted.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine leaks outside, and there is some cause for it to flow out, and there is sudden pain on the patient, then know that it is fatal.
However, other limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulders, back, vertebrae, ribs, hips, legs, and similar parts of the body, are saved depending on the greater disposition [of the patient] when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery, and is not a poisoned arrow.

And let the bloodletting be done skillfully.
However, if the escape of blood is prohibited, then you should repeatedly immerse the patient's foot in warm water and allow the blood to flow out until it is evacuated.
And if the bloodletter errs the first time, then repeat the bloodletting slightly higher up, because the spot is healthier; and do not fear harming the patient, but be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly, do the same to the sole of the other foot as well.
And for the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is below the heel, beneath the bone of the outer side.
And the benefit of bloodletting in this case, when it is due to hot blood, is relief from hip pain.
And the procedure for bloodletting in this case is that the patient enters a bath, and tightly wraps the thigh from the groin area up to above the heel with a long, thin bandage for four days, because it is only visible through this.
And therefore, when it appears, then you perform the bloodletting, according to each method available to you and along the vein—and that is better—either by incision or by section, because the area is inflamed.
And it is often deeply hidden in many men; and if you find it, and it doesn't appear to the touch, then bloodlet a branch of it, which is the one that appears on the back of the foot between the said auricular and anular [tendons/areas].
And be careful of the nerves, and let the blood flow out in the amount you want.
And then loosen the constriction and place a cloth over the bloodletting site and compress the area, for it will quickly heal.
Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping vessels are made of horns, or of earthenware, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with incision and blood extraction, and the other is cupping without incision.
And this cupping without incision is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cupping with incision and blood extraction, there are fourteen locations on the body.  One is at the nape of the neck, the back part of the head; and one in the middle of the two sides of the neck together; and cupping on the chin below the jaw, further down from the mouth; and cupping on both shoulder blades; and cupping on the elbow, and it is on the end of the forearm; and cupping on the two forearms; and cupping on both sides of the chest; and cupping on both thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood from only the large veins, but from the small veins scattered throughout the flesh, and thus does not weaken the body as much as bloodletting, nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by fullness until the whole body has been emptied.
However, if necessity compels cupping for illness or for a chronic condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of the month, in the middle of the month, or at any time.
And therefore, when any of these conditions occur, especially if it is in the middle or third of the month, then we command cupping to be performed after two or three hours have passed.
Cupping the neck is helpful when it relieves heaviness in the head and that which is clouded in the eyes, but this should be done after emptying the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advised to use it for those with cold conditions, or those with a cold, as it gives them great harm.
And therefore, it is not suitable for use on the elderly, or those with cold illnesses in their heads.
But for those on whom it is continuously used, numbness is caused;  and therefore you should instruct the cupper to gently move the cup slightly downward in parts, to prevent the generation of numbness.
But cupping with alcohol is in place of phlebotomy of the deep vein (vena fusca) and the basilica vein: and thus it is beneficial for asthma, and for constriction of the chest, and for splitting of the chest, and for coughs, and for congestion.
And the alcohol cupping should be gently raised, for if it is positioned too low, it generates weakness in the body and the stomach.
And cupping with Alhagdahan (a type of cupping) is beneficial for accidental head pains and for ophthalmia, and migraine, and fever, and for toothache; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press too hard with the scarification, so as not to cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin is beneficial for ulcers in the mouth, and for gum decay, and for similar ailments in the mouth, and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the veins that are in both lips.
But cupping done above the two shoulder blades is beneficial for heatstroke, which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on both sides within the armpits is helpful as a substitute for phlebotomy of three veins, namely the basilica, the cephalic, and the basilic veins, for it draws blood from all the smaller veins within the flesh, and those smaller veins draw from the larger ones until the drawing is directed toward the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply with the scarification, for the area is lacking flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping treatment applied to the buttocks is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and ulcers located further down.
And you should instruct the cupper to use a large, bronze cupping glass, for the area needs a strong suction;  and delicate, thin cupping glasses are easily broken and should be avoided, along with excessive scarification.
However, cupping of the legs should be done with careful consideration, for they draw blood from the whole body, and are beneficial for long-standing pain in the kidneys and bladder; they induce menstruation, and are helpful for fever and inflammation; they are a substitute for bloodletting from two arms, and in that case, two fingers or two tubes.
However, they significantly weaken the whole body and can cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of cupping in this instance is comparable to the benefit of cupping both legs.
And the proper method for applying cupping is to initially place an empty cupping glass and gently draw the suction, and do not prolong the cupping, but instead place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors may gather to the spot through natural accumulation. And do not cease repeating this and continuing until the area appears reddened and swollen and the redness of the blood is visible.
And then you make incisions and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the condition of the body's disposition.
For a person with delicate skin and prone to bruising, you should only incise once, so that the area is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the incision slightly, and to moderate the suction gently.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary to incise twice, because on the first occasion the path for thinning the blood and its watery components is opened. But on the second, the scrutiny gained from the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then a third incision should be made to achieve the final result.
And finally, when we want to extract blood slowly or a little bit, a single incision is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we make many incisions; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make deep incisions.
And the depth of the incision should be just the thickness of the blade.
If the skin where the cupping is to be applied is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, the sites for the cups should be anointed with soothing, opening, and resolving oils.  If it's summer, use almond oil, violet oil, mild rose oil, or chamomile oil; if it's winter, use narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or bloodroot oil, and similar remedies.
And if the excess is thick and cold, the oil should be of amaranth, balm, almond, or fennel, and similar ones.
But if the patient has wide pores and soft skin, the use of oil should be avoided.  Instead, the sites for the cupping should be washed with water or cool water from the sorrel plant, or with water from the pomegranate, or with water from the wild lettuce, and similar liquids.
And for those whose blood is excessively moist, wash their cups with sour wine, or with water mixed with myrtle and sumac, and similar remedies.
And for those with thick superfluities, wash their cups with old wine, or with water from yarrow, or a decoction of dill, or chamomile, and similar remedies.
And the cupping should be avoided before a bath;  it should be administered shortly after the bath, for an hour or two, and the patient should rest after the cupping.
What is it that should be governed (or controlled) in the ventosation or bloodletting before the cupping?
One must consider at the outset: if the person being cupped or bled is choleric, and has hot blood and inflammation, then they should take cooling things, such as  milfoil (or possibly mugwort) infused in vinegar, endive, lettuce, secanabin, and similar cooling drinks (juices or infusions).
And give them foods such as poultry, and the flesh of birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and similar things.
And for those with a cold constitution, they should drink syrup of almug, or syrup of alsecanabin, or of seeds, and sumac, and a light-smelling wine between new and old;  they should be advised to consume little food and drink, and their diet should be limited to foods like poultry, hempseed, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more substantial than the food.
And it is necessary to give certain people, at certain times, theriac, or medications containing musk (or musk-scented things), or alchelite, before cupping or bloodletting or shortly afterwards, so that the principal limbs are strengthened and the blood is refined; and they should not take them hot.
However, cupping without incision should be placed over the liver, spleen, gout, navel, and the joints of the knees and the side of the hips, for these limbs cannot tolerate incision upon them.
And this is not desired for drawing blood from a limb, but rather for drawing blood away from the limb, just as we place a cupping glass over the nipple during a bleeding from the nose; and we use it so that a cold, viscous wind trapped in the limb is dissolved, just as we place it on the abdomen and navel; for that method thins the limb, warms it, and relieves the pain by resolving the wind.
And it has already been placed on the ribs when there is a blockage or congestion in them, for through the power of strong suction it easily breaks up the blockage and eradicates the congestion from its place.
And similarly, it works when placed on the liver and spleen to remove wind trapped within them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty, with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and larger, either with warm water alone, or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire:  The width of the opening should be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and the amount of the opening should be half a palm; and it should be on its side, in the location in the circumference, half of it, the hole should be small enough that a needle can barely enter it.
It should be made of red or citrus wood, with thick edges, lion, oak, and other hardwoods so that it does not damage the limb when placed on it.
And there should be a transverse rod of wood or iron in the middle, upon which the candle with the fire is placed.
And this cupping glass should be significantly larger than the one previously described; and the smaller one should be smaller, according to the illness and the age of the person using it.
And the cupping glasses for children and the weak are different from the cupping glasses for young people and those with strong bodies.
And the way to place this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to heat the cupping glass with a decent fire of wood before a small candle of wax, and place that on the middle of the transverse rod in the center of the cupping glass, so that the fire is kindled around the lower part, so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be placed over the hole we mentioned, until when we have applied the cupping glasses and don't want the finger to be exposed and the vapor to escape through that hole, the cupping glass will quickly be dissolved.
And heat the cupping glass according to its method, and repeat as needed.
But a cupping glass used in a pleurisy ailment, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or hole, is administered only by filling it with water and placing it directly on the limb.
And this is its form:  And the larger this cupping glass is, the better, because it can receive a larger quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not administered to many limbs, but only to those limbs where the application of cupping glasses is not possible due to their small size, such as the feet and gums, and similar parts; or to a limb devoid of flesh, such as the penis and nose, and parts similar to them.
And the quality of using those leeches is that you should find them in clean, clear, spring water.
And then they should be left in clean water during the day and night until they are hungry and no residue remains in their stomachs;  and then the body should be emptied at the outset by bloodletting and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it becomes red; and then the leeches should be placed upon it.
And when they are full and drop off.
And if suction of the area is possible with a cupping glass, it is better still for treatment.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water; and it should be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged around the attachment of the leeches, and if it is oozing, then you soak a cloth in cold water and place it over it until the oozing stops.
And if the blood is excessive, then sprinkle crushed vitriol, or other similar styptic substances, upon it until the blood is stopped; or place the roasted halves of beans on the area, and leave them until the beans adhere to the area, and the blood will stop.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when possible changing them.
And if the leeches are prohibited from being used, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood flows; and then place them, for when they sense blood, they will immediately attach.
And when you want them to detach, sprinkle a little crushed alum, or salt, or ash, on them, and they will detach immediately.
Thus begins the treatment of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of the bones that occur by accident.
O, son, know that foolish and common physicians, and those who have never studied the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them, boast of this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of learned men in our land is hidden; and I have not forgotten any of it, nor have I embraced it hastily.  Instead, I have acquired it through the lengthy study of ancient books and the intense examination of their understanding, until I extracted this knowledge from them.
And subsequently I have applied myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my knowledge contains and upon which my experience has been applied; since I have abridged this for you and freed it from lengthy explanations; and I have condensed it to the very end of the summary; and I have shown it at the conclusion of the explanation; and I have presented various forms of the instruments used in it, as is done by adding explanations, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
A summary and collection of the essential elements of bone fractures, before we begin to discuss the repair of fractured limbs and their various types. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject matter; and the chapters, which the craftsman must understand at the beginning and stand upon the truth of these, that is, you and those who are eager to learn or learn this noble art from you.
Therefore, I say that when a fracture, dislocation, twisting, or fall occurs, then you should begin with phlebotomy or alleviation, or both together, if no reason prevents it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the victim is a child, or an old person in decrepitude, or if the time is one of extreme heat or extreme cold.
And afterward, let his diet consist of cool herbs and the flesh of birds and rabbits; and avoid fatty meats and foods that fill the veins with blood, until you are certain of a healthy abscess and if you do not fear the outflow of matter to the limb.
And then, restore the patient to his former regimen, with which he is accustomed.
And therefore, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nourishing foods, substantial and heavy, which have viscosity, such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and porridge, and calves' feet and belly, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar to these, and with this regimen, the fracture is more easily and better coagulated.
And know that broken bones in those who are in the prime of life and in the aged, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature because of the wasting of their limbs.
But nevertheless, nature joins and brings together over the broken bone some substance similar to glue, which is thick and adheres to and tightens it until it is completely glued and bound together, until it reaches the point of strength and firmness, like a fire at the beginning, until nothing hinders it from its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient eats foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the skull bone; and the fracture of the foot bone is different from the fracture of the arm bone; and similarly, all the other limbs are different from each other.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is diverse types of fractures within themselves. And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is a curved fracture, and those that occur in a star-like pattern; and sometimes a fracture runs along the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the bone, jagged, and splintered, and compound and incomplete, just as happens when a writing pen is prepared, and there is a fracture with a break and a crack in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for every type, there is a particular method or treatment for restoring it, according to the distinct memory of it in its proper places.
Various signs of bone fracture. And by these things is a bone fracture known:  its twisting, and the prominence and appearance of it to the sense, and that of it when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor is it made apparent by your touch when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not experience great pain, then there is no fracture.
However, it is possible there might be a twisting or small crack, so you should not waste your effort pressing on it with your hand and a full compression.  Instead, put on some of the medicines that bring memory to that moment, which are suited to that place, and then carefully bind it with a subtle bandage.
And know that when the place is bent but not split in two, or otherwise damaged in its arrangement, and when all parts of it remain in their position, then it is necessary for you to begin straightening it immediately, for fear of a harmful abscess occurring there.
And if an abscess forms there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then heal it in any way you can with gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that restoring and healing the soft tissues is easier than the bone in which damage has occurred.
And then strengthen it according to the memory of the method.
But if the bone is damaged, then it is necessary to extend that broken limb in two parts, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two cords, or with one cord and the hand.
And be careful in positioning the limb back in its natural shape, while the broken limb parts are extended, then you must make every effort to return those additions to their proper places with ingenuity and in any way you can.
And guard yourself from causing further pain or injury to the patient during your treatment; and endeavor, using your skill, to apply one bone to its corresponding partner according to their best forms.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that [bone] with your hand, and if you find any other reason, then correct that and make it even according to your skill.
Avoid excessive stretching and compression, as some foolish doctors, who often cause abscesses and contractions in the limb through their treatment, have been observed to do.
The type of binding for the immobilized limb is what I recommend; so that you understand that broken limbs vary in their smallness and largeness and in their shape.
And for those that are small, such as the arm, finger, or foot, and similar parts, then the wrapping should be of a thin, soft cloth.
However, for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, a strong and firm wrapping is needed, for a firm bandage connects the large limb, tightening it all around, so that no gap remains.
And then, proceed with applying suitable bandages to the fracture site, using three or four wraps, depending on the limb's needs; and slightly tighten the bandage with your hand.
And then move to the highest part of the fracture site, and tighten less than you did at the fracture site. And then slowly loosen the wrapping of the fracture site, gradually relaxing the tightening, until you have pulled a significant part of the limb back into place.
And then take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken area with a wrap, and then move the wraps to the lowest part of the fracture; and your procedure should consist of tightening the bandage and loosening it, following the method described for the first wrap above.
And then place between the wraps a pad of linen or cloth to address any twisting of the fracture, if there is any twisting; otherwise, do not place anything there.
And then place another bandage over these carefully applied wraps, if there is no inflammation or festering sore in the area; otherwise, apply something to soothe the sore and reduce the swelling; leave that on for several days; and then tighten the wraps over that.
And the splints should be made from half of a sturdy, well-shaped branch, or of pinewood, or palm fronds; and the width of each splint should be in this shape and form:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly larger and wider than the others, and the splint's length should match the limb's size; and then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your initial tightening; and then tie it securely above that, as previously described for tightening, and your tightening should be firmer closer to the fracture site, and the further you move from the fracture, the looser the tightening should be.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of linen; and if the threads are too thick, as I have seen some foolish surgeons use, using ropes or thick twisted threads, that is a big error, as the tightening will lack uniformity.
And similarly, very thin threads are not suitable, because you will not be able to tighten them as you would like, and there should be at least a finger's width between the splints.
And if the patient is irritated by the edges of the splints near the tightening, at the affected areas, then put some soft material like wool or soft linen under them, to prevent injury.
Sometimes the fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption of the continuity occurs; and I will discuss that in more detail in the second chapter.
And know that not every broken limb should be fastened with splints on the first day; for, if the limb is large, you should not apply splints until perhaps 3, 5, or more days have passed, depending on your judgment about the possibility of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters with which the ancients treated fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
A common method of plaster for fracture repair, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it neither causes heat nor cold: take powdered flour, specifically the fine flour that collects on the inside walls of the mill after grinding.
And prepare it just as it is, but sift it with egg white, and make a paste of it that is neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of plaster for treating fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take asafoetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and wild carrot root (which is the herb called "mignar"), all 1/4 ounce each.  Myrrh and aloe, 1/8 ounce each; aloes, 1/2 ounce; all ground and sifted, and mixed with warm water or egg white if the patient's constitution is heated. Then use this plaster, for it is a good composition, healing slight fractures, and beneficial to many people.
Another method of plaster, useful for fractures and sprains: take wild carrot (migrach) and white marshmallow root, 1/4 ounce each; myrrh and aloe, 1/8 ounce each; acacia, 1/6 ounce; and Armenian bole, 1/2 ounce.
All are ground finely in a mortar, sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and for pain and cracks and fractures: take wool, soak it in sour wine and boiled oil, and place it on the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoring, but rather provides a hot poultice to expel pain effectively.
Another method of plaster to restore a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy;  mix them together and make a plaster from them.
A quick plaster method, used for the restoration of broken bones and when you want to resolve remaining pus: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, violet flowers, and yarrow flour, all in equal parts; grind all together and mix with wine, unless the affected area is inflamed.
However, if it is inflamed, then treat it with damp coriander water or water and use that.
Another, stronger plaster for resolving stubborn pus before the limb is restored: take roots of marshmallow, flax seeds, balms, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all in equal parts; grind everything together and mix with willow water, fresh water, or wine, depending on the limb's condition and the color of its complexion.
However, Hippocrates in his book does not say that a plaster should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration, but rather a cerot (plaster) made of wax and oil, and nothing else;  he recommends it to be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
However, in Gaul, it is seen that plasters are placed on the broken limb during restoration, and in cases where there is drying out with a little warmth, such as myrrh, incense, and similar substances to those we have mentioned.
A plaster for healing wounds and pain: take equal parts of crushed figs, human skin (or bird's skin), marshmallow, and salt; grind and sieve them, then mix to form a plaster.
However, regarding the amount of time the bandage should remain in place and when it should be removed, consider whether the injury causes pain, swelling or if the bone is broken and displaced.  If not, then you should not remove it for several days. And if the area experiences intense pain, swelling, or inflammation, then you should remove or loosen the bandage immediately and remove the plaster from the area.
And take a clean cloth and a wet sponge, soak it in lukewarm water, and wash the area with it until the swelling subsides and the pain is eased.  Then, let the limb rest for an hour, and afterwards place a layer of wool soaked in vinegar and oil or rubbed oil on top, and bandage it for a night until you are certain of any heat or inflammation in the limb, and the pain is reduced.
Then apply a thin layer of this plaster again, and a small plaster, and do not apply the first constriction tightly; use a subtle approach until healed.
And if the abscess, stiffness, pain, inflammation, and all the symptoms have completely subsided, and you no longer need the plaster and bandage, then you should reapply them in the same manner as you did initially.
And if no symptoms of those we have mentioned appear in the limb, then do not loosen the bandage for three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes this is left on for twenty days, as you deem suitable based on the healing of the limb, as we have described, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh around it consolidates.
Then, increase the tightness of the bandage more than in your initial application, and increase the patient's intake of nourishing food, according to the patient's recovery.
But if you find the fracture site is becoming too dry and weakened, more than is appropriate, know that nourishment will not reach it.  Therefore, moisten it with warm water every time you loosen the bandage, for three days.  Ease the bandage a little, and through this action, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what those foolish healers do, restoring the fractured limb a second time when it was not properly restored at first, according to what is required, but rather restored in a crooked manner, is a mistake in their procedure, and great folly.  If they had done it correctly, the ancients in their writings would have explained it, and would have practiced it correctly.
And I have not seen any of them do this, which makes it more reasonable that it should not be done.
Second Chapter: Concerning fractures when they occur to the head.
The types of fractures are numerous, and their shapes are diverse, and their causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a blunt instrument, and the blow crushes the entire bone down to the skull, just as it does in a log, and thus this type is called a comminuted fracture; or the blunt instrument cuts a portion of the bone and cuts the flesh on it, and does not pass through to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called a simple fracture.
And the severity of these two fractures is either great or slight, and a fracture that is a crushing or grinding; and its cause is a blow with a stone or falling on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, approaching the membrane beneath the bone, or is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either great or slight.
And there is a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the fineness of a small hair, and therefore this type is called a capillary fracture.
And a fracture is caused either by falling or by a blow from a stone, and similar things, that causes some plate of the bone to be driven inward, and is made in the hollow place, as happens to the ribs when they are struck, and part of their entrance is driven inward.
This is more common in the bones of moist bodies, such as the heads of infants.
And in all these types of fractures, the pieces are scattered, like separated stars. And I will come to restore all of that to its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them, and by investigating them with probes and the removal of corrupted flesh that is on them.
A particular type is recognized because it is uncovered on the bone, and is cleaned and rubbed with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms.  Then, if you see symptoms that indicate significant danger, such as vomiting, diarrhea, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, swelling and stiffness of the limbs, and similar severe symptoms, the patient is in doubt, and will not likely recover.
And if you see less severe symptoms, you expect recovery, then you begin the treatment.
The reason for this is that if the afflicted person came to you at the beginning of the affliction, as in the winter months, you should consider removing the bone before the 14th day, according to the patient's condition.
And if it is during the summer months, then you should remove the bone before the 7th day, lest the area beneath the bone be corrupted by swelling, and the symptoms we have described occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the soft tissue covering the brain, and is with broken and crushed parts, then you should incise the broken and crushed parts, as I will explain to you.
And you should shave the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover the bone according to the custom and method appropriate to you, based on the nature of the affliction, and so that it is gentle on the patient.
And if you encounter bone exposure, blood flow, or a painful abscess, then you should oppose it with whatever is necessary, filling the area with cloths soaked in warmed wine and oil, and allowing the affliction to rest until the abscess subsides and the blood flow ceases; then begin with the cleaning of the bone and its removal.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow, sharp scalpel, and this is its form:  And then apply another scalpel, a little wider than the first; and this is its form:
And then apply a scalpel wider than the second one.
And it is necessary to have several different scalpels at your disposal, some wider than others; and some of them shorter than others, with sharpened edges, made of tempered iron or steel.
And use them with ease in striking the scalpel, so that you don't strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary to drill a hole around it before you apply the scalpels with the drills, which are called non-deepening drills.
And they are not named thus, except because they do not penetrate the bone's surface, as the drill's tip or edge that is below the pointed head is similar to a small edge or circle, which prevents submersion and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each appropriate to the thickness of each bone, so that you have on hand for every skull bone a drill tip that is sharp, its length and shortness adjusted to the thickness of that particular bone.
And this is the method for three types of drills:  To drill around a fractured bone, place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone is penetrated; then change the drill to another location, and make a hole roughly the size of the ray's thickness or approximately that.
And then you make incisions with scalpels between each hole in the bone, making it as thin as possible, as we have said, until you have removed the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument you have prepared, such as fine pliers and forceps.
And you must be very careful that the scalpel does not touch any part of the skull.
And once you have drilled around the bone, and if it is still connected to the skull, you must remove it by scraping and smoothing the rough edges of the bone with another tool similar to a scalpel, but it must be thinner and finer than the other scalpels.
And if any small pieces of bone remain, then remove them easily with the tools you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have mentioned.
And the other method of operation is gentle, free of fear, which Gualo described and praised highly; and this is his teaching: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin uncovering the part of the bone in the location of the most severe and obvious fracture, until when you have uncovered that part and have placed beneath it the tip of this slow and gentle scalpel, of which this is the shape:
And the lenticular part is blunt and does not cut anything, and the pointed part is on two sides, forward in length, like you see; so that the lenticular part is attached to the skull with the pointed cutting side against the bone.
And then strike the scalpel on one side with a small hammer until you cut through the entire bone with ease, as it has been turned; and you are certain of the skin that no accident will happen to it in any way. And if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were asleep.
Then, if any cause remains attached to the bone of the skin in certain places of the bone, you will cut it with the tip of the lenticular scalpel, and will release it with ease, for it is released without harm or fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the skin, and what is cut or removed from the bone is its surface, and it remains rough and with subtle irregularities, then it is necessary to smooth that roughness and remove those irregularities with thin razors, for which you have prepared various quantities, so that it is possible for you to apply the most suitable ones in every place and the most appropriate ones according to what you have in mind when you perform your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that in your procedure, with which you smooth the bone, you start with the widest of those razors, and then proceed to use the finest of them, and do not cease doing this successively until you come to using the finest and most subtle of all of them.
However, in each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, you must apply treatment according to what you perceive as necessary for their rectification.
And it is a cause not hidden from all those who have studied that art. And this, which we have written, demonstrates it in major fractures.
And if the pannicle is retracted when you reduce the bone, then you must take a cloth according to the size of the wound, immerse it in warmed oil, and place it over the wound's opening; then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and warmed oil, and place this on the first cloth, doing so as gently as possible, so as not to weigh down the wound; then apply a wide bandage over it, and do not tighten it more than is necessary to simply hold the wound closed.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a hot abscess; then remove it and apply medicines that are effective against dryness, such as flax seeds, barley flour, ashes, and similar powders.
And you should make these medicines into a powder, pulverized and applied to the wound, just as they are dried; and universally, apply any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to irritate.
And be careful, according to your skill, that the wound, in your treatment, remains clean, and is not clogged with ointment or anything else; nor allow the wound to become infected with harmful substances, for when they are applied to the membranes of the brain they corrupt and pollute it, and bring upon the patient an extremely harmful illness.
And this can happen in the skull, especially when the treatment of the bone is neglected, and there is blackening on its surface; therefore, if you observe the symptoms we have mentioned, know that this is a serious illness without a doubt.
And if the darkening is not from the wound itself but from the medicine applied, and the medicine itself is causing the darkening, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rubbed olive oil, and with these, moisten a cloth, then place it on the skull; and then treat it with the appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken in two parts simultaneously, but sometimes only in one.  This is because there are two bones in the nose; the lower part is cartilaginous and is not broken, but is only bruised, twisted, and deformed.
But if one of the two parts is broken, then you should insert your little finger into the nose, and with your index finger press the fracture inward, and with your thumb outward, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful not to cause more pain to the patient with your procedure.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it must be set with the end of a tool with a small, rounded tip.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same.
And strive for a restoration within the first day of the fracture, if possible; if not, then approximately the 7th or 10th, you must apply a soothing poultice.
And approximately insert a piece of linen cloth into the nasal opening, if the fracture is in one part, or put two pads, if the fracture is in two parts, and the pad should have enough bulk to fill the nasal cavity.
And some of the ancient healers say the pads should be soaked in a broth and changed daily, but I do not find this necessary.  Instead, the cloth should be soaked in a mixture of beaten egg and powdered spice.  Then leave the pad in place until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a pad, a bird's feather wrapped in linen is placed in the nose.  This is done when the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; if you wish, use this, and if you wish, use pads.
And if there is an abscess again, apply a plaster to the nose made with cerot or cotton dipped in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
However, if there is no suppurating abscess, then you should cover the external part with flour, powdered incense mixed with egg white.  Then place a linen bandage over that, and do not bind the nose with anything else.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then you should cut around them and remove them with instruments suitable for this; then close the wound and treat it with things that promote healing and consolidation, and with appropriate plasters.
And if there is an ulcer inside the nose, then you should treat it with pads and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by an open wound, then consider if the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two but bent inward at the edges, then its repositioning is easy.
And thus, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the said index finger of your right hand; and push the bend of the fracture inward with ease towards the outside, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; with that, gently straighten it.
And if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply extension to both parts according to their alignment until their reunion is possible.
And if you encounter a loosening of teeth along with a breakage, then hold those you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; then place a poultice on the broken jaw, then place a doubled cloth over it, and place a large, properly made bandage or a piece of lead, equal to the length of the jaw; and then tie it on top as you have the means to do so, applying the bandage, until it is healed.
And order the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him easily digestible food.
And if you estimate that the shape has already been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen it on the third day; and then you should correct whatever has been altered, and plaster it with powdered moli, with egg white, or with flour of oats, after removing the poultice, and place a soft wool bandage over the plaster.
Then, when this plaster is properly applied and the bone's position isn't further altered, leave it, do not remove it until the fracture is healed and strong; for often this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a painful boil occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its reduction until it is healed, and the boil is resolved.
And if the fracture is with a plaque, then consider whether it is a piece of the bone separated or splinters; then carefully remove those splinters with whatever is appropriate for that in the removal of splinters from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with leeches according to your need.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing further remains, then you dress the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, apply one of the appropriate plasters to it and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Often the collarbone is fractured on the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is fractured, or separated, or separated into two parts without any splinters, its restoration is easy; or it is splintered and difficult to restore; or the fracture is with an open wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without an open wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds and steadies the fractured collarbone, and the other stretches the neck towards that area.
And then grasp the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correctly formed, and is neither too prominent nor concave.
And if you require more extension, then you should place under the patient's neck a pad of cloth or wool, sized according to your needs; and extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you have correctly aligned the fracture.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone's end outward because it has adhered to the bone, then the patient should be placed supine on their neck, and you should place a cushion of medium size upon the patient's muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle below, so that the collarbone's bone is lifted away from the adhesion, and then you straighten the fracture and grasp it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are loose, then you should cut around them and easily remove those splinters.
And if it is embedded in the bone, then you create an incision with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, having placed the instrument used for the chisel beneath the collarbone; and this instrument is made of wood or iron, of which this is its shape:
And it is similar to a scoop, whose concavity is this; and its width should be according to your needs, depending on the size of the bone or the little amount of it.
But its length should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends, like a wedge, and one end be wide and the other narrow.
And if the rupture of the wound you have incised when you have extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are sure of a hot abscess, then you should bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's cut.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in vinegar, and rubbed oil and wine, and place it on top.
And ensure that the splint does not move away from the broken bone.  Therefore, you should see the patient daily, and when you see that the ligament is softened or the splint has become loose, then you should rectify this and tighten the bandage.
Then place the patient's pillow on their neck, and place a small cushion under their armpit beneath the pillow at night.  This is so that the patient's shoulder is elevated from the side of the wound, and thus the fracture of the clavicle is elevated from the muscle; or the patient's arm is tied to the neck.
Do not loosen the bandage unless there is a suppuration or abscess in the area, until twelve days have passed.
Then renew the plaster if you see the patient's skin reacting to it, and adjust it around the fracture, and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle is restored and solidified.
And it is strengthened and comfortable within twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is completed in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a fractured shoulder.
The shoulder blade is fractured at a point lateral to it, and it is not fractured from the center, but from the edges.
And therefore, when the fracture is located — and it is fractured in the middle of it — and is only known by touch, then according to the nature of the fracture, prepare it and restore it to its natural form by any means possible.
Then place on the area powder of millstones mixed with egg white and stop it; and then place a folded cloth; and then place a thin sheet of lead over it, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade or a little more.
But if beneath the lead there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you fill that hollow with plaster, and then raise the lead to an equal level.
And then press it (the lead) from above with a long, appropriate constriction and firm pressure on the lead until it is no longer displaced from its position.
And consider the ligature every day; and every time it is softened, bind it again and press it and re-position the lead if it has been displaced from its place.
And let the patient lie on their sound side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore, when this number of days has been completed, then you dissolve the ligature, and you must know for certain that it is from the bones from which the commencement is not feared, nor is their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the location of the weighty bone and place the splint underneath, then you cut it, remove it, and do as we said regarding the fracture of the furcula, for the treatment of an abscess, if this is the case here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this is less serious; but its extremities are often more seriously torn and crushed.
And the symptoms when the middle of the foot is broken include:  deviation of the parts inwards, great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, considerable weakness, and vomiting blood; and the broken bone cavity is apparent and not hidden from feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient be placed on their back, and a cushion be placed between their shoulder blades;  then firmly press on the muscles, and join the bones together with both hands, being meticulous in the procedure and adjusting according to every possible method and what is appropriate, until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then apply a poultice and bandage, and place a thin layer of willow or fig bark, or something similar in lightness, on top of it, and wrap this in cloth.  Then carefully bind this around the broken bone so it doesn't move; and occasionally bind it in a circular fashion around the back, and tightly bandage it in a suitable manner; and regularly check the bandage, and when it is softened, tighten it.
And if the bandage causes irritation, or swelling, or pain, or an abscess in the area, then you must loosen it and remove the poultice and rectify what has happened by a suitable treatment; and then replace the poultice, if it is appropriate for the condition; and maintain the bandage until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of broken bones.
Know that fractures of the ribs occur only in the thick parts, which are the two sides; and the extremities and front portion are not usually affected by injuries because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the feeling, through the investigation of it with words.
And the restoration should be such that the fracture is held together with words according to the best possible method, until it is properly joined, and then apply a poultice and bind the broken bone with a splint, if necessary.
And if the rib fracture is in the inner parts, then the patient experiences intense pain and stabbing, like the pain of someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and there is difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And already the ancients were ingenious in dealing with this with great skill.
And according to them, one should avoid foods that cause inflammation and gas, so that the stomach does not become bloated and distended, and the fracture is not pushed outwards.
But we should consider this, so that it is not an easy cause to generate a hot abscess, if it does not happen; and if it does happen, then it will help with that.
And some of them would say that a cupping glass should be placed on it, and then suction should be made strongly; and it seems like a reasonable approach, but nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass will draw the superfluities to the area because of the weakness.
And some of them would say: the area should be covered with wool soaked in warm oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that the dressing fits tightly when wrapped around the affected area; then the patient should be treated with a pleurisy-like diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause afflicts the patient, whom it cannot withstand, and the bone pierces the covering with a painful puncture, causing us concern about the patient, then we must make an incision over the area, expose the fractured rib, and then place an instrument on it to preserve the shape of the rib, the remembrance of which is prior, and to cut the bone with ease, and remove it.
And then, if the wound is large, we should bring the two sides of the wound together with stitches, and treat it with poultices until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and the patient should be treated with whatever is appropriate for the internal abscess.
