And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of fractures of the spine of the back and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and often in their articulation, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if you find their muscles already softened, numb, dead, and you can neither move nor stretch nor compress them, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle, they feel no pain, then know that they are not healed, and in such a state of disposition, death is imminent.
And if you move them and feel constriction with your fingers and pricks, then know that the bone's integrity is preserved and the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar things to this in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you see that the feet are also softened, as was the case with the muscles, and further, when the patient lies on their back, they experience involuntary emptying and expulsion, and sometimes they are lying on their back, attempting to urinate but cannot, then know that this is fatal, and you should not expend effort on their cure.
But if you observe none of those symptoms, the cause is milder and more easily dealt with.
And the cure for this is to apply soothing compresses of hot oil or crushed egg yolks to the inflamed vertebra.  Apply these dressings several times a day until the inflammatory abscess subsides.
And then apply a soothing and stimulating plaster, and bind it firmly.  Instruct the patient to rest and remain calm, and not to sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you observe evidence of bone fracture, or any other cause that has separated the bone from its place, then it is necessary to cut away the surrounding tissue and remove the bone.  Then carefully rejoin the two sides of the wound, with sutures if it is a large fracture.  Then treat the area with a flesh-promoting ointment until it heals.
But if the back of the bone is fractured, and the hip joint is affected, then you must insert the index finger of your left hand into the anus, and use the other hand to align the broken bone as best as possible using a second method; then apply a plaster and bandages, if needed, and bind the area firmly.
And if you find broken fragments of bone there, then carefully remove them, and treat the wound with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the [broken] hip.
The bones of the hip joint are often broken, and if they break, the fracture is usually only a separation of the extremities, they are split lengthwise and shifted out of alignment.  The patient experiences pain and throbbing at the affected area, and the leg becomes numb when fractured.
And the restoration involves you placing your hand over it [the fractured area] until it is positioned correctly, according to the correct anatomical alignment.
And if the fracture is only on one extremity, then you must align it using whatever means are available, until it is properly aligned with its natural position.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or has already shifted downward, then you must have the patient lie on their stomach until you are prepared to restore that fracture. And once you have aligned it, apply a plaster to it, then place a splint of wood or bone on top, and secure it tightly, so that you don't worry about the puncture of the fracture or the splint moving.
And align the concave area with whatever fills it properly until it meets the straight area, and tell the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
However, if a painful abscess arises, then cease treating the patient's condition and restoring it until the painful abscess is subsided, and apply what reduces it according to what is done beforehand; and then return to the treatment, and secure it properly.
And if the bones are shattered or splintered, then you should remove them.
Chapter 11.
Of restoring the fracture of the arm/forearm.
Adjutori" is what is between the elbow and the end of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, one of which is that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness according to this form:
And two ligaments are tied to its two ends, and it is then suspended from a high place.
And have the patient lie down on a bed; then gently place the patient's broken arm on the wood until its end reaches the middle point of the wood, to which the curve of the arm is attached.
And then suspend a heavy object over it, or the attendant should extend the wood downwards; and then the doctor and the patient's hands together should hold the fracture until it returns to its proper place.
The other way is for the patient to be lying on their back, and to tie the patient's arm to a support; then instruct two attendants to hold one of them on the area above the fracture with their hands, and the other to hold the lower part; and each should extend it in their respective direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then tie a bandage around the fracture and another bandage over that, and each of the two assistants extend in their respective directions.
And thus, if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, then it is necessary to place half of the bandage on the end and the other below the fracture, towards the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary to place the bandage over that spot and over the elbow.
Apply that easily and without effort, and then, when it is fastened as needed and applied with good application, tie it firmly to prevent a suppurating boil.
But if a suppurating boil occurs, then leave the bandage until the seventh day, and place on it wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil until the boil subsides, and then you tie it.
And the way to tie it is to place a plaster over the fracture, then place a wrapping of clean cloth over the plaster; then adjust the arm on this support, and place their open hand over their muscle.
And place the cloth and tie it to the support, so the arm is in the proper position, if no cause from the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And you should place on the fracture the splint that is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place the width of a finger between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tie the splints together with the binding I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your binding over the fracture site is tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the binding should be.
So, adjust the positioning of the splints and the binding as we described when you restore the limb to its proper place, then do [the following].
And if you fear a suppurative abscess, then leave the binding and splints in place for seven days, as we have stated.
And thereafter, monitor the ligament in all its parts for three days, so that no bruising or inflammation occurs.  Or, the limb should not receive food to prevent excess swelling from the binding.  Thus, correct everything according to what we have said.
However, if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, with their hands on their stomach.
And place a full, wool-stuffed cushion under the affected area.  And at all hours of night or day, be sure the shape of the broken limb is not distorted, or the ligament softened, then correct all this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we previously mentioned, so that the food is light at first until it has begun to be stronger; and then you must increase the food's quantity.
Because according to custom, the splint and leg should remain in place for forty days; and then they must be released, and a bath administered, and medicated with ointments that correct the affected area.
And if the fracture is severely inflamed, then do not release the ligament and splints for up to a month or up to two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called two "foils"; of which one is small, and it is the one that sits on the said finger, and the other is large and is the one that rests beneath the small finger, on the lower part.
And often the larger foil is broken alone, or the smaller one, and often they are broken together.
And when the small foil is broken above, then the restoration of it is easier, and its healing is more straightforward.
And when the foil is broken below, the fracture is serious, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse than that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the broken bone is the small foil above, then it is appropriate for the physician, in order to restore it, to gently apply a small extension until it is set.
But if the large foil is broken, then a more forceful extension is necessary.
And if both foils are broken together, then you must apply the pressure much more strongly.
And it is necessary that you place the hand's form to assist its restoration and extension, placed over a cushion, and let the thumb be positioned higher than all the other fingers, the said little finger be lower than the others, and the patient be lying on a smooth, flat surface.
And there should be a cushion in front of him, at his height or level, so that the patient does not experience discomfort.
And then the attendant stretches the arm of the lower part with their hand or a bandage, and another attendant stretches the upper part similarly.
And then the physician pushes the bone back in until it is reduced to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are difficulties, then you must exert yourself to restore each piece to its proper place with your skill.
And if there appears a separated fragment that pierces the skin and is not to your satisfaction in restoring it, then you should sew it up and close it according to the method previously mentioned.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already made a separate chapter on this, and therefore take care of that accordingly.
And if you encounter a suppurating abscess at the start of your restoration, then you should apply a cloth with a plaster made of roasted oil and white wax, and let it be of medium consistency and subtlety, and tightly bind the cloth over that until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a prepared plaster of powdered mortar with egg white, and then apply bandages; and let the bandage be placed over that pus-filled and sore fracture.
And know that the name of the bandages for the arm is used in the major arrangements, or whether the fracture is in one compartment or in two compartments at once.
Then apply your bandage firmly and forcefully to the fracture site, and if the bandage is placed on the parts above the fracture or on the lower parts, apply it slightly more loosely according to what was previously mentioned in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, and not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be of a medium consistency between subtle and coarse, as we have described.
And consider the limb and its ligaments for a few days. And if any cause arises for which rectification is necessary, such as swelling of the limb, then you must immerse the limb in hot water until the swelling ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the constriction is now softened and the bone is realigned and similar to the others, then rectify all of this with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess constriction, for then it is necessary to soften that slightly and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient does not experience any of the causes we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to loosen it for about twenty days or thereabouts.
And then, using your skill, suspend the patient's arm with the aid of active movement, and have them sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 or 32 days, or more quickly in 28 days, and all of this depends on the disposition of the complexions and the efficacy of the treatment.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand's wrist and fingers.
Know that the wrist of the hand and the joints of the fingers are frequently fractured, and often, the damage is due to friction rather than impact.
And when the hand suffers a fracture or injury, the patient should lie down, and the hand should be placed on a surface that is even, and then the patient's hand should be placed upon that extended hand.
Then the attendant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should hold them together until they are properly joined.
Then a plaster and bandage are needed, if there is no risk of an abscess; and a splint is placed on top, corresponding to the injured area.  You have now wrapped it in a cloth.
However, if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make a shape like a cloth sling, and instruct the patient to press that with their fractured palm; then it should be secured with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, with a cloth in which it is placed so the leather is applied to the palm; and press it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a splint on top and another splint from the part below the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the bandage, as if the hand is twisted to the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs to any of the joints of the fingers, if it is the thumb, it should be encased as needed and then be held in place by the palm.  If desired, place a small, straight splint on it to keep the fracture elevated and prevent it from moving.
However, if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or pinky finger, then it should be encased and bound with the finger next to it; or all fingers should be bound together consecutively, which is better; or place a small splint on it, just as we have described for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and after that, the disposition of a hot abscess and its opposite—with your operation—with what is necessary when any of those causes occur, according to how it has been repeated in the account.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken, and it is diagnosed by feeling according to whether it is displaced forward and most severely.
And its restoration is done by tightening a ligament over the fracture and another ligament under the fracture, and the patient is laid prone on their stomach and on their face.
And then the physician stretches all the ligament to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be close to the knee, toward the hip joint, so that the extension is sufficient; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the knee, then the ligament should be placed near the knee, and the extension should be directed towards the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both of their hands until it is restored to its natural shape and the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, lest the limb be afflicted with an abscess. But if it becomes infected with an abscess, then you leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied thus:  wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, in the lower regions, and let the ends of the splint extend from the superior part of the two sections, and then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place splints on the thigh, over the fractured area, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is stabilized.
And then apply a strong bandage around the center of the fracture area with three or four wraps; the further you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the weaker and lighter and more loose the bandage should become.
And then extend the two ends of the splint to the lower parts until they reach the ankle of the foot, and firmly hold the ends of the splints on the other side to ensure the bandage remains in its place.
And then leave the bandage on it, so that no swelling, abscess, heat, or inflammation, and similar things, befall the limb; and if any of these afflictions occur, then remove it immediately, and correct whatever has happened as soon as possible, as we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone protruding, then you must reduce that as much as possible; and if not, cover it and leave it, and treat it with what has preceded the remedy until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh, or other things that are bound to it, are splinted, as we have said in the section on the arm/leg.
However, in a restoration done in this way, the patient does not experience lameness; and if it is restored alone, or otherwise another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that that person limps, as is customary.
And know that the thigh is splinted for 40 days or a little more or a little less, and all this is according to the diversity of the constitutions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fractured kneecap often heals, but often results in a lot of friction.
However, if a fracture occurs, it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a bruise, and about that, everything is established by feeling.
And the restoration of it is done so that what is broken from its parts is joined together with these things until it is joined and connected as much as possible by matching, ease, and shaping.
And then place a plaster and put it on that round shape, if you need one; and bind it tightly with a suitable bandage.
And then consider all the methods that we have described for other fractures, just as for a boil, or anything similar that opposes an accident by what is suitable for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the restoration of a broken leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is large, and is called the thighbone; and the other is slender, and is called the shinbone.
And the treatment for any of the types of fractures that affect the two bones of the arm is the same as the treatment for a broken arm, and the operation for it is also the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is displaced in all directions.
And if the smaller bone is broken, the leg is displaced forward.
And if the larger bone is broken, you find it beneath, and it is among those things not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, reduction, and binding of the splints accordingly.
However, if the fracture of the leg is extensive with many fragments, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And if there is some need for an operation on the arm in conjunction with the leg, and when you have aligned the splints and completed all your work, then you will take pieces of pine wood, placed beneath the things placed on cushions, and these are made similarly from palm branches; choose those with a small thickness and avoid the large ones.  Their length should be according to the length of the leg, from the top down to the lowest part.
And then wrap each splint in two layers of cloth, according to its length, placing one layer along one side of the leg and the other on the other side, extending from the top of the leg down to the lowest part of the foot.
And then bind the two splints at three places: the two ends and the middle.  With this binding, the leg is prevented from bending to either side, and it is held in a good position.
And now a brace of wood, the length of the leg, has been placed on it so that the leg is kept from movement.
And it is especially necessary that you do this in cases of fractures, and when this occurs properly.
And then examine the leg daily and study it thoroughly, watching out for abscesses, inflammations, or other similar problems.  And if any such condition arises, then apply what is necessary until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the heel bone and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the heel doesn't happen often; but fractures of the foot bones do happen sometimes; and fractures of the foot toes happen rarely, and what occurs to them is mainly just friction in their positions.
And if the foot bones are fractured and sometimes those bones are raised above themselves, then the patient should place their foot on the ground, prepared as if walking.
And then place one foot on what is raised of those bones, and then press, and squeeze those until they return to their places.
And then apply a plaster and bandage over it, and place a small board with two flat ends under the sole of the foot.
And then secure that with a downward pressure on the foot, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things you need.
And after three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones squeezed together, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation on these is as you know it.
But if any of the fingers are broken, then restore them, and put them together as I and I have recounted to you in the restoration of fingers of the hand. And then place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and a little below it; and then, below the foot, this board that I have described to you, and secure it with downward pressure.
But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you place a splint on each finger, of its appropriate size, wrapped in linen cloth, and secure the board to the lower part of the foot, and place one end of the board over the stomach of the foot, so that it is held with good retention.
And it is fitting for you not to forget that you should consider all the things that I have told you about other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and be prepared for any accident with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thigh bone, and the pelvic bone, and the penis of the man.
When the thigh bone of a woman is broken, then make a splint from a polished board, and then gently incline it towards the back, and keep it behind.
And then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be placed on the thigh as a bandage; then the woman should be held, and raise her back a little at a time, because all this is done until the front of the thigh is in the expected position and the broken bone returns to its place.
And then place pillows on her back, and when she wants to urinate, she will remove the wool easily and then urinate, and you reposition it according to the method with which you positioned it at the beginning, then return it to its original position on the pillow.
And do this for seven days or so, for then it will be restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and place a reed cannula over the wound; and insert the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the cannula with force until the bladder is inflated within the thigh, for the fracture will return to its place.
And then it should be filled with wool and remain for several days, as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is fractured, then administer in its restoration and reduction what we have recounted for a fractured hip bone.
And what is correct is not hidden in these recent and easily-healed fractures, especially for the one who has some study, use, and understanding of this my book's words; for that fracture signifies to you, in the greatest ways, the correct path to restoration and the tightening of it, therefore understand well.
But in the case of a fractured penis, take a bandage of cloth, and place it around the penis; then apply a wrap of cloth, and let it remain wrapped for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when there is a plague.
It is convenient for one who has a fractured bone with a plague, and especially if the bone is large, such as the bone of the thigh or the humerus, or similar to those, that you apply cupping and phlebotomy as soon as the conditions for phlebotomy are suitable or as we have said above.
And if the plague is flowing blood, then you must apply cupping to retain it, using crushed glass powder over it, if no other cause is present.
And then begin restoring the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this, unless an abscess forms.
However, if a bad abscess forms, then you should leave the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not attempt it on the third or fourth days, for you will cause bad accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the exposed skin, then you must carefully reduce it and hold it in place with your hands with ease and minimal stretching.
However, if stretching is not appropriate and aligning it with your hands is not possible, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument.  This instrument is made of iron, its length is seven or eight inches, and its width is according to the size of the wound.  Therefore, it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these, according to the amount needed for the treatment in all types of fractures.
And it should be round, and have little thickness so that it does not buckle under the pressure during the operation.
And it should have a sharp end with a beveled edge on its ends; and the top of it should be thicker, tapering towards the middle sections.
And this is its form:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary for the pointed end to be placed raised above the protruding end of the bone.
And you push it in one go until the bone returns, and it is aligned with some equation.
And therefore, strive to align the fractured ends together.  If the broken end is small and doesn't readily accept the instrument, then it is necessary to cut the end of the bone until the instrument can grip it.
But if you cannot fully reduce the bone with what we have described, then cut it with whatever surgical instruments are appropriate or saw it with a saw in any way possible.
And then remove any remaining rough edges and small fragments from the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and immediately after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position; and therefore, do what you deem best to greatly help the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and if it is summer, do not apply a plaster or anything containing oil to the wound, so that you do not cause festering and decay.
And afterward, administer remedies at the time of completing the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered, then cut the wound with scissors and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to compress the wound with the fractured bone, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and it has caused death or a corroding illness or a severe sickness, i.e., gangrene, upon their patients. And if your [treatment] is gentle, soft, different from the treatment of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear the harmful accidents we have mentioned, and you find pain in the area, then it is not appropriate to place remedies there, but make a dressing of hard cloths instead of remedies and bind it tightly.
And when, after about a day or two, pus has begun to form in the wound, then remove the cloth you had placed on it with wine, and then administer poultices, ointments with which, according to our custom, we medicate wounds, such as tetrafarmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage and examine the wound each morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed, and the limb must be prepared in such a way that the pus can drain from it to the lower parts easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed nor is the pus draining, then know that there are complications, and you should examine the wound with a probe. And of those complications, those that are separable, remove and extract them; those that are not separable and cause pain to the limb, force you to incise them as far as possible.
And if pus or a discharge, that is, an inflammation, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, occurs on the wound, then you should apply whatever is appropriate for its treatment, as previously described in its chapter.
And concerning this, where my advice and your skill come in: when a large bone is broken, you should treat the limb in a similar manner to a broken thighbone, or other injuries of the large bones, and avoid attempting to pull it (the bone) out, or to remove it, because often death results from this; leave it to heal, because it will often fall into place by itself within 20 to 30 days; and then treat the wound if you see fit, or if it doesn't heal.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of inflammation that occurs in the area of a fracture.
Often this inflammation occurs in the area of healing from a fracture, especially if it's close to the joints, and it disfigures the limb's shape; and it greatly hinders the limb's natural function.
Then consider if the inflammation is recent and apply medicines that have astringent properties, such as aloes, linseed, myrrh, cerat, and similar substances; you may take some or all of these, and mix them with astringent wine, or egg white, or vinegar, and apply this mixture to the inflammation on a bandage, firmly binding the bandage over it.
And leave it bound tightly for several days; then loosen it and apply another, until the inflammation is gone; or apply a thin lead plate firmly over it, for lead has the property of resolving any swelling in the limbs.
However, if the inflammation has become hard and tight and it is necessary to remove it forcefully, then cut it along its most prominent part, and remove the excess and prominent tissue, or cut it with some razors until it is gone; and treat the area until it heals.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture, when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural state.
When a broken bone is restored, and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, this is due to various causes: one is an excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not as it should be; or due to excessive tightness of the ligaments, hindering the flow of nutrients to the limb; or due to excessive application of remedies; or due to improper movement; or due to a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to feed the patient and make their body soft until the blood is replenished; and administer baths; and introduce cheerfulness and joy upon them.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water compresses, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and prevent the operation from being properly performed.
When an accident causes a limb that has already been set to be deformed, with a crooked or protruding broken bone and a callus, and this has affected the limb's shape, but nonetheless, the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not believe someone who claims that the limb is broken again.
But many foolish doctors and bone-setters have done this in our land; and this practice is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is deadly.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which soothing herbs have been steeped, such as marshmallow leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and a softening poultice should be applied, such as a well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of marshmallow root should be made and mixed with hen's egg yolk or with beeswax oil, and applied as a poultice; or fat figs should be crushed with pigeon dung, and similar medicinal remedies that are known as healing salves.
And sometimes it is dissolved by diligent gentle friction with moist hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in every part at all times.
But if the twisting has progressed and become hard and stony, and if it requires surgical treatment with iron, then the inflammation on top must be stopped, and the continuity of the bone must be broken.  The excess callus of the bone itself must be cut away with fine scalpels, and care and attention must be given to the process.
Chapter 23.
Concerning dislocations.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place, preventing movement, distorting the shape of the limb, and causing the patient significant pain and injury.
Therefore, when an accident causes a dislocation, it is crucial to attempt reduction immediately, without delay, because if it is delayed, the area becomes inflamed and hardened, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And therefore, the limb should not be manipulated or stretched during the period of inflammation, because it often causes spasms and prolonged pain.
But when this occurs, you should apply a poultice to the inflamed area of the patient, and allow it to remain until the inflammation subsides; then the limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and the dislocation should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated in this way, so that the dislocated part returns to its proper place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocations, following the precedent set for fractures; from the most superior part of the body down to the most inferior.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocation of the lower jaw.
So, the two jaws are dislocated if not quickly; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their places and slightly softened; or they are completely and entirely dislocated, until they are softened near the joint, so much that the patient is drooling and cannot hold the jaw together or close his mouth, and his tongue is swollen (or twisted, entangled).
However, when the dislocation is slight, it often returns to its place on its own with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and severe, then gentle reduction must be administered promptly, and no delay is permissible.
And the attendant must hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the fingertip of one hand into the area of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hand should be outside, helping to stabilize the jaw; and he should instruct the patient to gently close and separate their jaws; and the physician then manipulates the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its proper place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer a vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easier, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And therefore, when they have returned to their place and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over those powders bandages with a plaster, made of wax and rubbed oil; and then bind it gently with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to the right or left; and do not force them to chew anything; until the pain subsides and the jaws are healed, then let them eat as they please.
And this should be administered with care, and do not put pressure on opening the mouth for eating, drinking, and breathing until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then often fevers and persistent sweating occur, and the patient's belly is strongly affected, and vomiting of pure bile is frequent.
And when you see this, then know that it is dangerous; and often the one afflicted by this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated inwards from its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is clear from the feeling/examination.
And the restoration involves the patient lying on their back and extending their arms. And then firmly press the area with your palm, for it will return to place.
And then apply a poultice and powders, and compress it.
However, the extremity that connects to the elbow and continues from that point is not dislocated in any given day; therefore, it must be reduced and bound as we have said, and as is appropriate for your procedure.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and a bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to quietness and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated on the part toward the top, and on the part below.
And the second is that it is dislocated on the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated behind by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and comes out on the lower parts towards the top, and mostly in those whose flesh is scant, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is much flesh, it is pushed out with difficulty.
And frequently, some men are struck or injured, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and is swollen because it has already been dislocated.
Then you must examine it until you are absolutely certain about it, and then proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts toward the shoulder; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, for you find a manifest difference between them; and you find a concavity at the head of the muscle and below the shoulder, near the touch of the head of the muscle, just like an egg, and the patient cannot bring their hand to their ear, nor move it in any way.
If it is dislocated towards the foot or above, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a gentle reduction of this dislocation is done when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand towards the upper parts; then place your hands below the patient's shoulder, and raise the joint forcefully back into its place, and the minister raises the patient's hand, and extends it upward; and then places it back down, so that it returns easily.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a considerable time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient must be bathed in warm water and an application should be administered that softens and soothes, such as the juice of marshmallow root, fennel, and melilot in water, and it should be applied.
And then the patient should lie on their back, and a wool pad be placed under their armpit that is neither too loose nor too tight, then the physician places their heel on the pad, and lifts the head of the muscle with strength, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not pulled down towards the lower parts, and another attendant holds the patient's head so that it is not pulled down, and then the joint soon returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the opposite side, and he should place his arm under the patient's armpit and lift the patient's arm upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another attendant pulls the patient's hand downwards from the area below their abdomen. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary to have something else suspended with him, to weigh him down, so that the dislocation returns quickly.
And it is easily reduced in another manner: a long pole is placed in the ground, the top of which is round, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin.  Then, the patient is placed beneath the pole, thick cloths are placed over the top of the pole.  The patient stands facing the length of the pole, and then the patient's arm is stretched downwards to the other side.  The patient's body is stretched in the opposite direction with strength, and the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult by all the methods we have described, then use this treatment: take a pole whose length is about two arms' length and width is about four fingers, and its thickness should be about the width of the armpit, shaped like this:
And then, thick cloths should be tied around the round top of the pole, so that the pole does not injure the patient.
And then, it is placed under the patient's armpit, and the arm or hand is stretched along the pole towards the lower part, and the pole is tied to the support, arm, and hand.
Then, the arm is placed on a step or rung of a ladder according to the width, and the hand is stretched downwards, and the other side of the body is suspended on the other side, for the joint will quickly return to place.
And when the reduction is completed by any method possible, you should place a thick wool pad under the armpit; then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder, ashes, and egg white to the entire muscle, wrapping it around; then bind the armpit with a suitable bandage; wrap the bandage over the plaster, and tie the patient's hand to the neck.  Leave it and do not move the hand for seven days.
And the patient's food should be kept light until the limb is strengthened, for this will be more helpful for its recovery.
Then, the diet is gradually increased to more than seven or five, and movement is encouraged; and if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated repeatedly due to moisture affecting it or another cause, then a cautery with three punctures should be administered, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage is removed and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and bandage have been reapplied several times, and it is still not firm and fixed and the patient cannot raise that arm to the parts above, then understand that the nerves, which are at the end of the muscle, are already cut or extended or softened, and know that the joint will never return to its proper place.
For dislocations of the foot and the breast or back parts, reduction is achieved through pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns to its place.
And another bandage should be applied to that area, and treat it until it is healed.
And if, after the healing, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original natural state.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of elbow dislocations.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and especially in the front part and the back part.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, for it is evident from the observation or touch, whichever way it is dislocated.  And when you adjust the dislocated elbow with your hands, this is clearly demonstrated to you, because the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes a suppurating boil, for if it becomes a suppurating boil, reduction is difficult, and the limb is not completely healed; and especially when it is in the back parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, with greater pain, and often leads to death.
And the restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's arm be extended with two hands, and the arm be stretched out. The physician's hands should be on the elbow, below it, and the physician pushes the joint with the tips of their fingers together, or with the heel of their palm, until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is returned by bending the arm with the hand, until it strikes the elbow with the heel of the palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong, forceful extension.
And it is that two assistants extend the arm, and hold that extension, so that it is not pulled back from the extension.
And then the arm should be turned in every direction, since it is wrapped with a folded cloth/bandage long over the hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that these things help lubricate the joint easily; then push the joint with forceful pressure until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary to place a compress on that area in which there is astringency and binding with egg white, and it should be bandaged tightly, and the arm should be suspended from the neck and left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and therefore, if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen the bandage from it and leave it.
And if you see the joint is not well tightened, then you repeat the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then loosen it.
But if it happens that there is a severe injury near the reduction of the joint, and its condition is in motion, then administer moistening in a bath and gentle friction and compression, until it is relieved; or place a soft, oily substance over the joint; and then bandage it, and leave it on the limb day and night. And then remove that, and put that limb in a bath; and when it sweats, then you rub the joint with moderate friction, and then repeat the application of the oily substance, once, and again, and a third time. with intervals of bathing, until it is clean.
And if you wish, place a warm, damp cow's hide over the wound and press firmly upon it; and do this several times, for it is moist and helps to restore it to its proper position.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated shoulder, that is, the joint between the arm and the hand.
A dislocated shoulder is sometimes dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, and the opposite is true for other joints. However, speed in reducing it at the time of dislocation is necessary, before the area becomes inflamed or a painful abscess forms there.
And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the injured person's arm on a table, and the attendant extends the arm; the physician then places his hand, or palm, over the protruding joint and pushes until it returns to its place.
However, it is important to consider if the palm is already turned towards the palm of the hand, then the patient places the back of their hand on a table to facilitate the extension and reduction.  And if the dislocation is on the back of the hand, the hand of the patient should be placed palm-down on the table, so the physician's hand can be placed on the projecting joint.
And therefore, if it returns quickly, it is good.  But if not, then hold it with a bandage, preventing inflammation, and leave it; and do not repeat this, for if you do, it will not endure or be able to improve in the reduction since days have passed.
However, if the joint remains crooked, and the patient doesn't feel any cause, but the hand is weak and cannot grip anything, then know that the nerves are severed or damaged.  In this case, there is no cure, but it should be cauterized near the injury, and a strong remedy should be applied.
Therefore, once the injury is healed, apply a bandage as previously described, and let it remain bound for five days; then, loosen it and let it heal, and exercise the hand.
However, if the movement is impaired and some weight is added, then you should alleviate it with hot water and some friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various directions.
And when any of those fingers are dislocated toward the palm side or the back of the hand, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation away with your fingers until it returns to its place.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
And then suspend it and extend it until it is properly fitted into its place.
And when it is night, bind it according to that description, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is straightened.
And do likewise if it is dislocated toward the back of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocations of the vertebrae of the back.
When an accident causes a complete dislocation of a vertebra in the back or neck, and the displaced vertebra remains out of place, then in that case there is no cure, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's bowels are expelled involuntarily, and they cannot retain them; and often some of their limbs or both legs or both arms, or one of them, become paralyzed; or a vertebra is displaced, for often it is displaced, and often the removal of those (vertebrae) is done in four parts.
Therefore, if it has already been moved to the rear parts, it is called a dislocation.
However, the treatment for it is that you then consider if the dislocation occurred due to youthfulness, for then in that case there is no cure nor complete healing.
But in those cases resulting from accident or impact or similar causes, the Ancients described various methods of treatment in lengthy accounts, from which the most beneficial ones can be learned; but I have already shortened that, avoiding the excessive length, for the sake of clarity in my presentation.  I have composed this using various instruments of which I will demonstrate and explain them.
And only those dislocations occurring on the back, specifically as I'm recommending, can be cured.
And the patient should be laid out on their face, close to a wall, and underneath them there should be a soft instrument to prevent the foot from being damaged.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the ground at the edge of the wall near the head, and another pole, at the opposite end of the wall, supporting the feet.
And one attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another attendant should hold the other pole in a similar way.
And then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and, beneath it, tightly tie a strong ligament; and extend the end of the ligament to the pole positioned by the head, and tie it there; and then tightly bind another ligament over their hips, and down to their legs; and finally adjust all the ligaments and tie them to the other pole, which is positioned by the feet.
And then each attendant should extend the ligament to the pole's position, and the two poles and the ligaments should not be moved from their places, but nonetheless, should not be fixed quite so tightly as we have mentioned.
And then the physician places their palms on the spine with force until it returns to its proper position, or places a table there, and then makes the patient adjust their feet on the table until they are in the correct position.
But if it does not return in this manner, then take a board whose width or length is approximately three arms' lengths, and carve a space in the wall near the patient where the end of the board can fit in. And then place the middle of the board on the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its proper place.
And if you wish, use a turning device held in your hand;  place two poles, each one arm's length long, in the ground behind the patient's head, and similarly, place two more poles behind the patient's feet.  The distance between each pole should be a palm's width.  And each pole should have a hole in it through which the turning device can run. Make sure each pole is firmly anchored in the ground so that it doesn't move.
And place a round rod, on which the turning device is located, and turn it within the two holes of the two poles.
And at the end of that (rod) is a hole where the (rod) fits, its length being one palm's width, and this is the same for the other two poles.
And now tighten the bandages you have placed around the patient's feet on the part of the device near their head, and tighten the bandages around their thighs on the part of the device closer to their feet.
And near each turning device, let a helper turn the device with their hand, while the physician aligns the vertebra as we have described.
And after the vertebra has been returned to its proper position, and the area is aligned, you should apply a desiccated plaster with egg white, then bandage it.  Then place a thin wooden board, whose width is about three fingers, and whose length covers the affected area of the spondylosis, over the plaster and onto the spondylosis area.
And then bind it with the bandage we've described, and let the patient consume light nourishment until healed.
And if any lingering problem remains in the area after healing, then medicines that soften and cleanse through prolonged use of the table-method, as we have described, must be administered; and a lead sheet is applied to the area.
And sometimes a problem occurs in the lower vertebrae of the spine, caused by that dislocation; and that is the bone which is threatening to protrude, so that no presumption should be made with this medicine, for it will likely cause death.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle are only susceptible to dislocation, and do not suffer the same conditions as other joints with minor displacement and cavity issues.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, or outwards, or forwards, or backwards.
And it is more often dislocated inwards, and less so forwards or backwards.
A sign of dislocation inwards is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected leg, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the knee, and the area where the bending should be is inflamed with a hot abscess, because the head of the thigh bone has lodged there.
The sign of dislocation outwards is that the symptoms are the opposite of those in inward dislocation.
The sign of a forward dislocation is that the leg extends fully, but cannot be bent without pain in the knee, and if forced forward, it cannot go further; and their urine is retained and their groin is abscessed, and they walk on their heels.
The sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can it be bent forward as the groin bends, and the leg is shorter than the other, and the groin is swollen, and the head of the thigh bone is in that area.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and chronic, already affected by the passage of time, you proceed with its reduction and it remains in its position, then that treatment is not complete; and therefore, no presumption of cure should be made in that case.
But in the case of a recent dislocation, one of the four types, you must try to reposition the joint, extending it both inward and outward, and moving it to the right and left, as strong, light pressure returns it and requires no other treatment.
However, if it does not return in this way, then you must prepare a strong assistant and extend the leg from the lowest part, either by hand or with a bandage tied above the knee; and another assistant should extend the hand of the patient so that they place their hands under the patient's armpits; and then tighten a strong bandage on the thigh root, and have a third assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And its extension should be from the inside of the knee to the groin area, or from the back to the buttock area.
And the extension of all of these should be done at once until the patient is lifted from the ground and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is a common procedure for all four [types of dislocation].
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type [of dislocation] to proceed with the specific treatment.
In the proper reduction of a dislocation, when it is dislocated inward, the patient should be placed on their healthy side.  Then, apply bandages to the root of the thigh in the area between the top of the thigh and the groin; and then extend the bandage upward from the groin area to the highest part of the groin.
And then another strong attendant should take the patient's arms, and firmly grasp the thick part of the affected thigh, and extend it outward with forceful extension, for this will return it to its place.
And this type is easier to treat, with which this limb is reduced than the other types.
But if you are unsuccessful and do not obey the method of treatment, in this type of case, it is necessary to bind the patient's two feet together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each be about four fingers, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by two fingers.
And then the patient should be suspended above the head of the bed, and remain there; and their height from the ground should be about two arms' lengths.
And then order a strong young man to grasp the head of the thigh, and let another young man suspend the patient, and the other young man, grasping the thigh with strength, should push, so that the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when there is a dislocation to the outside, the patient should lie on a stool, as we have described when the knee has been bent; and the bandage should be applied firmly to the injured leg and to the patient's foot.
And then two beds should be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at their head; and then beds fitted to the middle of the stool should be placed, covered with damp cloths, so that the patient is not harmed by the bed being between their thighs, so that the extension to the lower parts is not pulled.
And then each attendant should extend their part, and the physician, with their hand, will guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then the leg should be slightly extended, and it is according to this same method on a stool; and the physician places the palm of their right hand on the painful part of the limb, and then holds it with the other hand, and with that holding, extends that part of the leg downwards towards the front.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended downwards, but elevated above the ground; instead, they must be placed on a hard surface, just as is required for the lifting if their hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about their positioning on two stools; and it is on their face, and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, not much.
And pressure must be applied with a bandage to the area where the joint is located.
Therefore, when the reduction is complete, as it should be—and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see them equal and the patient tightens their leg and expands it without excuse, then you know that the limb is returned as required—and then you bring the two thighs together and apply a poultice and bind them tightly so that the hip is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then release the bandage and the poultice, and compare one leg with the other.
Now, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and tighten it according to your initial tightening, and leave it for three days.
And then release that, and have him/her walk on each leg for a few days, until he/she is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Of the cure of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outward, inward, and downward (or behind), but not forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that, when you tell the patient to apply their leg with the thigh, then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you should have the patient lie down straight, so that they extend their leg, if they can; place your arm on their side, and place their foot between your feet; then apply your palms to their knee, and grip them firmly with your fingers on the knee, and another assistant extends their foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return (reduction) is that their leg adheres to the thigh with a light, effortless touch.
And then bandage it and attach the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult, then administer strong traction with the ligaments, about which the narration of the hip treatment has previously discussed.
Chapter 33.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
The ankle is sometimes displaced by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated according to the extent, and its dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle often swells on the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for a small displacement is gentle repositioning; the ankle should be gently extended by hand and held until it returns to its place.
However, when it is dislocated to a significant degree, you must have the patient lie down straight, and have a strong and capable attendant hold the leg behind the heel in the middle of its displaced position.
Then, with your right hand, grasp the foot at the highest point, and with your left hand, grasp the lower part of the foot at the location of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand, twice; then extend it with your left hand, and push the foot of the leg with your right hand towards the thigh effortlessly.
Do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the foot of the leg a third time, and you will be at the ankle.
Therefore, if it returns in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot is well-aligned, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation, for that is the return.
But if you are unable to reduce it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a tightly-tied cloth firmly on the ground, so it is positioned between their thighs, and you have already covered them with a cloth so that the patient is not injured.
And then the attendant holds their thighs, and another attendant extends the leg with their hands or with a bandage that is tied around the top of the leg; and then each attendant extends it against the extension of their counterpart, and the cloth is stretched between the patient's thighs, holding it there, so that the patient's body is not pulled down towards the parts below their extension.
And then the physician aligns the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh below, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so, when the dislocation returns and you are satisfied with the reduction, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with bandages.
And you must be careful of the nerves that are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and injures it severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage has loosened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from movement for 40 days. But if he is forced to move before that time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be dislodged and ruined, and thus not receive a cure.
And if an abscess occurs, then you should apply to it what we have previously described in our narrative, as a cure and embrocation, until it is resolved.
Chapter 34.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
The dislocated one should be straightened with little effort, without struggle; and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, then you should have the patient place their foot on a flat surface, like the ground or a table, and be positioned as if they were walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on the affected area of the joints, and then press down on it with your foot, firmly, until it returns to its place, and you see that it is now straightened and no longer protruding.
Then place a board under the sole of their foot, covering the entire foot, with two supports, and firmly bandage it properly for three days; and then loosen it and have them refrain from walking for many days, until it is firmly set and the return to normal function is ensured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocations caused by blows, or fractures, or both.
When any of those causes occur, and you exert yourself in the treatment and restoration of the patient, often death results.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such a patient, but only the experienced and skilled physician, compassionate and patient, cautious, neither hasty nor presumptuous; and who should initially use only soothing remedies to reduce inflammation, and leave the patient to the judgment of God,  except for those in whom there is hope of survival, with alleviation of the illness, and a possibility of recovery. Then, strive immediately to reduce the injury at the beginning of the problem, before inflammation sets in.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that reduces inflammation and treats the injury with appropriate drying ointments.
And if a dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you should treat their separation, and do for it what we have said for simple injuries in the previous sections of this book.
And, according to your ability, avoid the pitfalls of fear in the paths of knowledge, as my teaching has instructed you; for this is more beneficial to you from your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more health for your true self in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, for which may God be praised. Amen.
